*dhammadīpā dhammasaraṇā anaññasaraṇā.*”

“Therefore, Ānanda, be islands unto yourselves, be your own refuge,
having no other;

let the Dhamma be your island, let the Dhamma be your refuge, having no
other refuge.”

> *- Mahāparinibbāna Sutta (DN16; The Great Passing)*

**Forward**

**By Venerable Bambalapitiye Gnanaloka Thera**

It came to mind—suddenly I see this book and I feel the things people
are going through. For me, it is very much about reality, about real
life and what is happening to them. I hope this will play a part in
removing their suffering. Otherwise, no point, you know?

Bhante's gone. I will be gone. All things pass away. Meanwhile, these
teachings should help suffering go away.

My hope is that they inspire people to read the *Suttas*. Or protect the
*Suttas*, maybe some good monastics, or some lay people become
monastics. As lay people they will have more good supporting karma
(accumulated merit). Then they will put effort and more people will find
the right way. This whole thing will help, otherwise what’s the point?

That's why Buddha became Buddha. It should help their liberation, and
others' liberation too.

Before going to the higher Himalayas and the deep jungles of Sri Lanka,
I'd entrust these notes to an immediate attendant—to hand over to a
specific devotee in case I didn't return. They moved through different
hands during this time. Finally, when I wanted to get them back to give
to you all—they were missing. My heart sank. I felt some attachment,
some concern. I felt sorry that people wouldn't get this opportunity,
these ideas. Finally, we found them—inside another cover.

Ultimately, I was waiting for a time when I could give the proper
respect to Bhante. The book is one. The other is the running of the
monastery, meditation retreats, ordinations and the wider spreading of
the Dhamma.

This (book project) gives people a chance to follow the teachings and
make them available to others. If it doesn't happen now, the opportunity
is lost.

In some sense, I can see it has already happened—all of those involved
in these projects. In that sense, I'm really free. Free in the sense
that a weight has been lifted because there’s nothing I can regret.

How do I know? It’s that I don’t get the slightest thought about the
past in regards to my duty.

Venerable Ñāṇavimala contributed so much to my spiritual path. Through
him I received the living Dhamma—the noble vision that I could not have
found on my own. For others who did not have that opportunity, this work
will be a service for the future.

When I hold this (notes and reflections presented in this book), I feel
the spirit of Venerable Ñāṇavimala.

I hope all of this helps people strive and be freed from suffering.

It's a short, fleeting moment. These things will also pass away.

*Bhikkhu Bambalapitiye Gnanaloka*

*\[Editor’s Note: This foreword was adapted from Venerable Bambalapitiye
Gnanaloka Thera’s spoken reflections and was refined with his direct
guidance for clarity and flow. The meaning and tone remain entirely
his.\]*

**Introduction**

“To tell you honestly, some interesting things happened to me” Venerable
Gnanaloka said, looking up from the pages. “I was getting very strong
emotions on occasions, you know. I was seeing... like... seeing myself
on very key occasions, where I am... meeting Bhante.”

He paused, searching for words.

“Which I don't recall like that.”

For over two decades, Venerable Gnanaloka has practiced not holding onto
memories. His teacher, Venerable Ñāṇavimala—whom he addressed as
"Venerable Sir," later "Bhante"”—trained him in the art of living in the
present. Memories, left unattended, don't arise—even memories of his own
life.

But when he read the third draft of the Reflections in this book—stories
of his teacher woven from multiple sources: his own notes from 25 years
earlier, existing literature, his public talks, and our conversations
with him—something broke through. Not just images, but *presence*. “Very
key occasions” returning with such clarity that he was seeing them
again.

We asked him what he meant. What was it about these stories?

“I don't recall him in a sentimental way,” he said carefully. “But if I
put a focus on that…” The precise word remained unuttered, but the
implication was clear. "It touches a very core part of my heart."

It was in that moment we understood: This book needed to exist.

For seven years, a young man named Shanaka kept notebooks filled with
his teacher's words. He began in 1994, during his first solo retreat,
and continued until 2001, even after his own ordination as Venerable
Bambalapitiye Gnanaloka (Ñāṇāloka) Thero.

The notes trace a complete training: how to live ethically in daily
life, face desire and attachment honestly, cultivate qualities like
compassion and equanimity, and develop the understanding needed for
liberation. From basic practice to advanced insight, they preserve a
rare, complete transmission of the Buddha's path.

The teacher was Venerable Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera. The student, Shanaka,
had seen him before—but it was a chance encounter on a roadside in 1989
that changed everything.

Venerable Ñāṇavimala had come a long way to be standing on that
roadside. Born Friedrich Möller in Germany in 1911, his path to Sri
Lanka had been unlikely—through war, imprisonment, and discovering a
Buddhist text in an internment camp. After ordaining in 1955, he spent a
decade at Polgasduwa Island Hermitage— a tiny sanctuary nestled within a
lagoon, lush with tropical trees and swept by the cooling breeze —before
becoming a *cārikā* bhikkhu, walking barefoot across Sri Lanka for
twenty-five years, traversing the island three times over. He carried
only what the Vinaya permitted: his alms bowl, three robes, and the few
basic allowables needed for survival—including a piece of soap wrapped
in jackfruit leaves. It was said that there was no village in Sri Lanka
his feet hadn't touched.

He was practicing a rare echo of the Buddha's original instruction—a
tradition nearly lost to time. "Wander forth for the welfare of the
multitude," the Buddha had told his first awakened disciples. This
became known as *cārikā*—the practice of wandering on foot without
settling, forming no attachments to place or possession. He taught that
the wise cling to nothing, like a droplet on a lotus leaf—touching but
never sticking. The monk moves through the world the same way—being in
the world while clinging to nothing.

The sight of a monk like this—his presence, his simplicity—provided a
blessing to all who encountered him.

Eventually, the body that had sustained this journey grew frail, and the
walking reached its natural conclusion in 1991. He told the young
disciple that the *cārikā* had been his service to others, performed as
far as his body allowed.

Those who encountered him often found their lives altered. Venerable
Katukurunde Ñāṇananda, whose influential writings on early Buddhism
would be studied worldwide, described him as the “Mahākassapa of this
age”—the Buddha's chief disciple, an exemplary model of the ascetic
practice—telling Bhikkhu Bodhi: "If you want to get a sermon from
Mahākassapa, go see Venerable Ñāṇavimala." Bhikkhu Bodhi, translator of
major Buddhist texts, would note that “he would adjust his Dhamma talk
to meet the other person in precisely the way that best fit the other
person’s needs”, crediting him for shaping his early understanding on
meditation. He also noted that while Ven. Ñāṇavimala exemplified the
ascetic ideal, he cautioned it was not the only model the Buddha
praised.

The late Ven. Ñāṇadīpa, revered for his asceticism, recalled that while
their approaches differed, he was still moved: “Years after he has gone,
I can at times feel greatly inspired by thinking of him.” He noted the
talks he heard from Ven. Ñāṇavimala typically emphasized “the
preliminary parts of the practice.”

Few received the depth of teaching that Ajahn Brahmavaṁso experienced.
Now one of the most recognized Buddhist teachers in the West, he
describes that rare full discourse as “the best Dhamma talk that I have
ever had the good karma (accumulated merit) to listen to!”, recalling
how Ven. Ñāṇavimala “wove quotes from the *Suttas* together with
explanations from his own experience into such symphony of Dhamma.”

Venerable K. Pemasiri, a respected meditation teacher in Sri Lanka,
observed: “There was nothing put on. There was no conceit. One could
honestly say that this person was definitely on the way to Nibbāna (full
enlightenment). There was clearly no doubt about it!”

Despite his influence on a generation of Buddhist teachers, Ven.
Ñāṇavimala himself remained almost invisible. He gave no public
discourses, wrote no books, built no institutions. When he passed in
2005, most of the Buddhist world—even in Sri Lanka, where he'd walked
for decades—barely knew his name.

None of that mattered to him anyway.

Ven. Gnanaloka describes him as embodying a rare combination: the deep
*Sutta* understanding of a Pāli scholar united with the relentless
striving of a hardened forest ascetic. His theoretical foundation came
from studying under Venerable Ñāṇatiloka and Venerable Ñāṇamoli, two of
the first Western monks to master Pāli and translate key Buddhist texts.
This lineage of going directly to the *Suttas* as primary authority
shaped everything about how he taught.

Though fluent in Pāli, the language of Buddhist scriptures, his
knowledge of the local language, Sinhala, remained deliberately
limited—perhaps to stay anchored in the teachings rather than
conversation—and if spoken it was with a heavy German accent. This was
so much so that even local people occasionally couldn't understand him.
Yet once, addressing visiting monks from Wathuravila, he spoke with
unexpected clarity: *"Lankawe Sinhala handuruwata saddawa thiyanawa,
habai, shakthiya madiy"*—Sri Lankan monks have faith, but not enough
energy.

Ven. Gnanaloka recalls asking him how he accessed words he had never
heard him speak, he explained: mindfulness opens access to knowledge
stored across lifetimes.

His simple utterance to those monks was a call to match faith with
effort.

Visitors would watch him meticulously sweeping around his *kuṭi*. His
door was locked most of the time. Most dared not approach him.

Yet those who did approach—arriving with frantic eagerness, carrying
offerings—experienced something unexpected.

In his presence, something would shift. Ven. Gnanaloka describes them as
leaving in a bewildered calm.

It wasn't just what Ven. Ñāṇavimala said. It was that he was completely
present. Each small action—receiving an item, pouring water, adjusting
his robe—done with total attention.

Somehow, that presence became contagious.

Yet it also demanded something. There was an intensity to his focus—a
sharpness that could unsettle those who weren't grounded. Being near him
required maintaining one's own clarity, or the encounter could prove
difficult to bear.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala's final recorded teachings were captured in 2001. The
world has transformed since then—smartphones, social media, infinite
distraction at our fingertips. One might think his teachings about
embracing discomfort, seeking simplicity, doing one thing at a time with
full attention would feel impossibly distant now.

But perhaps that's exactly why they matter more than ever.

Ven. Gnanaloka remembers overhearing when fellow monks would
enthusiastically speak about the new technology of internet, Venerable
Ñāṇavimala would reply simply: "This is also in the sensual world."

In 1996, Shanaka had recorded something in his notebooks—a warning that
reads differently now than it did then:

*"It is sad to say that the essence of the practice is almost lost
everywhere. People are getting more used to living a comfortable life."*

1996\. Before Google. Before Facebook. Before anyone carried the
internet in their pocket. He was already seeing what the world was
drowning in.

Today, Americans check their phones 144 times a day—once every eight
minutes. Modern society has more comforts, more distractions, more ways
to avoid boredom than ever before. A study conducted in France revealed
that adults who use social media heavily have 3 times the risk of
depression and over 3 times the risk of anxiety compared to light users,
with risks rising over time as technology use intensifies.

In an age of constant stimulation, his life poses a question modern
seekers are perhaps too afraid to ask: Are modern comforts truly leading
to real happiness? How can we find stillness in an age of infinite
scroll?

Venerable Ñāṇavimala's profound turning away was not merely a personal
choice, but a rare and authentic expression of his insight into the
Buddha's teachings—the path to escape suffering, not through comforts,
but through wisdom and effort.

And beneath that question lies another one: How do you find the
authentic Buddha's voice through all this noise?

In his course on Early Buddhism, reflecting upon the instance when
Mahāpajāpatī Gotamī - the Buddha’s maternal aunt, foster mother, and the
first ordained nun - came to the Buddha to seek advice, Bhikkhu Sujato,
founder of SuttaCentral and a scholar of early Buddhist texts says:

“*While Mahāpajāpatī (Gotamī) wants to go on retreat, do intensive
meditation, Buddha is not exactly giving her specific meditation
instructions. He is not saying do Ānāpānasati…do this meditation, or
that meditation*”

He identified something telling about the way Buddhism is taught today.

*“In modern Buddhism, when people talk about meditation, they often
focus on details of meditation techniques. In the Suttas we don't find
nearly so much detail about techniques, but a lot more about how you are
living, what is the attitude in your mind, the focus and direction your
mind is taking. These are the things that really matter.”*

When one observes the notes taken by Ven. Gnanaloka in his teacher’s
presence, it becomes clear that this is the exact spirit that Ven.
Ñāṇavimala would guide him.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala was direct and sparing with detail. He would often
prescribe specific *Suttas* or passages, but rarely elaborate on it at
length. Other times he would share a story to inspire or to convey a
certain Dhamma lesson. In meditation, he offered practical suggestions,
yet refrained from outlining fixed techniques.

But being with him was like stepping through a doorway into the Buddha's
way of practice.

During his first retreat with Ven. Ñāṇavimala—then twenty-three years
old and still a layman—Ven. Gnanaloka experienced the power of this
approach. Rather than lengthy explanations, his teacher directed him to
read through key *Suttas*. He gave a captivating explanation on the
conditioned nature of consciousness and what it meant to final
liberation, as Ven. Gnanaloka reflects “It's not like intellectually
pondering on this”, relating how he was trained “we have already had
this habit of applying it to ourselves and looking at it.”

On the last day of that retreat—the fourteenth day, a full
moon—something broke through.

“I will never forget that day,” Ven. Gnanaloka says. “That was the time
I really felt I had faith in Bhante. I also felt: these *Suttas*, 2,600
years old—these are the real teachings. From our level of defilements,
our interpretations have come.”

The importance of the *Suttas* revealed itself “that was the first
occasion where from the *Suttas* I had a perception of Dhamma.” Adding
that “That was enough to inspire me to know that the Buddha is alive in
these teachings. As well as to know that this could be the end of our
journey. There is nothing else to do.”

Ven. Gnanaloka recalls those days fondly. Being alongside his teacher,
he felt an almost kindred connection. “I saw us like comrades in
battle,” he says. A spiritual battle.

He began to understand that true understanding of the *Suttas*
transcends intellectual study—it requires direct experience. These
insights arise naturally in moments of quiet contemplation, when the
mind is relaxed and free from expectation, whether reading a few words
of the Buddha or contemplating a single teaching.

In 2005, Ven. Gnanaloka was staying in a cave in the hills of Badrinath,
a sacred valley sealed off by towering Himalayan peaks. Each day, he
would walk eight kilometers down the mountain for alms, then eight
kilometers back up. He was having difficulties—a foreigner near the
China border (Tibet region), permissions uncertain.

With the rains retreat approaching and needing to find a place to
settle, an attendant devotee invited him to join a pilgrimage north. He
left his bowl and extra robe behind and set out. Only along the way did
he discover the route led to Leh, Ladakh—and he remembered he could
contact a monk there. A senior monk named Bhikkhu Sangasena welcomed him
to stay for the rains.

During his time in Leh, he confessed something to Bhikkhu Sangasena: “I
feel I will not meet Venerable Ñāṇavimala again.”

A few days later, descending from the monastery with an attendant
devotee to make a phone call from a booth in town, he received the news.
His teacher had passed away. The funeral was already over.

There was no grief—his practice had long ago released such attachments.
Only profound gratitude. His teacher had given him everything that
mattered: direct access to the Buddha's teachings, and the living
example of how to practice them.

Several months later, he was invited to the stupa consecration ceremony
of Ven. Ñāṇavimala. A crowd of about a couple hundred gathered. At
Parapaduwa Island Hermitage; an island hermitage adjacent to
Polgasduwa—they expected him to speak—to share memories, to eulogize his
teacher. Even his parents, present in the audience, encouraged him to
say something.

But he refused.

“I was very serious,” he recalls. “I didn't want to be in the public
eye. It was very personal for me—Bhante, my guru. Even the notes were
very personal for me. I was waiting for that time, when it could be
passed on.”

Instead of speaking, he quietly went and worshipped where the ashes were
kept. And in that silence “I made a vow” he says.

*“Though I'm not going to say anything now, in a hundred-fold I will
help them, give them the teaching.”*

This book is the second flowering of that vow.

Something had been pulling him forward long before. When asked decades
later why he'd felt compelled to record everything, he paused. A long
pause.

"Somehow, I knew it was precious," he said finally. "Even then, I didn't
quite know why—only that it had to be preserved."

He had simply acted—reached for the notebook again and again without
asking why. Some instinct beyond language, beyond decision. It felt as
if it had a destiny beyond his own understanding.

When we first proposed creating this book, Ven. Gnanaloka's response was
immediate: “Let it be anonymous. Release the notes.” He insisted the
focus should be on his teacher.

For an ascetic monk who had spent three decades cultivating
self-effacement, the idea of a book bearing his name contradicted
everything he'd trained for. “I am training to dismantle this idea of
self,” he would say—not to perpetuate it. But we had a problem: the
notes alone—concise, sometimes cryptic, written in the shorthand of
direct experience—couldn't reach beyond those already deep in practice.

To serve wider audiences, they needed context: the spirit in which they
were spoken, the question that prompted them, the life that gave them
weight. They needed his voice, his memories, his stories.

Which meant he couldn't remain anonymous.

Eventually, he agreed—not for himself, but for the teachings. For his
teacher's legacy. For the vow he'd made.

The work took nearly two years. Meeting him was no easy matter. As an
ascetic, he moved between an undisclosed location and Sri Sambuddha
Ramaneeya Ashramaya Forest Hermitage—nestled in forest surrounded by
villages. Evening meetings with devotees and young monks would often run
late. We learned to wait.

When we finally did meet—a little over ten times over those two years—he
was remarkably generous with his time. Sessions would begin near
midnight and continue until three or four in the morning. The world was
silent. His recollections, perhaps even sharper at night, ranged freely.

We'd read him his own notes, written decades earlier. Stories spilled
out—details that hadn't made it into the original entries, moments he'd
forgotten, teachings that needed the context of place and time to make
sense. **These became the reflections that accompany the notes in this
book—the stories behind the teachings, the moments that gave them
life.**

His public talks—which we'd studied closely—were measured, each word
like a mindful step. But here, in person what was expressed was deeper.

Months into the process, during one such late-night session, he said
something that stopped us.

“Sometimes when I read,” he began carefully, “I was in tears actually.”

He explained: “I don't go back to memories of those experiences. I am
not trained to go back to those memories that much. It's a big strain
for me. If I want to, maybe I have to specially make an effort.”

But the book had done what his own will wouldn't—it brought him back.

“It brought me to those feelings of *saddha*—faith,” he said. “I'll
never forget. And it's very clear. Bhante's talking of the same points,
especially now when I'm reading—the same way.”

He paused, then added something we hadn't anticipated.

"I was also doubtful—did I tell the exact details?"

We understood then what it meant to be entrusted with something
precious.

Ven Gnanaloka would sometimes tell us as he left Sri Sambuddha Ramaneeya
Ashramaya Forest Hermitage “don’t think we have much time left.” This
cryptic line often puzzled us, however Ven. Gnanaloka explained. Now
having come of age of fifty four, there is great need for service and
fulfilling of his duty. He would compare his life to the timeframe of a
day, “when you look at it from the perspective of a day, I am
approaching the evening.”

This book was carefully reviewed by a team of eight, including Ven.
Bhikkhu Anandajoti and Mr. Chittapala—both instrumental in compiling
‘*Pure Inspiration: Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera—Recollections of His Life,
Practice and Teaching*s’ and who knew Ven. Ñāṇavimala
personally—together with two other monastic practitioners and four lay
practitioners. The process involved extensive research: reviewing
hundreds of hours of Ven. Gnanaloka’s public talks in Sinhala and
English, studying the *Suttas* referenced in the notes, cross-checking
dates and details, and consulting existing literature such as *Pure
Inspiration*.

As for the writing team, between us we brought nearly fifty years of
meditation practice—one with a background in social research and
conducting interviews, and three with management and senior leadership
experience—deeply influenced by time in Saddhammadhara Sutta retreats
with Ven. Gnanaloka, as well as experience in traditions such as the
Thai Forest, Mahāsi, and Goenka lineages. We weren’t monks or scholars.
We were practitioners. We knew the landscape of practice well enough to
recognize authentic teaching when we heard it.

Venerable Gnanaloka trusted us to help bring these teachings forward—a
responsibility we've tried to honor. Much of this work was done at Sri
Sambuddha Ramaneeya Ashramaya Forest Hermitage during retreats—an
environment of practice that shaped our reflections.

The original notes are Ven. Gnanaloka's, written between 1994 and 2001.
The Reflections—the stories surrounding those notes—are our attempt to
transmit faithfully what we researched and heard in those midnight
sessions.

**The Volunteer Writers & Editors**

*<span class="mark">Friedrich Müller (far right), Taken during Asoka
Weeraratna’s visit to Germany to survey Buddhist activities and identify
sites for a Buddhist Centre and Vihara (1953).</span>*

*Buddhist Mission To Germany—Training Centre in Dalugama, Kelaniya
(1953). Left to Right: Mr. Simpson Wijeratne; Ven. Galle Anuruddha; Ven.
Ñânaponika; Ven. Ñânatiloka; Upâsaka Friedrich Möller (later known as
Ven. Ñāṇavimala); Ven. Kudawella Vangîssa; Mr. Asoka Weeraratna
(Secretary, German Dharmaduta Society)*

*Ven. Nyanatiloka Mahāthera—the first European Buddhist monk and founder
of the Island Hermitage—with Samanera Ñāṇavimala, his newly ordained
novice and final disciple, who would later become a legendary cārikā
(wandering) monk (1955).*

**Life and Legacy of Venerable Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera**

Venerable Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera (1911–2005) was a deeply revered yet
elusive ascetic monk who remained almost entirely out of the public
limelight. The unshakeable spirit and wisdom he embodied made him a
quiet legend amongst dedicated practitioners, both lay and monastics.

Born in Germany as Friedrich Möller, one of four children, he displayed
a spiritual inclination from an early age. He grew up in the small
village of Rinteln, near Hannover, in a devout Christian household,
during a time when the Dhamma was not widely known in the West. He
enjoyed sports like athletics and gymnastics—particularly the horizontal
and vertical bars—but felt out of step with the world around him. Even
as a boy, he would slip away to the forest alone, seeking solitude,
neglecting his farm duties. His mother worried about what would become
of such a child.

One time, when asked to slaughter a chicken during farm duties, he found
the experience devastating. That moment left a lasting mark on him, and
he vowed never to do such a thing again. Even then, he seemed to know
that this was not his path.

A chance meeting with an Indian medical student later introduced him to
the Bhagavad Gītā. Its message of compassion and spiritual discipline
resonated with the same tenderness he had shown toward animals. This
awakened in him an aspiration to travel to India.

Leaving Germany was no easy matter however. From 1935, Adolf Hitler’s
consolidation of power began demanding the enlistment of young men in
military service. Though the full wartime draft was not yet active,
Möller, who despised the regime’s militarism and ideology of violence,
needed a way to secure passage out of Europe.

Möller had not qualified for university (the *Abitur*), having failed a
critical requirement — Latin. Instead, he went to Hamburg to pursue
vocational training in business and commercial studies. A few years
before the war began, this training secured him a position as a
stenographer at a Hamburg trading h<span class="mark">ouse—his</span>
narrow passage out of Europe during an increasingly tense time. Ven.
Ñāṇavimala would later remark that his failure to enter university had,
in fact, been his good fortune; had he qualified, he would almost
certainly have been conscripted into the militar<span class="mark">y.
.</span>

The post was initially located in Madras (renamed to Chennai in 1996),
India. He performed his work with utmost capability, enabled by a
concentrative power developed through his personal practice of yoga. He
quickly rose <span class="mark">through</span> the ranks to a top
position. About a year before the outbreak of World War II he was
transferred to Colombo, Ceylon (pre-independence name of Sri Lanka), as
its branch director. This job offered him a pleasant and luxurious life.

But when World War II erupted in 1939, this comfortable life ended
abruptly. Though he had done no wrong, being a German national under the
British colonial rule of Ceylon during that time, meant that he was
classified as an “enemy alien.” Along with other German nationals in
Ceylon, he was interned at Diyatalawa, Ceylon.

As a man in his early thirties, now confined to cell walls, he had to
come to terms with life’s bitter reality—that the comfortable life he
had built was not as secure as he had imagined, and he was powerless to
do anything about it. Through this disillusionment he fell into a
depression. However, a sudden relocation to an Internment Camp in Dehra
Dun, northern India, in 1942, proved to be a hidden blessing. It was
here that the cool breeze of the Buddha’s original words first touched
his heart.

He was placed in the same wing alongside Ven. Ñāṇatiloka Mahāthera—the
esteemed German monk who had brought the light of the Dhamma to Europe
and Asia—along with his German disciple, Vappo. Through Ven. Ñāṇatiloka,
Möller discovered his published book, *The Word of the Buddha*, compiled
from the elder monk’s handwritten notes and regarded as his most
influential work.

First composed in German in the early 1900s, it was intended as a
concise yet complete anthology of the Buddha’s actual words, drawn
directly from the Pāli Canon. Ven. Ñāṇatiloka compiled and arranged key
sutta passages in a way that allowed newcomers and scholars alike to see
the framework of the Dhamma. In later years, it was translated into
multiple languages and became recognised as a foundational text for both
Eastern and Western students of Theravāda Buddhism. For Möller,
encountering these words in their pure form during the solitude of
internment, it clicked in him - “this is it.” The wheel of Dhamma began
to turn within him, never to stop.

Repatriated to Germany in 1946, Möller left behind nearly seven years of
internment as an “enemy alien”—a dark period that however forged his
spiritual conviction and direction.

Post-war Germany was a land of ruin and loss—many young men had
perished. Although he had a strong aspiration to go back to
<span class="mark">Ceylon</span> to ordain as a monk, he was deemed
ineligible because he had not been a monk in Ceylon before the war. Ven.
Ñāṇatiloka and other Buddhist monks however were spared the same fate,
and were able to return immediately with the help of increasingly
politically powerful Sinhalese Buddhists and their several
organisations.[1]

Faced with the harsh prospect of rebuilding his life in a devastated
homeland, Möller took <span class="mark">work</span> as a farm
<span class="mark">hand</span> in his village in the countryside near
Hamburg. However, the only remuneration he received here was free food
and lodging. Work was so demanding that he didn’t have any time to
dedicate to practising the Dhamma. Additionally, he had a sensitivity to
the insects he was unintentionally killing whilst ploughing and the
animals he was harming as part of his farm duties. One day he read a
Dhammapada verse: “*Though one should live a hundred years, heedless and
unvigilant, yet better is one single day’s life of one vigilant and
meditative.*” (Dhp 110). This gave him the impetus to leave for
Hamburg<span class="mark">—</span>the “Gateway to the World.” There, he
managed to find work as an English teacher and was able to stay with his
former landlady, who treated him like the son she had lost in the war.

With many German men having perished in the war, the classrooms were
filled largely with women; Möller, however, maintained his physical
distance, as the call of the Dhamma never left his heart. Post-war
Germany was facing a spiritual hunger, a deep yearning to heal, as many
churches also started <span class="mark">re-es</span>tablishing. Möller
was however able to participate in a different kind of support, by
getting involved in *Sutta* discussion classes organised by a local
Buddhist group.

Hamburg had an emerging Buddhist community—part of a post-war spiritual
revival. It also provided the support Möller needed to fulfill his
spiritual resolve. Through his involvement, he managed to meet Asoka
Weeraratna, the visionary founder of the German Dharmaduta Society
(GDS). Möller translated a speech for Weeraratna at a meeting in Hamburg
in March 1953, leading directly to Weeraratna arranging his passage back
to Ceylon to begin his monastic training.

On September 19, 1955, he was ordained as a novice monk (*sāmaṇera*) at
the Island Hermitage on Polgasduwa, a secluded monastery renowned for
its strict adherence to the Theravāda tradition. His full ordination
(*upasampadā*) followed shortly thereafter, with Ven. Madihe Paññāsīha
Mahā Nāyaka Thero—one of the most respected Sri Lankan scholar-monks and
monastic leaders of the 20th century, as his preceptor.

The Island Hermitage on Polgasduwa was a secluded monastery situated
within a lagoon, founded by Ven. Ñāṇatiloka Mahāthera in 1911. This
hermitage became a center for serious Dhamma practitioners, particularly
Western monks drawn to the Theravāda tradition, including Ven.
Ñāṇatiloka’s discip<span class="mark">le, V</span>en. Ñāṇapoṇika
Mahāthera, whose later writings would help introduce Theravāda Buddhism
to the West.[2] The monastic training and emphasis on the direct study
of the *Suttas* shaped Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s path, leading him to uphold the
purest form of Buddhist monastic life.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala was the last living disciple of Ven. Ñāṇatiloka, placing
him within a distinguished lineage of Western-born monks dedicated to
reviving classical Theravāda Buddhism. Although Ven. Ñāṇatiloka was the
key influence in guiding him onto the path, it was another resident
monk, Ven. Ñāṇamoli Thera, whom he regarded foremost as a mentor and
source of theoretical guidance. Ven. Ñāṇamoli was <span class="mark">an
English monk</span> renowned for his deep study and translation of the
Pāli Canon, having himself learned Pāli from Ven. Ñāṇatiloka.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala resolved that before teaching others the Dhamma, he must
first directly experience its truth himself.

After 11 years of dedicated practice at the Island Hermitage, he took up
the ancient practice of *cārikā*—walking from place to place as a
wandering monk. This was a profound training in freedom, designed to
systematically break all attachments to places, people, and possessions.
He travelled the island on foot for twenty-five years—from south to
north, east to west—meeting people of all walks of life. His journey was
one of silent blessing; each step was a teaching. To those who met him
on the ro<span class="mark">ad—vil</span>lagers, travelers,
housewiv<span class="mark">es—he</span> offered the chance to give alms
and thus earn merit. Occasionally, with just a few words, he would
deliver profound Dhamma teachings that left lasting impressions on those
who heard them.

He would usually stay no more than three days in one place except during
the <span class="mark">rains</span> retreat. The austere practice he
developed was reminiscent of Ven. Mahākassapa Thera, one of the foremost
disciples of the Buddha. Ven. Ñāṇavimala embodied the similar
asceticism, wisdom and unshakeable detachment from worldly concerns.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala by nature kept to himself. He didn’t encourage
disciples, didn’t build monasteries, and didn’t seek followers. He lived
detached—from people, places, even from his own body.

The physically demanding practice of *cārikā* <span class="mark">over
the years</span> took its toll. After 1987, a hip affliction prevented
him from walking long distances, and he finally ceased his wandering
life in 1991. He then spent four years in Colombo at the Siri Vajirarama
temple before returning to the Island Hermitage in 1995. He later moved
to the adjacent, more secluded island of Parappaduva, where,
<span class="mark">in</span> the last years of his life, he was
bedridden and cared for by dedicated attendant monks.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala became an inspiration for many of the most eminent monks
of his era, who recognized his extraordinary practice and unwavering
dedication to the monastic path. A testament to the reverence he
inspired is powerfully conveyed in Pure Inspiration: Venerable
Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera; Recollections of His Life, Practice and Teachings
(2016), which offers first-hand accounts from monks who, at the time,
were young monast<span class="mark">ics—ma</span>ny of whom went on to
become highly respected senior monks or teachers. These include, among
others, the recollections of the late Ven. Gampaha Pemasiri, late Ven.
Katukurunde Ñāṇananda, the late Ven. Ñāṇadīpa, Ven. Bhikkhu Bodhi, Ven.
Ajahn Brahmavaṁso <span class="mark">an</span>d Ven. Guttasīla, who
detail<span class="mark">ed</span> their memorable encounters with him.

On October 10, 2005, at the age of 93, Venerable Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera
passed away on Parappaduwa Island. At the time of his passing, he was
the oldest and most senior Western Buddhist monk in the world. His
passing marked not an end, but the consummation of a life lived with
radical simplicity—a life that had long since transcended the need for
worldly markers. Today, as many of the forests he once trod have been
claimed by <span class="mark">cultivated fields and</span> concrete, his
legacy lives brightest where his teachings are put into practice: in the
mindful step of a *cārikā* bhikkhu, in the stillness between thoughts,
and in the unwavering return to the Buddha’s original words. For those
who encounter his story, he remains what he always was—a mirror
reflecting the Dhamma’s fullest possibility.

**HOW TO USE THIS BOOK**

**What This Book Is**

This is a window into how the Dhamma was communicated, person to person,
from teacher to disciple. At its heart are the teachings of Venerable
Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera—an ascetic wandering monk of rare discipline and
simplicity—captured in the handwritten notes of his student, Venerable
Bambalapitiye Gnanaloka Thero.

For general readers, this book also offers a collection of
reflections—stories of a disciple's training that show what dedicated
practice looks like, inspiring accounts of the Dhamma embodied in daily
life, and the gradual transformation of a student under his teacher's
guidance. These narratives provide an accessible entry into the
teachings, illuminating the challenges faced, the wisdom offered, and
the lived reality of walking the path.

For practitioners, behind these stories lie the original handwritten
notes—a seven-year training curriculum (1994-2001) rooted directly in
the Buddha's *Suttas*. Preserved exactly as recorded, unfiltered, with
minimal edits only for clarity, they offer complete guidance from
ethical foundations through advanced practice, always pointing back to
the Buddha's words as ultimate authority.

For everyone, this book serves as a gateway—not a destination but a
signpost directing you toward the living source: the Buddha's own
teachings in the *Suttas*.

This book is not a biography, nor an abstract study of doctrine, nor a
comprehensive manual. It is an intimate record of authentic
transmission, offered in the hope that these teachings might inspire
your own direct investigation of the path.

**The Offering**

For two and a half decades, these notes remained private—a way of
remembering the Dhamma as it was lived. They now offer:

1.  A rare lineage preserved — Ven. Ñāṇavimala combined the Sutta
    mastery of a Pāli scholar with the iron discipline of a forest
    ascetic. His approach—rooted directly in the Buddha's
    words—represents an almost-lost tradition.

<!-- -->

1.  A complete training path — From layman's ethical conduct to advanced
    monastic practice, these notes trace a gradual, systematic
    development rarely documented in such detail.

<!-- -->

1.  Inspiration for direct investigation — In the spirit of Ehipassiko
    ("come and see for yourself"), this book serves as encouragement to
    undertake your own study of the *Suttas*, which remain the ultimate
    authority.

<!-- -->

1.  A glimpse of what is possible — Ven. Ñāṇavimala's profound austerity
    exemplifies the Dhamma's highest capacity—not as a requirement for
    all, but as a measure of what becomes possible when renunciation is
    perfected.

**The Structure**

Each chapter contains two parts:

**Notes**

Ven. Gnanaloka's original records from 1994-2001. The discourses
themselves were brief—often lasting only fifteen to thirty minutes—but
carried weight that lingered long after. After each meeting, during
retreats stretching weeks or months, he would write down what he could
recall in his own words. Some had to be recopied as the originals,
battered by sun, rain, and long journeys, began to disintegrate.

These notes capture teachings in the concise language of direct
experience—often brief and direct, even abrupt and as they were written
in the language of direct experience.

These are the core instructions—treat them as direct curriculum.

Edits were made only to correct grammar and sentence structure for
readability. In a few instances of ambiguity, minor word adjustments
were made to convey the intended meaning, always after direct
consultation with Ven. Gnanaloka. All *Sutta* references, bracketed
editor's notes \[like this\], and footnotes have been added for clarity.

Wherever the original notes contained words or phrases in ALL CAPS,
underlining, or an asterisk (\*), we have rendered them in **bold** in
this edition to preserve the writer’s emphasis—signalling points
regarded as especially significant or relevant.

**Reflections**

Stories and context illuminating the notes, developed from Ven.
Gnanaloka's public talks, retreat teachings, and interviews. These
provide the atmosphere of practice under Ven. Ñāṇavimala—the questions
that prompted teachings, the impact on a devoted student, and bridges
connecting scattered notes into coherent narrative.

**The Teaching Progression**

For decades, these notes remained private—a way of remembering the
Dhamma as it was lived.

Read in sequence, they reveal the gradual unfolding of a disciple’s
training under a noble master. At first, Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera directed a
young man—unfamiliar with the Buddha’s teaching—towards The Word of the
Buddha by Ñāṇatiloka Mahāthera. From this foundation, he encouraged
Shanaka to live as a good layman: to observe thoughts, words, and deeds,
to care for his parents, and to fulfill everyday duties.

As the training deepened, the advice turned toward facing sensuality and
relationships, exposing the dangers of attachment and the illusion of
security in worldly life. Alongside this, the Mahāthera introduced
meditation—beginning with mettā, mindfulness of breathing, and
Satipaṭṭhāna—always grounding practice in sutta study. He guided Shanaka
through the cultivation of the enlightenment factors, warning against
the temptation of chasing attainments instead of patiently developing
the conditions for wisdom.

In time, the emphasis shifted toward monastic training. The notes
reflect encouragement to prepare for ordination, to learn the
step-by-step discipline of a bhikkhu, and to balance study with
practice. Later still, the guidance moved into advanced teachings such
as the nature of consciousness, and vigilance against Māra’s subtle
snares.

The record is not continuous. Some notes were never written down.
Natural gaps arose when Shanaka was abroad—studying at university in
Cyprus, working in Colombo, on solitary retreats, or during long periods
in India and the Himalayas. What remains, however, carries the full
weight of the teaching and traces the outline of a training both
personal and timeless.

**How to Read**

**For Serious Practitioners**

Read the Notes carefully as direct curriculum, then use Reflections for
context and inspiration. Treat this as a training manual. Test teachings
in your own practice. Return often—let understanding deepen over time.

**For Those New to Buddhism**

The Reflections can be read on their own as inspiring stories—a gentle
introduction to the path. They offer glimpses into what dedicated
practice looks like. When ready, return to the Notes for deeper study.

**For Everyone**

- Read slowly. Don't rush. Let teachings settle.

- Read contemplatively. This is not an academic text to analyze but a
  source for quiet reflection.

- Return often. Meaning unfolds over time and through practice.

- Follow the Sutta references. The notes constantly point to the
  Buddha's words—these are the living sources from which all authentic
  Dhamma flows.

**On Translation and Language**

The Reflections were developed from sources in both English and
Sinhala—Ven. Gnanaloka's recorded talks, retreat teachings, and
interviews as well as literature such as Pure Inspiration. Where
appropriate, quoted remarks have been translated from Sinhala, combined
from multiple instances for clarity, or slightly adapted for
readability. The original meaning and spirit have been carefully
preserved.

This book aims to be both authentic to the source and accessible to a
broad audience. At times, this meant expressing certain Pāli terms and
subtle scriptural meanings in simpler language. While some nuanced
details may not be immediately apparent to readers unfamiliar with the
original terminology, this approach bridges the gap for newcomers while
preserving the essence of the teaching.

On Pāli terms: You'll encounter many—the language of early Buddhist
scriptures. Most are explained in context. A comprehensive Glossary at
the back provides reference for all terms.

**Using the Appendix**

The Appendix is organized for different levels of practice and
commitment:

**For Lay Practitioners**

Curated teachings on ethical conduct, generosity, and meditation
suitable for household life.

**For Monastics**

Comprehensive curriculum addressing training for those who have gone
forth.

**Walking Contemplations**

The precise sequence Ven. Ñāṇavimala would verbalize during *caṅkama*
(walking meditation)—rare insight into a master's moment-to-moment
practice.

**Path to Nibbāna**

A specific, step-by-step sequence Ven. Ñāṇavimala described as leading
to liberation, preserved exactly as given. This direct curriculum
reflects decades of lived practice distilled into essential guidance.

**Complete Sutta List**

All *Suttas* mentioned throughout the book for deeper study.

**This Book as Gateway**

View this as a guide pointing to the ultimate source: the Buddha's own
words. Keep these texts close while reading:

- The Connected Discourses of the Buddha (Saṃyutta Nikāya)

- The Middle Length Discourses of the Buddha (Majjhima Nikāya)

- The Numerical Discourses of the Buddha (Aṅguttara Nikāya)  
  (All translated by Bhikkhu Bodhi)

- The Word of the Buddha by Ven. Ñāṇatiloka Mahāthera

May this book serve as a humble signpost pointing toward liberation.

*<span class="mark">Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera on piṇḍapāta (alms-round)
near Siri Vajirarama Temple, Colombo. His quiet presence halted a young
Shanaka—an encounter that would shape the course of a life (circa
1990s).</span>*

**Prologue**

Sometimes a single encounter can alter the course of an entire lifetime.
For Shanaka Dharmakeerti—later ordained as Venerable Bambalapitiye
Gnanaloka Thero—that moment came on a quiet street in Colombo in 1989.

He was a teenager visiting his grandmother in Bambalapitiya, near Siri
Vajirarama Temple, when she invited him to offer alms to a foreign monk
who had come to their house on *pindapāta* (alms round). While she
stepped into the kitchen, Shanaka remained standing beside the monk. A
stillness emanated from him. A quiet light. The young man could not step
away.

This was Venerable Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera—wandering ascetic, a noble
saint, and a rare embodiment of the Buddha’s path in its purest form.

Shanaka could not have known then that this meeting would mark the
beginning of a lifelong spiritual apprenticeship.

At the time, he was far from any traditional Buddhist influence. At
fourteen, he had moved with his family to Lesotho, Southern Africa,
where his mind became absorbed in the vastness of physics. Like many
young people passionate about science, he was convinced that its laws
alone held the key to revealing the ultimate nature of reality. By
eighteen, while walking along the Caledon River, he experienced a deep,
powerful recognition of impermanence—a moment so vivid it tilted his
life toward solitude and contemplation. It left him with the bold, naïve
conviction that he was his own teacher.

He was still carrying that conviction when he stood beside the monk in
Bambalapitiya.

Days after their first meeting, fate intervened again. In Panadura Town,
Shanaka saw a monk walking barefoot under the midday sun—robes weathered
from the road, carrying a walking stick. His slight frame somehow held
the presence of a giant. It was the same foreign monk—Venerable
Ñāṇavimala. Stopping his motorbike, he offered to bring a bottle of
‘SMAK’ fruit drink. The monk looked at him with eyes that seemed to see
far beyond the surface and said:

“This is not what we expect from you; we want you to follow this path as
well.”

The words rang through him like a struck bell. He had never spoken of
his spiritual leanings to the venerable, yet here they seemed to be laid
bare.

They sat together on the roadside. Shanaka spoke of his experiences of
perceiving impermanence, weaving them with his knowledge of physics and
philosophy. Ven. Ñāṇavimala listened in silence before quietly saying:

“This is also in the mind.”

The sentence cut through every layer of self-assurance. What he had
taken to be profound realisations were seen for what they
were—experiences of the mind. At that moment, the ground shifted. A
knowing emerged: he had found his teacher.

Two days later, he returned to Siri Vajirarama Temple and shared more of
his experiences. Ven. Ñāṇavimala listened and said:

“Read the *Satipaṭṭhāna Sutta*, and everything will be okay.”

From then on, Shanaka began reading the *Suttas* earnestly, seeing them
not just as texts for study but as subjects for contemplation, shaping
his practice around them even while pursuing his studies. Yet, while
working towards a degree in Business Studies in Cyprus, his practice
weakened and even keeping sīla became difficult. By the time he was
twenty-three, the spiritual calling had grown too strong to ignore, and
he returned to Sri Lanka.

Hearing that his teacher was at Polgasduwa Island Hermitage, he went to
seek blessings before leaving for a self retreat at Athdalagala forest
monastery. Instead, Ven. Ñāṇavimala quietly offered:

“There are empty kuṭis here.”

It was a rare invitation to practise under his direct guidance. The
abbot, however, refused to allow a layperson to stay at the monastery,
seeing only a young man in jeans and a T-shirt. But Shanaka was
unwilling to let the opportunity slip away. Inwardly, he resolved to
live outside the island under a tree, to walk for alms in the village
with a plastic bowl, and to visit the hermitage for his teacher’s
guidance.

As Shanaka walked back behind Venerable Ñāṇavimala, heavy with
disappointment, Ven. Ñāṇavimala turned gently toward him and said:

“There may be some *kamma* behind it.”

Ven. Ñāṇavimala advised him to obtain a letter of approval from the
Mahānāyaka Thero[3] at Maharagama Siri Vajiragnana Dharmayathanaya. The
letter was granted by Venerable Madihe Paññāsīha Mahānāyaka Thero—who,
five years later, would become his preceptor. With the letter in hand,
Shanaka returned to Polgasduwa and was finally allowed to stay.

In 1994, during his first retreat there, he experienced a deeper
intuitive connection—not with contemporary forms of Buddhism, but with
the Buddha who had walked the earth more than two and a half millennia
before. Through the *Suttas*, he felt close to that timeless presence,
and in Ven. Ñāṇavimala, he perceived not merely a person, but a living
embodiment of the Dhamma itself. Every two days, he sat before Ven.
Ñāṇavimala, receiving words as rare as they were illuminating, each
pointing him back to the *Suttas*. Days were spent in the library
reading the texts his teacher recommended, contemplating and meditating
on them.

At night, under the dim glow of a kerosene lamp, he carefully recorded
each instruction—knowing he was preserving something precious. Those
notes, guarded for decades, are now offered here. They are not simply
records; they are the living current of a master’s guidance encouraging
a seeker towards the original *Suttas*, capable—if one listens
closely—of altering the course of a life.

*Handwritten notes by Shanka Dharmakeerti, capturing teachings and
reflections from his time with Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera (1994).*

**- Chapter 01 -**

**“Avoid Every Bondage With The World”**

**(February 1994 – April 1994)**

**Polgasduwa Island Hermitage**

**16 February 1994**

- Read the *Word of the Buddha* \[by Venerable Nyanatiloka
  Mahāthera\][4].

- Advice to Rāhula (three ways of conduct)—*Ambalaṭṭhikarāhulovāda
  Sutta* (MN61; advice to Rāhula at Ambalaṭṭhika)[5].

- Study the verses in *Dhammapada* (Dhp; verses of the Dhamma).

**18 February 1994**

- When practicing the three ways of conduct[6], whatever was unwholesome
  should be tackled individually in order to overcome them completely.

- Read *Khandhavagga* (SN; the book of the aggregates)[7]. Especially on
  the burden*—Bhāra Sutta* (SN22.22; the burden).

- Read *Satipaṭṭhāna Sutta* carefully and practice it.[8]

- Read and practice *mettā bhāvanā* as instructed in the book written by
  Venerable Ñāṇamoli Thera \[*The Practice of Loving-Kindness*\].

**20 February 1994**

- Refer *Vatthūpama Sutta* (MN7; the simile of the cloth)*, Sallekha
  Sutta* (MN8; effacement)*, Sammādiṭṭhi Sutta* (MN9; right view)

- Refer *Sa<span class="mark">ṁ</span>yutta Nikāya* regarding sayings on
  the incalculable beginning (SN15; *Anamataggasaṁyutta*)

**22 February 1994**

- Refer *Saḷāyatana Vibhaṅga Sutta* (MN137; the exposition of the
  sixfold base)

**23 February 1994**

- Refer *Saḷāyatanavagga* (SN; the book of the six sense bases)[9]

**9 March 1994**

- When bhāvanā (*samatha*), Sleepiness = Not a good time for *Samatha*.
  To overcome it, refer to the discourse given to the great Moggallāna -
  *Pacalāyamāna Sutta* (AN7.61; dozing). Rubbing the ears, Looking at
  the sky etc. or do some kind of alternative activity.[10]

- Too much *upekkhā* makes you slack in progress (*viriya* is
  necessary).

- Like this you have to balance everything.

- To have a good foundation to continue this practice in every activity
  and all one’s life, one must establish oneself in the three ways of
  conduct, mindfully reflecting in three stages.

- Read some *Suttas* from *Majjhima Nikāya* (MN; the middle-length
  discourses)*, Aṅguttara Nikāya* (AN; the numerical discourses).

- When on retreats, be careful of attachment to food and sleep.

**13 March 1994**

- *Meghiya Sutta* (AN9.3; to Meghiya)

- *Upāli Sutta* (MN56; to Upāli)

- *Sammādiṭṭhi Sutta* (MN9; right view)

- *Ānāpānasati Sutta* (MN118; mindfulness of breathing)

- *MahāSatipaṭṭhāna Sutta* (DN22; foundations of mindfulness)

- *Kāyagatāsati Sutta* (MN119; mindfulness of the body)

- *Udāna* (Ud; inspired utterances)

- *Suttanipāta* (Snp; the collection of discourses)

- *Thera <span class="mark">Gāthā </span>*(Th; verses of the elder
  monks)

<!-- -->

- Wait for some time, you will have more understanding and become more
  mature (regarding marriage).

**16 March 1994**

- *Majjhima* 7, 8, 9, 10—*Vatthūpama Sutta* (MN7; the simile of the
  cloth)*, Sallekha Sutta* (MN8; effacement), *Sammādiṭṭhi Sutta* (MN9;
  right view), *Satipaṭṭhāna Sutta* (MN10; the foundations of
  mindfulness)[11].

- This is the process in *Satipaṭṭhāna* *Dhammānupassanā* (perceiving
  impermanence, one breathes in and out, noting and reflecting the
  impermanence of the breath itself).

- The breath that is sent in, is sent out. Like food when it is sent in,
  it is sent out. In this circumstance, it is unpleasant, but in the
  case of breath, it is not.

- Look at things perceived mentally as or consider them in the same
  manner as when considering with the five senses and build up
  equanimity.[12]

- Whenever there is disturbance, one must see the causes of those
  disturbances. You must remove the causes. The disturbance could be
  external and internal.

- First of all, a good foundation should be laid down with regard to the
  three ways of conduct.

- These things we have seen before. (Referring to what was sensed at the
  time).

- **<span class="mark">Must try to find the way of Removal in the
  *Suttas* as advised by the Buddha if possible.</span>**[13]

- When meditating pleasant peaceful feelings arise.

- When one tries to get the experience again, it might not come.
  Therefore, always see them and must see everything with equanimity.

<!-- -->

- **Always it must be a pleasant abiding. Nothing should disturb. Seeing
  everything as impermanent.**

**21 March 1994**

- Read the *Dhammapada* verses. The ones that strike you, learn them by
  heart.

- Never be upset. Regard everything as impermanent, not yours.

- Try to avoid every bondage with the world and to be free from any
  actions that are *akusala* (unwholesome).

- *Jhāna* is an opening for \[Dhamma\] vision[14].

- When there are disturbances, go one step below. Read the *Suttas*.

- Don’t delay. Make use of the situation. A letter might come, or maybe
  sickness, or maybe an outside condition that might make you leave.

- Fasting is good \[from\] time to time to get rid of cravings for food.
  It is good to avoid certain foods that are attractive. Fast one day
  and eat one day. Fast two days and eat two days. Fast three days and
  eat three days. Fast four days and eat four days….five days and so on.

- This will be helpful, especially when one is going on piṇḍapāta.
  Sometimes you might not get any food.

- “Ah happy do we live, we who have no impediments. Feeders of joy shall
  we be even as the gods of the Radiant Realm” (Dhp 200)

- When drowsiness comes, use one of the techniques said to Mahā
  Moggallāna—*Pacalāyamāna Sutta* (AN7.61; dozing).

- Avoid everything that increases *lobha, dosa and moha* because it will
  bring suffering. Do the things that will reduce *lobha* (greed)*,
  dosa* (hate) *and moha* (delusion); it will bring happiness.

- Always, the world is there to attract you.

- As a layperson, it is a must to observe *sīl* on *poya* days[15]. Time
  to time it is good to retire for a couple of days.

- Due to *kamma* one would get a teacher. Some teachers would tell you
  to read the *Suttas*, learn *Pāli*. That is if you decide to be a
  monk. But make sure you do not take any decision without the liking of
  your father.[16]

- The things that disturb—these things will be there throughout life.
  They are *kamma*. Surely, we have done *akusala kamma* before. What
  you must do is direct the mind slowly in that given condition to turn
  towards the goal. You have to consider.

- If one doesn’t have great understanding, one must think how one has
  endlessly roamed in *samsāra.* See *Anamatagga Saṁyutta* (SN 15;
  connected discourses without discoverable beginning). In order to
  refrain and arouse great energy to practice on the path, in whatever
  condition however unsuitable it may be. Never to deviate from the
  path, always sticking to it. Seeing the danger.

- Always, suitable conditions would not remain. That will be the
  situation throughout life.

- *Dhammapada—*there is advice in it for all situations.

- Surely, we would not last. These Aranyas would vanish. The buildings,
  all these things are artificial. The *sāsana* is declining.[17]

**24 March 1994**

- When unwholesome thoughts arise, try to subdue them mentally. Example:
  by *asubha saññā* \[perception of unattractiveness\]*,* or, if it
  doesn’t work, try reading the *Dhammapada* and practice to remove the
  cause of this. Generally, it is due to ignorance, things that are
  impermanent, and which are not yours.

<!-- -->

- **Say you were a businessman, and that you did your business rightly
  and justly, for birth to take place consciousness is required.**

<!-- -->

- When his body becomes old and when it comes to its end, it cannot
  continue. It needs another body to continue; therefore, it is born
  where necessary conditions would be available (a family that would
  give necessary encouragement and he will grow up to do business).
  Consciousness needs an object to arise.

- If one makes an effort and rises to a certain sphere, one would
  experience the object in that sphere. The effort you make is *kamma*.

- Your object of meditation is the presently arisen consciousness and
  the components constituting that framework, that is past *kamma*[18].

- At the moment of *lokuttara sammā-diṭṭhi* \[supramundane right view\],
  when *nibbāna* \[the unconditioned; the cessation of greed, hatred,
  and delusion\] is an object, one firmly establishes in past
  formation[19].

<!-- -->

- **Read *Sammādiṭṭhi Sutta* (MN9; right view).**

**28 March 1994**

- For the mental factors to arise, this body comes to be, with its
  unique nature and functions, such as the in-and-out breathing etc.

<!-- -->

- Must not expect any vision of Dhamma but just create the conditions
  like sīla and so on. Know that every perception even of Dhamma is
  impermanent, sticking to nothing that is connected to impermanent
  elements.

- If something or an activity is unpleasant, look at it as impermanent,
  not yours, and due to *kamma*, do not be displeased.

- In and out breathing, and the perception arising due to the mind
  element, the process taking place due to our past *kamma*. The body
  and body process have come to be, the way it is, based on past craving
  and ignorance.

- **And one cannot delight in the breath for it is impermanent and it is
  not yours.**

***Vegetarianism***

> Everything that takes place in this world entails killing. Even the
> cultivation of rice. When the field is ploughed, what is prepared you
> eat, making sure there is no intention for something to be killed. The
> best way is to follow the path that will make the stopping of the
> arising of the body that needs to be fed is given more emphasis.
> Rather than being defiled by pride or a certain craving for the idea
> of a better world that all people will stop killing animals. But it is
> only a small amount that is killed by human beings compared to other
> factors that destroy life.

Regarding the *Suttas*:

- *Majjhima* 9, 10—*Sammādiṭṭhi Sutta* (MN9; right view), *Satipaṭṭhāna
  Sutta* (MN10; the foundations of mindfulness).

<!-- -->

- **First, perceiving impermanence and then where there is no craving,
  there is no new clinging, becoming, birth, old age and death.
  *Paṭiccasamuppāda* (dependent origination).**

<!-- -->

- Aṅgulimāla, though he has killed so many people, he has previously
  cultivated the faculties required for Arahantship.

- Everything is conditioned.

- Mustn’t try to reason out things that are said to be unthinkable by
  the Buddha, for example how *kamma vipāka* takes place, the *jhāna*
  etc.

<!-- -->

- **Must stick to facts.**

**1 April 1994**

- Read *Majjhima* 13,14—“quite good” - *Mahādukkhakkhandha Sutta* (MN13;
  the greater discourse on the mass of suffering), *Cūḷadukkhakkhandha
  Sutta* (MN14; the shorter discourse on the mass of suffering)

- Read *Majjhima* 117 too—*Mahācattārīsaka Sutta* (MN117; the great
  forty)

- If there are any disturbances from outside, you remove them in a way
  that you have nothing to regret. Read *Sammādiṭṭhi Sutta* (MN9; right
  view) again and again.

<!-- -->

- **Establish yourself in the three ways of conduct so that at the end
  of the day, when you reflect, you have nothing to regret.**

- **Keep in mind all the time, the four factors - noble friend, reading
  the *Suttas* etc.**[20]**<span class="mark">—*Sotāpattiphala Sutta*
  (SN55.55; the fruit of stream-entry)</span>**

> \- Make a firm determination to stick to the word of the Buddha
> always.
>
> \- Aversion arises because there is a lot of attachment.
>
> \- Memories that arise \[are\] connected to the sensual world.
>
> *\[Personal Note:\]* I have to go back and make a basis (exams and
> subsequent employment) in the world of the senses.[21]

**7 April 1994**

- *Vat Pirith Potha—Maharagama* \[*The Mirror of the Dhamma* compiled by
  Ven. Narada and Ven. Kassapa\]

- *Sutta Nipāta*, *Vijaya Sutta* (Snp1.11; victory over fascination with
  bodies)

- Stick to the facts. See things as they are. Bring the mind under
  control. When meditating on the breath, if the mind strays to any
  object, know that it should be brought back to the breath. The senses
  delight in and search for happiness generally. In general life, we
  avoid unpleasant things and get attached to pleasant things, this is
  done even in meditational experience. So, you must always stick to
  *Satipaṭṭhāna*.

<!-- -->

- Generally, we see the body as pleasant, which is an illusion. The
  actual perception is impurities.

- Sexual urges should not be suppressed, but by constantly perceiving
  the impurities of the body, must make the urge subside.

- Must take all sounds, even mental sounds as distractions and must
  stick to things as they are: the breath. Must stick to facts.

- Must concentrate on the in breath and out breath. The whole body
  means, the whole body of breath. Calming the body means calming the
  process of breath.

- We are always either living in the future or in the past.

- Must stick to the present situation and investigate and see the
  situation as it is or the reality of it.

- When practicing mindfulness of breathing, at that time when certain
  feelings arise, observe them carefully, see things as they are, and
  investigate how they arise, the senses. Thoroughly reflect their
  impermanence, the nature of not belonging to you.

**12 April 1994**

- Taking part in Sinhalese New Year is only a convention. It is better
  to spend that time striving on the path and that merit could be
  transferred to relatives. For the wishes of the people who are pure
  and striving resolutely on the path are effective.

- Maintaining equanimity is very important, and developing and
  cultivating it, so that there is no more forming or constructing of
  rise and fall, with intentions, efforts and craving.

- Commentaries are useful; the *Suttas* that strike you very much, go
  through them again and again. But all of them have the same principle.

- Must do what your parents say so that you would not have remorse
  later.

- Not mentally expecting any particular vision or something. It is
  continuously observing the real situation. *Satipaṭṭhāna*\* (breath
  also one should not imagine but should grasp what actually is).

- When you are in retreat, you must continuously try to keep your mind
  not wandering by sticking to the object of meditation or second-best
  thing, reading the *Suttas*. Of course, you have to keep this body
  clean and the dwelling, the surroundings. You may attend to these
  things with full attention mindfully as to what you are doing, when
  finished doing the job, one may come and sit down to meditate and
  reflect carefully of the memory of the last work done, and there is no
  disturbance, then one must think of something or recite which will
  calm oneself quickly, that one has to know, after that one can rest
  the mind on the object of meditation e.g. breath etc. (breath also one
  should not imagine but should grasp what actually is).

- In the beginning it is natural to conceptualize.

**18 April 1994**

- \[Quote from Ven. Ñāṇavimala\] I also had a migraine; it was there for
  some time. To overcome it, I started yoga, and became a vegetarian.
  Nothing worked, but when I entered the Saṅgha, when I looked at it
  from the point of view of Dhamma, it all vanished. There is past
  *kamma*. Have you read anything of Cayce? \[Edgar Cayce\][22]. In a
  dream, it was (consciousness). I got to know the cause of my migraine.
  I was a soldier in the army. I had inflicted pain. Then I knew the
  cause.[23]

<!-- -->

- So don’t worry about these things. Any illness or pain, don’t try to
  overcome it by this action or that which is *Sīlabbata-parāmāsa*
  \[clinging to mere rules and rituals\]. Avoid that which increases it
  (whatever that is unpleasant). Practice of *Satipaṭṭhāna* is very
  effective. Maintain equanimity, let that *kamma* pass on. When that
  past *kamma* is finished, the pain will also be over.

<!-- -->

- **Look at the body as if from outside—the perceptions, consciousness
  and feelings and see them as not yours and therefore maintain
  equanimity.**

<!-- -->

- Keep the mind detached, don’t let the mind be defiled by the pain.
  Make sure you do not act in the present in such a way that pain will
  arise in the future. Make sure all unwholesome activity is avoided.

<!-- -->

- **Try to live as minimally as possible.**

<!-- -->

- Think how the Pacceka Buddhas lived and died at Isigili—*Isigili
  Sutta* (MN116; Isigili: the gullet of the seers). You will show the
  highest gratitude by practice.

<!-- -->

- **Get established in *Satipaṭṭhāna*.**

<!-- -->

- Don’t have any hopes or wishes. Try to cut down any bondage with the
  world of the senses. Be detached from the past, the future and the
  present.

<!-- -->

- **When faced with the world, when one restrains and controls, and
  maintains a certain level of detachment or equanimity, that is *kamma*
  and it will bear its fruits in due course of time. (Food should be
  taken as medicine).**

**21 April 1994**

- For the *sāsana* to last long and prosper, the best service you could
  \[<span class="mark">administer\]</span> is to yourself, properly make
  an endeavor to establish oneself in the proper Dhamma.

<!-- -->

- **But if you try to change or get involved in changing the present
  situation, either the Bodhi pūjās \[worshiping the Bodhi tree\] or the
  conduct of the bhikkhus, even what is left will disappear. So be
  careful, do your own practice in accordance with the word of the
  Buddha, but don’t encourage or assist the wrong practices, in any
  circumstance, it is like swallowing red hot iron balls (using money as
  a bhikkhu etc.)—\[*Aggikkhandhopama Sutta* (AN 7.68; fire)\]**

<!-- -->

- When you go to different established places you should compromise
  according to their rules and regulations, but you must see that they
  are in accordance with the word of the Buddha.

<!-- -->

- **You must observe this (*piṇḍapāta*) with Right View
  (*sammā-diṭṭhi)*.**

<!-- -->

- It is alright if you go on *piṇḍapāta* taking the 8 precepts with the
  white overall. It is good to go to a solitary place on a full moon day
  and observe \[precepts\]. It is good if circumstances permit, to
  observe 8 precepts on the other 4 *Poyas*. Failing that, choose the
  Sunday closest to the *Poya* or whenever there are holidays.

<!-- -->

- **Everyday, you must do things carefully so that, when you reflect in
  the night, you have nothing to regret in regard to the three ways of
  conduct.**

- **When the mind is restless, you may read particular *Suttas* that
  struck you. You may read them over again and again. You may study and
  memorise the verses in *Dhammapada*, that particularly interest you.**

- ***Milindapañha* (Questions of King Milinda)**[24]**: it is alright if
  you read them but accept the ones that are in accordance with the
  *Sutta* and *Vinaya <span class="mark">piṭaka</span>*.**

- **So always stick to the *Suttas*.**

<!-- -->

- The higher rules are there to arouse the *jhāna*. The minor rules are
  not necessary. But when one is directed to purifying the three ways of
  conduct, then the rules are automatically observed.

- You may continue not to eat after 12 o’clock. If it affects the health
  or if you are involved in a lot of work you may eat something small
  (milk or 2 slices of bread). But on *Poya* days, milk is also not
  permitted, water or tea etc. \[can be consumed instead\].

**27 April 1994**

- The seed that you have planted, the tree that you have grown, you must
  look after it, protect it, water it and tend to it carefully. So that
  it will come to full growth with flowers and fruit. Don’t let it be
  destroyed.

- Like a sailor when crossing a river, slowly, not losing the other
  shore, direct the boat slowly, gradually cutting the stream.
  Similarly, you should direct your life.

- Read *Sutta* No. 2 of *Majjhima Nikāya*—*Sabbāsava Sutta* (MN2; All
  the Taints).

- Don’t let the Dhamma seed that you have planted be destroyed. Look
  after it, protect it, and tend to it so that it will grow, flourish
  and bear fruit.

**30 April 1994**

- Always make sure to purify the mind.

- Purified mind brings purified action.

- The Buddha left everything, settled to purify himself and served all
  beings in the highest.

<!-- -->

- **<span class="mark">Though we are separate we will be united in
  mind.</span>**[25]

- **Avoid *akusala* \[unwholesome deeds\] even if you have to go through
  hardship.**

<!-- -->

- Do things slowly, carefully and mindfully and always watch the three
  ways of conduct and just wait.

- Do *Metta bhāvanā* \[loving-kindness meditation\] and do a bit of
  *Ānāpānasati* \[mindfulness of breathing\].

- Open your eyes.

- Be an example to others. It is a skill, the way one directs everything
  towards Dhamma.

- \[Like\] Nakulamātā and Nakulapitā \[who were regarded by the Buddha
  as exemplary householder disciples\].

- Direct the others in the highest—wish them the highest.

> *“Abhivādanasīlissa, niccaṁ vaddhāpacāyino*
>
> *cattāro dhamma vaddhanti, āyu vaṇṇo sukhaṁ balaṁ* (Dhp 107)
>
> *sukhaṁ niddukkhā nirogyā nibbāna paccayā”*
>
> All blessings.
>
> \[“*For one who is respectful and always honors their elders, four
> things increase: life, beauty, happiness, and strength.*
>
> *Happiness comes from being free from suffering, free from illness,
> and from the conditions leading to liberation.*”—A traditional
> blessing commonly recited by monks at the end of a discourse\]

*<span class="mark">Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera walking mindfully from the
monks’ dining hall to his kuti at Polgasduwa Island Hermitage— though
his body had grown frail with age, his composure and presence reflected
the exceptional mindfulness for which he was revered, steady and
luminous to the very end (circa mid–late 1990s).</span>*

**Reflection 01: Living as an Island Unto Oneself**

Living in close association with Venerable Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera often
brought to mind the Buddha’s final instructions to the Saṅgha, as
recorded in the *Mahāparinibbāna Sutta (DN 16)*. Speaking to Venerable
Ānanda shortly before his passing, the Buddha said:

*“Tasmātihānanda attadīpā viharatha attasaraṇā anaññasaraṇā, dhammadīpā
dhammasaraṇā anaññasaraṇā.”*

“Therefore, Ānanda, be islands unto yourselves, be your own refuge,
having no other; let the Dhamma be your island, let the Dhamma be your
refuge, having no other refuge.”

This single instruction seemed to encapsulate the way Ven. Ñāṇavimala
lived. He was beholden to no institution, title, reputation, or
following. His refuge was not in monasteries or teacherhood—it was
solely in the Dhamma.

One day, Shanaka—later to become Venerable Gnanaloka—saw him slowly
making his way back to his kuti. At that time, Ven. Ñāṇavimala was
already well into his eighties. Decades of walking barefoot across Sri
Lanka had worn his body thin. A hip injury sustained in his youth had
worsened with age. He leaned heavily on his cane, walking slowly, each
movement deliberate.

Out of concern, Shanaka approached and asked, “Bhante, can I help you?”

Ven. Ñāṇavimala slowly turned, his eyes calm and bright, and replied,

“This body is not mine—why should I give it to somebody else?”

That response left Shanaka speechless. In just a few words, Ven.
Ñāṇavimala revealed what deep letting go truly looked like—not as a
concept or philosophy, but as lived reality. This was the source of his
unshakeable composure: when the body is no longer taken as ‘self,’ by
means of wisdom its inevitable decay is no longer a personal tragedy.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala never claimed disciples and never sought to build a
following. Yet those who approached him with sincerity found themselves
quietly and often deeply guided.

He would often say: “If anyone is sincere, they will guide you to the
*Suttas*.”

And he meant it. To him, the Buddha was not a distant figure from the
past—the Buddha lived through the Dhamma. The early *Suttas* weren’t
relics but living instructions meant to be practiced. He would often
stress that the teachings are within reach for anyone who genuinely
seeks them.

Although he never took on the title of *Ācariya* (teacher), from the
moment Shanaka met him to the time of his passing, Ven. Ñāṇavimala
offered steady guidance. Sometimes he pointed Shanaka to the *Suttas*;
sometimes, he gave direct advice—often only fully understood years or
even decades later. But more than anything, it was his presence—his
silence, his unwavering commitment to the path—that deeply shaped the
young aspirant’s mind and heart.

In Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s final years, Shanaka had the opportunity to serve
him closely. Through those quiet, unspoken moments, he tried to express
the immense gratitude he felt toward his teacher. But in time, he came
to understand that true repayment doesn’t come through service or
offerings—it comes only by walking the very path that Ven. Ñāṇavimala
had lived so fully.

Though they first met in late 1989, it was not until 1999—after much
hesitation—that Shanaka finally decided to ordain. He felt torn between
two worlds: a strong pull toward ascetic life, and a lingering
doubt—could he truly live a celibate life for the rest of his years?

For nearly three years, Ven. Ñāṇavimala gently encouraged him—never
forcefully. He would sometimes remark, “How great it would be if you
were to ordain.” On another occasion he even went as far to suggest:
“for one year you can ordain, then you can disrobe again.”

One day, hearing his encouragement again, Shanaka responded with
frustration: “The temples are deteriorating, the forest monasteries are
deteriorating, the monks are deteriorating...”

Without missing a beat, Ven. Ñāṇavimala replied,

“Your consciousness is deteriorating.”

The remark landed with striking clarity. Shanaka had no response—just
silence.

And he was right.

Yet, confidence to take the next step did not come immediately.

Sometime later, while on a solitary retreat at Athdalagala forest
monastery in Kurunegala—spent meditating, reading the *Suttas*, and
reflecting quietly—something shifted. The doubts dissolved, and
hesitation fell away. A deep clarity emerged: it was time.

The following morning, Shanaka boarded a bus to Colombo, following the
advice of the forest monastery’s abbot to act swiftly. A part of him
feared that Ven. Ñāṇavimala might pass away before he could ordain in
his presence.

A quarter century has passed since that moment, and now, as Ven.
Gnanaloka, he encounters others who come seeking guidance on entering
monastic life. Even as he guides others, his reverence for his teacher
remains absolute. He reflects that even after decades of intense
practice, he has yet to match the profound wisdom and unwavering
dedication of Ven. Ñāṇavimala. As he often says, “I am but a tiny speck
of dust compared to my teacher.”

Following the example of his own teacher, he does not see himself as a
teacher. Instead, he simply tries to hold space—offering guidance when
needed, and gently pointing them to the *Suttas*, where the true teacher
still speaks.

As the Buddha instructed, the goal is not to rely on others, but to
stand firm—an island unto oneself.

*Handwritten notes by Shanka Dharmakeerti, capturing teachings and
reflections from his time with Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera (1994).*

**- Chapter 02 -**

**“The Buddha Never Lets You Down”**

**(May 1994 – June 1994)**

**22 May 1994**

- The phenomena that you encounter, look at them as things conditioned
  and subjected to change.

- You may practice *mettā* *bhāvanā*.

- When you practice *Satipaṭṭhāna*, greed, hate and delusion slowly
  reduces.

- **Read the verses in the *Dhammapada* (Dhp; verses of the Dhamma), and
  memorise some.**

- Advice to Rāhula - *Ambalaṭṭhikarāhulovāda Sutta* (MN61; advice to
