  Rāhula at Ambalaṭṭhika).

- *Girimānanda Sutta* (AN10.60; to Girimānanda).

- *Mahāparinibbāna Sutta* (DN16; the Great Passing).

- *Word of the Buddha* \[by Venerable Nyanatiloka Mahāthera\].

- Never let the mind be in a state of worry or fear. Always, it should
  be a pleasant abiding.

- When we are practicing, though we may be far away in body, we will be
  united in mind.

- Practice the three ways of conduct, so that there would be a sound
  basis, you may reflect on your actions every evening, to see if there
  was any transgression, and determine to avoid it in the future.

- Recommended āraṇyas \[forest monasteries\]: Galduwa, Wathuruwila,
  Thapovanaya (Angoda), Kalugala.[26]

- If you become a monk, you may not get the chance in the beginning; to
  practice the way you did when you were here (2.5 months). You might
  have to get involved in activities like learning Pāli and so forth.
  After five years you may spend more time on the practice.

- **May you be of service to your relatives and later to all beings.**

**21 June 1994**

- You mustn’t plan anything, if you do you might face disappointment.
  But you must try to come close to Dhamma in that given environment.

- You must realise *saṁsāra dukkha* \[the suffering inherent in the
  cycle of repeated existence\].

- There is attachment and aversion due to ignorance.

- Everything in the world is covered by a thin surface. If it is
  penetrated there would not arise attachment.

- Sometimes there is a danger that some people get used to repulsive
  perceptions, and it changes to a pleasurable activity. E.g. Doctors
  who dissect, people who catch fish—they get used to doing evil.

- <span class="mark">Remember these examples: Seeing the open ulcer in
  the body, he became a hermit in Egypt.</span>[27]<span class="mark">  
  The other one who was too tall, so then her head was chopped off to
  put the body in the coffin. Seeing the head.</span>[28]
  <span class="mark"></span>

<!-- -->

- One day reverend sir visited a dissecting theatre of human beings, a
  leg here, some flesh there and so on.

- **The Buddha never lets you down**

- Do something \[occupation\] that would give enough time and would be
  in accordance with Dhamma.

- Don’t think of a particular teacher and so on.

- All the time there would be obstacles. You must work for it as a
  layman. Then things would change if you do things genuinely.

- There should be a good basis in meritorious actions, and the three
  ways of conduct are very important for a good foundation.

- When you make a determination, that is *kamma*; it will bear fruit in
  the future.

- You are facing this situation (studies) because you have given support
  previously, and now you are getting it. Something you liked and valued
  previously.[29]

- When your father supports you in this way, he will get similar support
  in the future. If he supports you spiritually, he will get the same in
  the future.

- Don’t take any steps without your father’s approval.

- All the people in Sri Lanka were in India before.

- What is inside will manifest to outside<span class="mark">.</span>[30]
  <span class="mark"></span>

*Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera reading in the library at Polgasduwa Island
Hermitage. For him, the Buddha was not a distant figure of history, but
a living presence—guiding the earnest practitioner through his words
preserved in the Suttas (circa 1990s).*

*.*

**Reflection 02: Returning to the Source**

When Shanaka first met Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera as a young layman, he
was quietly swept up in the charisma of the elder monk’s presence. There
was nothing overt in Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s manner—if anything, it was
understated and austere. Yet even in his eighties, with a body worn and
frail, there was an extraordinary stillness and alertness in each step.
Every movement seemed to arise from a deep inner silence.

One day at Polgasduwa Island Hermitage, Shanaka stood watching Ven.
Ñāṇavimala walk toward the dining hall. A sense of awe filled him; the
elder monk’s stillness and compassion felt majestic. In that moment,
Shanaka saw him not just as a guide, but as the embodiment of the path
itself—his true teacher.

As if sensing this very thought, Ven. Ñāṇavimala suddenly paused and
turned. His calm, piercing gaze met Shanaka’s eyes as he said:

“Your teacher and my teacher is the Dhamma—Vinaya.”

The words landed with the force of a direct answer, penetrating
Shanaka’s heart and remaining with him for life.

In time, Shanaka came to understand what Ven. Ñāṇavimala was pointing
to. In the world, even the most sincere teachers can mislead—however
unintentionally. Devotion can become entangled with personality,
lineage, or charisma. But the Buddha—the true refuge—never lets one
down. The Buddha made no promises beyond what could be seen and verified
directly. He asked for no blind faith, only careful and honest inquiry.

This very inquiry led him to appreciate the difficulty many modern
seekers face. He began to understand why the Buddha’s words, though
timeless, could feel so distant to modern seekers—and why contemporary
voices often seemed to reach them more easily. The gap wasn’t only time
or culture—it was about resonance. The unadorned power of the ancient
words carried a subtle vibration that minds conditioned by constant
noise struggled to detect. To truly hear the *Suttas*, the mind itself
had to be quieted, re-attuned. He found that stillness first in the
forests, where the primordial silence carried the same quality as the
Buddha’s voice.

Through his practice, he came to see the critical difference. While the
words of modern commentators could be like a reflection of the moon, the
*Suttas* were the moon itself. He affirmed that the *Suttas*
“may<s>-</s>look simple, but they point clearly to that insight.”

He further reflected on the sacred support of places he practised in Sri
Lanka, noting; “where arahants and beings practiced…. We get that faith
and the mind inclines towards that.” Yet he came to see that within its
nature lay only a training ground. The true seclusion the Buddha taught
was internal—a stilling of the mind and a guarding of the senses that
could be accessed anywhere. He would later experience this with profound
clarity in the midst of a crowd of grieving family and relations after
his cousin went missing; even there, the stillness of a guarded mind
allowed the Dhamma to be his refuge.

To Shanaka, it became apparent that the *Suttas* were like a well-lit
path through a tangled forest. They were direct, practical, and
complete. If followed sincerely and attentively, they would lead to
freedom. That moment near the dining hall had not been just a passing
remark—it was Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s way of pointing Shanaka away from
himself and back toward the source. Don’t cling to me—or to anyone. Go
to the Dhamma. Trust the Buddha.

Although Ven. Ñāṇavimala lived as a *cārikā* bhikkhu and never claimed
to have students, many were quietly drawn to him. Even senior monks
sought him out. Their mutual preceptor, Venerable Madihe Paññāsīha
Mahāthera, held Ven. Ñāṇavimala in deep respect. So did venerable
monastics like Bhikkhu Bodhi, Ven. Katukurunde Ñāṇananda, Ven. K.
Pemasiri, Ajahn Brahm, and many others.

Prominent lay meditation teachers also drew great inspiration from him.
During his time at Polgasduwa, Shanaka met individuals such as Godwin
Samararatne, founder of the Nilambe Meditation Centre, and Brindley
Ratwatte, the key figure in establishing Dhamma Kuta, the first Sri
Lankan meditation centre in the Sayagyi U Ba Khin / Goenka tradition.
Both had been inspired by Ven. Ñāṇavimala and had come seeking his
guidance. Years later, Brindley Ratwatte ordained as Venerable
Siddhartha, and Ven. Ñāṇavimala asked Ven. Gnanaloka to support the
newly ordained monk during his early time at Polgasduwa.

Whether they were monastics or laypeople, Ven. Ñāṇavimala never allowed
himself to be elevated or revered as a “guru” or spiritual figurehead.
Though his presence was undeniably compelling, he refused to let
personal devotion obstruct the path. Again and again, he directed others
not to himself—but to the Buddha.

“If anyone is sincere, they will guide you to the *Suttas*.”

That simple line, often repeated by Ven. Ñāṇavimala, was not merely
advice—it embodied the very core of his teaching. It became the
foundation upon which Shanaka, who would later ordain as Ven. Gnanaloka,
built his entire path. It remains the guiding light by which he
continues to live.

The lesson of the encounter was clear. Even the most gifted teachers may
offer invaluable guidance and inspiration, but they can also falter, for
charisma fades, institutions shift, and personalities fail. The only
ultimate refuge, as Ven. Ñāṇavimala himself would say, is the timeless
Dhamma, for ‘the Buddha never lets you down.’

*Handwritten notes by Shanka Dharmakeerti, capturing teachings and
reflections from his time with Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera (1994).*

**- Chapter 03 -**

**“The Way You Deal With Your Parents”**

**(Oct 1994 – Dec 1994)**

\[NOTE: Content added by the editor is highlighted in blue/red for ease
of reference\]

**26 October 1994**

- It is good to start the practice early in life (earlier the better).

- You must see in your own self \[the body\] the impurities and how it
  is rushing towards old age.

- When one sees this with full concentration, there is no craving
  (*<span class="mark">paṭiccasamuppāda</span>*). You must also see the
  other bodies with the same destiny and impure nature.

- When you are old, it is difficult.

- Watch over the three ways of conduct[31] and practice *mettā*
  *bhāvanā*, and wait.

- If you have to do a job \[get one\] that will give you enough time and
  is in accordance with Dhamma.[32]

**28 October 1994**

- *Suttas* to Mahānāma. Though they were laymen they attained the path.
  Practice the way it is taught there. *Buddhānussati, Dhammānusati and
  Saṅghānusati* then *Mettā, anicca saññā.*[33]

- As a layman or a bhikkhu, in any case, *Majjhima*
  61—*Ambalaṭṭhikarāhulovāda* *Sutta* (MN61; advice to Rāhula at
  Ambalaṭṭhika).

- *Majjhima* 107, 125 gradually to *jhāna* and the *anicca* of
  that—*Gaṇakamoggallāna Sutta* (MN107; to Gaṇaka Moggallāna),
  *Dantabhūmi Sutta* (MN125; the grade of the tamed).

- *Majjhima* 64—*Mahāmāluṅkya Sutta* (MN64; greater discourse to
  Māluṅkyaputta)

- Word of the Buddha \[by Ven. Nyanatiloka Mahāthera\] (not what is
  mentioned in the commentaries). But follow advice strictly from the
  early Buddhist texts.[34]

- *Mahā Parinibbāna Sutta* (DN 16) (there is a lot of valuable advice in
  it)—*Mahāparinibbāna Sutta* (DN16; the Great Passing).

- Word of the Buddha \[by Venerable Nyanatiloka Mahāthera\] has a very
  good right view (*sammā-diṭṭhi)* in essence. You can practice
  *ānāpānasati* but with Right View (*sammā-diṭṭhi)*.

- Soon as you enter the Saṅgha, there is certain training one has to do
  (new bhikkhus). Venerable Mettāviharī knows the *Suttas* and their
  grouping very well.

<!-- -->

- **Now you are 24, you may have to start an apprenticeship which your
  father would also like. Then before you take up another job or
  anything, take a one year holiday. Then you may visit certain
  *aranyas* and stay for a couple of months and see the practice.**

<!-- -->

- I recommend two *aranyas*, Galduwa and Wathuravila and also Kanduboda.
  They have branches.

- But you must make sure you are given all opportunities to devote fully
  to the practice otherwise impossible to attain to the path.

- If you decide to be a monk after that one year, make sure it is under
  those conditions. Otherwise, it is better to be a layman.

- The Saṅgha may disappear any moment.

<!-- -->

- **If you are a laymen try to join an organization like Red Cross or
  civil service where you can perform action doing merit at the same
  time supporting the body.**

<!-- -->

- If you take up a job in the management field you may earn a lot of
  money and now you are 24 but 25,26,27, these ages you will be
  approached by parents and relatives to have a partner. Here you have
  to make a decision. *\[Personal Note:\]* <span class="mark">I must be
  frank about this \[about marriage\]</span>

- Jotipāla and Ghaṭikāra (MN 81): Ghaṭikāra, he was a layman—an anāgāmī,
  who reached the suddhāvāsa \[*Brahma world*\]. Jotipāla joined the
  Saṅgha, attained nothing at that birth and became an arahant as
  Sammāsambuddha \[Gotama\] before Ghaṭikāra.

*\[Quote from Ven. Ñāṇavimala\]* So I don’t know, these are only
suggestions. You might know a better way.[35]

- At this stage, it is most important to do your studies properly.

- I really don’t know your exact intentions, all this Dhamma knowledge
  etc. (the reverend sir seems to ask “for what”)[36]

<!-- -->

- **All my blessings to your endeavors.**

**29 October 1994**

- How much work you have in the layman’s life, still you should allocate
  certain time for the practice and in any case during *poya*
  \[holiday\] you should take *sīl*. If possible before and after *poya*
  you may retire \[for\] 2 or 3 days at a stretch.

- You need merit, don’t fear merit.

- The Buddha was once asked why he was so powerful? etc. He replied how
  he did *mettā bhāvanā* and how he rose in the Brahma world and how he
  became the *Cakkavatti king* many times as a result of his *puñña
  kamma*.

<!-- -->

- **Learn to experience everything from a distance, as everything is
  *anicca, dukkha and anatta*.**

**30 November 1994**

- Were you free from disturbance from inside and outside. From inside it
  is not possible to be free from stray thoughts. Unless you attain
  *jhāna* where you have one thought.

- To attain *jhāna*, you should be highly *sīlavanta* (virtuous). The
  *Ambalaṭ<span class="mark">ṭhika Rāh</span>ulovāda Sutta* (MN61;
  advice to Rāhula at Ambalaṭṭhika); in this *Sutta* not only verbal and
  physical, but mental. So, you must purify the mind. It is based on the
  state of mind that one’s physical and verbal actions follow.

<!-- -->

- **Therefore, the mind should be watched as in *Sutta* No. 19 in
  *Majjhima Nikāya*—*Dvedhāvitakka Sutta* (MN19; two kinds of
  thought)*,* and controlled as mentioned in *Sutta* 20—*Vitakkasaṇṭhāna
  Sutta* (MN20; removal of distracting thoughts)**

<!-- -->

- You may read *Khandha Vagga* (SN22; connected discourses on the
  aggregates) and *Saḷayātana Vagga* (SN35; connected discourses on the
  six sense bases).[37]

- and note down striking passages in Pāli. Repeat it and go through them
  slowly.

- When you are a layman, you must make sure to follow what was advised
  to Mahānāma[38].

- *Samādhi: mettā bhāvanā, Satipaṭṭhāna, Cāga*: giving up (even the
  hindrances), *Bahussuta* \[knowledgeable\]: reading the *Suttas* daily
  a bit.

- *Mettā bhāvanā* should be practiced as advised by Ñāṇamoli Thera \[The
  Practice of Loving-Kindness\].[39]

  - You must get *samvega* by reading what is mentioned in the
    *Maharagama Pirith Book* \[*The Mirror of the Dhamma* compiled by
    Ven. Narada and Ven. Kassapa\]

  - *Caturarakkha bhāvanā* \[Four Protective Meditations\]

- If you want to be a bhikkhu, you must really look into your heart.

**25 December 1994**

- Explanation of walking \[meditation\] of Rev. Ñāṇavimala, from the
  kuṭi to dāna sālā (*sati sampajañña*[40]), it is looking at the body
  from outside as if looking at another person, especially the movement
  of the legs and seeing the impermanence in the movement of the legs
  constituted by flesh, bones, and in this way establishing dispassion
  and at other times sitting down to meditate and attain *jhāna*.[41]

- Working is a good idea, because of your concentration you may achieve
  good results in meditation \[and at work\]; and there may arise
  temptation when higher things are offered \[at work\] and at that time
  you must be careful.

- Do not stick to one meditation object, but to eradicate different
  kinds of defilement you should choose \[different meditation objects\]
  and practice.

- Verses in the *Dhammapada* and *Word of the Buddha* are good.

- Practice *mettā bhāvanā*, don’t just wish happiness, but do it in the
  way taught by Ven. Ñāṇamoli \[The Practice of Loving-Kindness\].

- Don’t pray or wish that you should come to this or that, there should
  not be any tension.

- You must meet the teacher and discuss when there is a disturbance of
  thought or such like.

**26 December 1994**

- **Consider the way you deal with your parents when dealing with other
  Godly beings. You must work and do your duty in such a way to get
  their blessing. But do not go for ultimate refuge, as they are still
  in *saṁsāra*. \[You\] must go for refuge in Dhamma.**

<!-- -->

- We have created this situation.

<!-- -->

- **Try to practice this awareness of the body (that is, as if looking
  at it from outside) or as if observing another body, cultivate it or
  practice it as much as possible because at that time you are living in
  the present.**

- **Read, grasp and practice a couple of *Suttas*. Concentrate on
  them.**

<!-- -->

- Don’t try to memorise a lot of *Suttas*, like in the case of a
  scholar. Practice is important.

<!-- -->

- **It is very important, this practice (to reflect on all activities
  during the day and to think over these activities one by one before
  your sleep, then to make determination \[*adhiṭṭhāna*\] to avoid any
  activities you did the previous day that are regrettable).**

> It is good to end the day with *Mettā* *bhāvanā*, after doing
> Buddhānussati \[recollecting the Buddha’s virtues\] and when doing
> *Mettā* *bhāvanā* in the end reflect on the impermanence of that also.

- **Father too will definitely come to understand. He will definitely
  give his deep blessings.**

- **When certain impressions come, when reading or reciting the gāthās
  or *Suttas*, don’t sort of feel, I should immediately go, get there
  and things like that**[42]**. No frustration, wishes and so on.**
  **You must remember that situations change.** So, you should work for
  it in the desired direction continuously and constantly. Like when you
  desire to reach a certain place (Kandy city), simply by wishing you
  would not get there. But if you start moving in the right direction
  and working towards it, stage by stage, you would finally come there.
  It is the same way.

**27 December 1994**

- There should be a good foundation of *sīla* and then *jhāna* and
  *Satipaṭṭhāna*.

- First 10 *Suttas* in *Majjhima Nikāya* leading to final
  liberation.[43]

- Purified mind is followed by purified action. It is important to first
  concentrate on the state of mind then after that other action will be
  all wholesome (first *Dhammapada* verses[44]).

- Read a couple of *Dhammapada* verses daily.

- While attending \[to\] normal activities, it is not possible to attain
  the *jhānas*. But when you practice *sati* or awareness, then when the
  time for sitting meditation, it is more supportive to attain the
  *jhānas*.

- When you practice this awareness of the body (that is looking at it as
  if another body) and the things that constitute the impurities, then
  there is a turning away of the mind from the body (dispassion), then
  due to this a certain feeling of peace may arise. Now one must
  understand the impermanence of feeling and should not accommodate any
  craving for it. When one does that clinging (*upādāna*), existence
  (*bhava*), birth (*jāti*), old age, sickness—all this is not there
  (*paṭicca samuppāda*).

- But to do all this one must build a sound foundation of *sīla*.

- Don’t think about what to do in the future or how to act because all
  situations change. When the time comes, consider and decide.

**28 December 1994**

- Must make sure there is a good basis for *sīla*, for the Dhamma
  building to be constructed.

- One cannot always be in meditation or *jhāna*. But when one is
  attending to other work like moving about, one should establish
  oneself in mindfulness as much as possible and it becomes the most
  wholesome condition to attain the *jhānas* when one sits down to it.

- One should establish three ways of conduct and should reflect every
  evening, before sleeping any activity that is regrettable, that which
  should be avoided the next day and so on. It should continue day after
  day. This practice should be put into effect without delay.

- Then one practices *Satipaṭṭhāna* to overcome the hindrances, and then
  attains the *jhānas*. Afterwards one turns away from that as well,
  that is when one enters the path.

- Know you have a theoretical grasp, but experience is important. You
  must experience it.

- Whenever there is a decision to be made and you do not know what to do
  then you should practice *Mettā* *bhāvanā* before you sleep and then
  think about the ultimate goal you want to achieve, and the decision
  you have to make and then you can fall asleep. In the morning you
  would get the answer.

- Normally, reasoning out is not correct. This way it is more reliable.

- **In a nice appealing fashion, someone may appear (maybe the wife in
  the past lives), you must know that this is Māra**[45]**.**

- But when you practice *Satipaṭṭhāna* it is quite safe. Don’t worry
  about anything. Every moment should be \[a\] pleasant abiding.

- You must be one with the *Mahāsaṅgha*, not considering an individual,
  then though we are separate in body, we will be one in mind.

*At a Previous Discussion*

- **You must go and stay in a jungle. If you are forced from inside, not
  otherwise. You must see a tremendous need to turn away from the
  *kāma-loka* \[world of sensual pleasure\].**

- **A lot of arahants were near the Himalayan region. This area is
  extremely difficult. This was the reason that many became yogis, to
  escape from the *kāma-loka*, because there is nothing pleasant in this
  outside world of five senses. So, they turn away inwardly.**

*After a decade of close association with Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera,
Shanaka finally entered the monastic life as Ven. Bambalapitiye
Gnanaloka Thero, with the full blessings of his parents (Siri
Vajiragnana Dharmayathanaya, 19 December 1999).*

## **Reflection 03: The Karmic Bonds That Shape a Path**

Shanaka was born in 1971, during the height of the youth insurrection in
Sri Lanka[46]. At the time, his parents were living in Anuradhapura,
where his father worked as an agricultural engineer. However, his
maternal grandfather—a doctor—insisted that Shanaka’s mother travel to
Colombo and stay with them until the baby was born. According to his
mother, this decision turned out to be a blessing in disguise.

Shanaka came into the world several weeks premature—not in a hospital,
but in the backseat of his grandfather’s car, near a Buddha statue, as
they were rushing to the hospital. His grandmother, overjoyed, is said
to have exclaimed:

“*Mage budu putha ipaduna*” (“My son, blessed as a Buddha, is
born.”)[47]

Though they may have hoped for a saintly child, what they got was a
whirlwind of energy. From his earliest years, Shanaka was rebellious and
intense. When his father’s work later took the family to Kandy, he was
enrolled at Trinity College, a prestigious private school known for its
discipline and refinement. But Shanaka resisted conformity at every
turn. He fought, broke rules, and challenged authority. Bruce Lee became
his hero. Canings at school were frequent, and at home, his mother
struggled to contain his fierce spirit.

Yet one thing stood out, even amidst the chaos: Shanaka’s instinctive
reverence for monks. As a quiet foreshadowing of his later path, at
around eight years old, in an act of childhood play, he and a friend
pretended to be monks, earnestly circumambulating a school tree they
imagined to be a sacred Bodhi tree. It lasted a couple of days. Then it
was back to fighting, back to trouble—the usual boyhood chaos.

His life took a dramatic shift when his father accepted a foreign
assignment in Lesotho, Southern Africa. There, Shanaka’s unchanneled
energy found a new outlet in sports, particularly athletics and cricket.
At the same time, a quiet curiosity about the nature of life began to
emerge. He would often walk alone along the Caledon River, contemplating
questions of time, space, and existence. Albert Einstein became his new
inspiration, and he began to dream of studying at Princeton University.

He was particularly drawn to the theory of relativity—how time slows
down near the speed of light, and how one might, in theory, travel
backward in time. Of course, nothing with mass could surpass the speed
of light, but to Shanaka’s mind, memory itself was already a form of
time travel. During one such walk, a question arose:

“What is the cause of any phenomenon?”

And then, just as instantly, came an insight:

“Anything that begins in time must also end.”

The realisation struck him deeply. He saw that true understanding would
not be found in beginnings and endings, but in the cessation of
time-bound experience. In that very moment, an image appeared in his
mind—a monk, calm, solitary, and detached. A quiet knowing followed:

“I must become like that.”

Everything became clear. This body was temporary. This life, fleeting.
Even his dream of studying at Princeton began to fade. The nature of
impermanence was becoming evident.

When he returned home, he informed his father that he wanted to ordain.
That single sentence sent shockwaves through the family.

From that day on, his once restless and fiery manner softened naturally.
He walked more intently, ate mindfully, spoke less. His mother, who had
a spiritual inclination, supported him wholeheartedly and became his
first student. His father, however, was deeply disappointed. To him, it
seemed as though his son was throwing away his future—his education, his
cricketing talents, and his worldly potential.

Their relationship grew strained. Shanaka knew he had caused pain, and
with time, he came to understand just how difficult it must have been
for his father to accept. But shortly after this turning point, he met
Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera—a meeting that would shift the trajectory of
his life entirely.

Had he not met his teacher, Shanaka might have rushed blindly into
renunciation, creating more conflict and widening the rift with his
father. But Ven. Ñāṇavimala guided him with wisdom and restraint,
offering a principle that left a lasting impression:

“Don’t break your *Sīla*[48] for anyone—not even for your parents. But
short of that, do everything you can to make them happy.”

During one of his retreats, Shanaka had a deep insight into the karmic
ties he shared with his father—why he had been born into that family,
why he was his father’s son.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala encouraged harmony, not defiance. He reminded Shanaka
that parents are not random strangers—they are beings bound to us
through past *kamma*, who have cared for us since birth. Following this
advice, Shanaka chose to honor his father’s wishes. He enrolled in
business management studies in Cyprus and later worked at an auditing
firm for two years.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala once told him:

“You don’t overcome *kamma* by changing the environment—you remain
equanimous and let it pass.”

These words shaped Shanaka’s approach to challenges. Rather than
reacting, he learned to stay centered—to uphold his precepts and let old
*kamma* unfold without adding new layers of conflict.

It would take nearly a decade for Shanaka’s father to give his blessing.
He never argued or pleaded. He simply followed his teacher’s guidance:
he meditated, studied the *Suttas*, and undertook silent retreats—both
in Sri Lanka and later in India—while continuing to support his parents’
happiness.

Eventually, his father began asking him when he planned to ordain.

In 1999, during what would be his final solitary retreat as a layman at
Athdalagala Forest Monastery in Kurunegala, days of meditation, *Sutta*
reading, and quiet reflection brought matters into focus. The
uncertainty about timing eased; the decision to seek ordination became
clear.

This time when he returned home, and informed his father that he wanted
to ordain. His father embraced him and said:

“Son, don’t look back.”

That blessing gave Shanaka strength beyond anything he could have
imagined. Years later, as Venerable Gnanaloka, during a particularly
grueling *cārikā* through the Knuckles mountain range, he became lost
for several days. He fell down a ravine, was stung by fever nettle,
covered in leeches, and had neither food nor water. Some later
speculated that he must have possessed supernatural powers to survive.
But in truth, what sustained him was something much simpler.

A quiet mantra arose in the depths of the forest:

“My father blessed me… My father blessed me.”

That blessing, that acceptance, became his source of strength.

Looking back nearly three decades later, Ven. Gnanaloka sees just how
true his teacher’s words were. Ven. Ñāṇavimala once told him:

“The only way to truly help your parents, your relatives, and all
beings… is to purify yourself. This will inspire others.”

He was right.

The seeds of Dhamma that Shanaka planted over the years bore
extraordinary fruit. His father—once his greatest critic—became his
greatest supporter, and in time, both parents were inspired to enter the
holy life. His father ordained as a monk and passed away as a bhikkhu in
2024. His mother, his very first student, ordained as a nun in early
2025. Their journey is a testament that the call to a spiritual life can
be answered at any age, bringing the family’s story full circle from
resistance to shared commitment in the Dhamma.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s guidance became the living bridge between the Buddha’s
words and Shanaka’s life. The Blessed One had declared that even
carrying one’s parents on each shoulder for a hundred years would not
repay the debt owed to them[49]. His teacher showed him the way to
honour that truth—not through grand gestures or conflict, but through
patient conduct, unbroken *sīla*, and the steady purification of the
heart. In following this counsel, Shanaka found that his ordination was
not a turning away from his parents, but the highest act of
gratitude—one that, in time, inspired them to walk the same path.

*Handwritten notes by Shanka Dharmakeerti, capturing teachings and
reflections from his time with Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera (1995).*

**- Chapter 04 -**

**“More Pleasure More Danger”**

**(Jan 1995 – Mar 1995)**

**28 January 1995**

- **To overcome these \[sensual\] thoughts, *asubha saññā* \[perception
  of unattractiveness\].**

- **How to spend the week.**

<!-- -->

- Read the verses in Pāli as if the Buddha is speaking to you, then the
  mind will be directed to that. Always the translations would not be
  adequate.[50]

- You must choose the *Suttas* that would help you to get on in the lay
  life. Read and practice loving kindness as
  \[<span class="mark">outlined\]</span> by Ñāṇamoli thera \[*The
  Practice of Loving-Kindness*\], and in *Vandana Vatpirit Potha* \[*The
  Mirror of the Dhamma* compiled by Ven. Narada and Ven. Kassapa\] there
  are 4 important things to be reflected on: *mettā, buddhānussati,
  impurities of the body, maranānussati.*

<!-- -->

- **<span class="mark">Concentrate on *sīla* and *bhāvanā*
  (*vipassanā*).</span>**

- **Try to elevate the state of mind - that is the greatest service.
  Because what follows depends on it.**

- ***Ambalaṭṭhikarāhulovāda Sutta* (MN61; advice to Rāhula at
  Ambalaṭṭhika)**

- **Don’t study with the intention of accumulating knowledge.**[51]

<!-- -->

- You can earn a living even by sweeping.

- Learn to be content with little.

- Sensual things are the most dangerous.

<!-- -->

- **More Pleasure More Danger**

<!-- -->

- **Like moths who fly into the flames. So be careful.**

<!-- -->

- Know that the Saṅgha is in decline.

- Because when living near towns, going to *pirivenas* \[monastic
  colleges that educate monks\], there are more sense impressions
  arising that arouse inner worldly inclinations, so they disrobe.

- So, one should retire to the forest, follow the instructions of the
  teacher.

- *Satipaṭṭhāna* should be practiced with Right View (*sammā-diṭṭhi)*.

- *Jhāna* \[meditative absorption\] and then *Satipaṭṭhāna*
  \[foundations of mindfulness\]

- For *jhāna* to arise, there needs to be a foundation of *sīla* \[moral
  conduct\].

> ***Regarding sensual desire:***

- **When elephants are in rut or other animals are, \[as well as\]
  lovers—they are fully absorbed and intoxicated by that, no sense is
  seen.**

- **People work just to earn money, to support their body. All the
  activities are centered on that. No time for the practice.**

<!-- -->

- ***When on retreat* food and sleep or negligence are the hindrances
  one must watch, not too much effort either, but one should balance.**

<!-- -->

- **First you should raise the mind or consciousness to higher deva
  worlds. Then after that you direct the mind to the Dhamma.**[52]

<!-- -->

- **<span class="mark">Saṅkhār</span>a brings about birth.**

**31 January 1995**

- *Yoniso-manasikāra* \[Wise Contemplation\]—is these three ways of
  conduct.

- *Satipaṭṭhāna* should be practiced with the right understanding.

- The 5 faculties (*indriya*), *saddhā, viriya*, *sati*, *samādhi* (4
  *Jhānas*) and *paññā.*[53] <span class="mark"></span>

- No hate, no greed should arise.

- If hate arises, you should control it with *mettā*.

- When greed arises, practice *asubha*, when delusion arises, read the
  *Suttas*.

- Refer to the *Suttas* *Majjhima* 117, 25, 50—*Mahācattārīsaka Sutta*
  (MN117; the great forty); *Nivāpa Sutta* (MN25; the bait);
  *Māratajjanīya Sutta* (MN50; the rebuke to Māra).

- It is good to be with the teacher in the jungle.

- It is very difficult to practice otherwise with unnecessary
  impressions.

- Also read *Khandha Vagga* (SN22; connected discourses on the
  aggregates) and *Saḷāyatana Vagga* (SN35; connected discourses on the
  six sense bases).

<!-- -->

- **This life is full of suffering, old age, sickness and the trouble of
  always searching for food. Even bhikkhus are not free from that, all
  others are working day and night only for this, and weakness of the
  body due to sickness, the food you put in is only for the purpose of
  supporting the body, constituted of the 32 parts of impurity, and if
  you vomit it back, it is so repulsive. When it goes from the throat it
  is all foul, especially if you keep it for some days. When you reduce
  the craving or attraction to these things (though there \[was\]
  initial pleasure, there \[was\] more suffering connected to it) and
  cultivate the happiness in the absence of deluded pleasure, and when
  you turn away from the cause of this suffering
  (*paṭicca-samuppāda*)—no illusion, no craving, no clinging, birth and
  old age, death etc. The causes are *lobha* (greed)*, dosa* (hate) *and
  moha* (delusion).**

<!-- -->

- When you are doing *caṅkamana* first you must arouse a sense of
  urgency.

- Thinking of this endless round of rebirth. Each step towards death.
  Then you should exclude all other thoughts and must stick to the
  present.

- With thoughts of *mettā*, supporting each other, harmony in mind to
  achieve this one goal, deliverance from all suffering, *Nibbāna*.

- When practicing *mettā*-*bhāvanā*, not just wishing long life and
  happiness, but wishing the highest happiness to end this suffering
  \[for all beings\].

- You must stay with the teacher in the jungle for the practice to
  establish firmly, so that you can face any situation in time to come.

- When you give into food and other things, craving increases and
  clinging, birth, old age and death.

- *Mettā-bhāvanā* and the impermanence of feelings, perceptions and
  consciousness.

- *Khandha Vagga* (SN22; Connected Discourses on the Aggregates)

- Read the *Dhammapada* verses, recite them, and learn by heart in Pāli
  certain verses that strike you.

- *Ambalaṭṭhikarāhulovāda Sutta* (MN61; advice to Rāhula at
  Ambalaṭṭhika)

- Mustn’t have inner conflict. Your father would like you to have a
  sound education. You can support each other.

- You must stick to a few *Suttas* and put them into practice.

**3 February 1995**

*Morning*

- *Majjhima Sutta* 125—*Dantabhūmi Sutta* (MN125; the grade of the
  tamed) , *Khandhasaṃyutta* (SN22; Connected Discourses on the
  Aggregates)

- You must purify yourself. In other words, you become selfish (retire)
  to solitude to purify oneself in order to overcome the ‘I’ conceit and
  thereafter serve in a selfless way.

- A teacher generally has an idea of all the *Suttas*. So, he prescribes
  to the pupil, according to where the pupil is and where he has to get
  \[to\] and it is a step-by-step process.

- If the pupil is a layman and wants to meditate then he should start
  with *Ambalaṭṭhikarāhulovāda Sutta* (MN61; advice to Rāhula at
  Ambalaṭṭhika), the three ways of conduct.

- If he is just a layman, *Sigālovāda Sutta* (DN31; advice to Sigālaka),
  etc.

- You must always start with the three protections[54] and with arousing
  of spiritual urgency, when beginning on *caṅkamana*, or any kind of
  *bhāvanā* or spiritual endeavor.

- You must always try to curb and annihilate *lobha* (greed)*, dosa*
  (hate) *and moha* (delusion) by a suitable *kammaṭṭhāna*.

- One cannot rely on anything that is from outside that is
  happiness—only the inner peace.

- *Caṅkamana* is sticking to the present without giving into thoughts
  from outside, this should be done with understanding and reflection of
  the situation of the body, how all activity is centered around
  supporting the impure body that is connected with all kinds of
  suffering—old age, sickness, and all kinds of illusive happiness.

<!-- -->

- **Certain things are practiced for the benefit of future generations
  e.g. certain Vinaya rules, Venerable Mahākassapa was staying in
  solitude.**

<!-- -->

- The three ways of conduct should be done unremittingly and
  immaculately.

- **May you get all the protection in *saṁsāra*.**

*Afternoon*

- *Majjhima Sutta* 53—Sekha Sutta (MN53; the disciple in higher
  training)

- It is for the monks, when you become a monk, it is useful, especially
  regarding the \[spiritual\] faculties \[*Indriyas*\].

- *Satipaṭṭhāna* leads to *Jhānas.*

- Do this *caṅkamana* practice when you feel sleepy and control this
  hindrance.

<!-- -->

- **All my blessings are there for your practice.**

<!-- -->

- When you go on *caṅkamana*, think of this body made up of defiled
  actions in the past, so impure and repulsive[55].

- Always look at *Khandha Vagga* (SN22) and *Saḷāyatana Vagga* (SN35).
  Look at it as it is.

<!-- -->

- **Always stick to the present, don’t expect anything from the future
  nor regret or go back to the past. What you do in the present makes
  the future.**

<!-- -->

- Read the *Dhammapada* verses. Memorise some.

**4 February 1995**

- When you are in retreat solitude try to control the 5
  <span class="mark">*indriyas*</span> \[five spiritual faculties\] and
  practice *caṅkamana* (*sati-sampajañña*).

<!-- -->

- ***Khandhasaṁyutta* (SN22; Connected Discourses on the Aggregates)**

- ***Saḷāyatanasaṁyutta* (SN35; The Connected Discourses on the Six
  Sense Bases)**

- **You may speak about the Dhamma when asked.**

<!-- -->

- *Rāhulovāda Sutta* (MN61), In the evening *mettā* *bhāvanā* and
  *anicca*[56]. When *caṅkamana* *“sabbe saṅkhārā aniccā”,* verses in
  *Dhammapada* (Dhp277—279).

- *Sacittapariyodapanaṁ, etaṁ buddhāna sasanaṁ* (Dhp183).

- You may give support to each other, the family, not only this material
  support, but also this spiritual support.

- See everything as impermanent, and suffering; and not yours.

- *Atta Vagga* (last verse) in the *Dhammapada* (Dhp 166) and Bubble &
  Mirage <span class="mark">(Dhp 170)</span>

**16 February 1995**

- *Bahussuta* \[knowledgeable\] is important.[57]

- After practicing *bhāvanā* like in Rishikesh \[India\]. More ability
  to concentrate. Therefore, successful in worldly activity. But what
  you build up in the sensual world also comes down very fast.[58]
  *“sabbe <span class="mark">saṅkhār</span>ā aniccā”* \[all conditioned
  things are impermanent\]

<!-- -->

- As a layman you observe verbal and physical actions. As a monk higher
  *sīla* is first restrained by the *Pātimokkha* \[monastic discipline\]
  and *indriya saṁvara sīla* \[sense restraint\] and then thoughts,
  *ariya sīla* \[noble morality\].

- You should always reflect on the impurities of the body, and the
  impermanence of food. Especially if you vomit out, or having some food
  still in your stomach undigested, and so on. And also, when you are
  taking food, the trouble involved in meeting lay people and just to
  support this body, with people working day and night for a body which
  is rushing to old age, sickness and death. But when you know that, one
  is able to remove the cause of suffering or the arising of this
  body**—**which is craving, by clear seeing or understanding, so that
  ultimately the craving will diminish and extinguish. The arising of
  new suffering will extinguish.

- One should retire with the teacher into the forest for *jhāna* to take
  place. Such an environment with less sense impressions to work with is
  necessary.

- During the day you should work in a *sīlavanta* \[virtuous\] way
  because this affects the sleep and meditation at the end of the day.

- Writing letters and so on by monks, it is not that you should forget
  your relatives. It is always good to \[reduce\] bonds and ties with
  all people, even in a lay life[59].

- Even the brahma world is suffering. But one may re-arise even in the
  hell realms.

- *Mahācattārīsaka Sutta* (MN117; the great forty)

- Sleep—you should try to sleep generally 5 to 6 hours. It is good to
  reduce it.

<!-- -->

- **You should allocate a specific time of the day for this. And at
  other times you must not allow sleepiness.**

- **You must share the Dhamma with your relatives, not by going behind
  them and talking but by your own actions.**

- **This solid knowledge should arise. That is insight into Dhamma.**

<!-- -->

- Today more money is needed and people’s needs are more.

- When you are in retreat, you should have Dhamma thoughts.

- You mustn’t eat very little food or sleep too little; you may fall
  sick. But you should try to do it with little effort. You have to
  maintain this body and keep it in good shape.

- *Jhāna*; then *vipassanā*.

- When you have wishes, and when they are fulfilled, the craving
  increases more, wish after wish will follow.

- Do not wait with certain ideas for a long time, even arahants discuss
  from time to time[60].

**2 March 1995**

- **Supply the causes for the things that you wish to arise.**

- **Make sure the foundation is firmly established.**

- **Read *Majjhima* 125, 117, 107, 118—*Dantabhūmi Sutta* (MN125; the
  grade of the tamed)*, Mahācattārīsaka Sutta* (MN117; the great forty),
  *Gaṇakamoggallāna Sutta* (MN 107; to Gaṇaka Moggallāna), *Ānāpānasati
  Sutta* (MN118; Mindfulness of Breathing)**

- ***S<span class="mark">aḷāy</span>atana Vagga* (SN35; The Connected
  Discourses on the Six Sense Bases), *Khandha Vagga* (SN22; Connected
  Discourses on the Aggregates), *Bojjhanga Saṁyutta* (SN46)**[61] **all
  in *Saṁyutta Nikāya.***

- **Read *Word of the Buddha* \[by Venerable Nyanatiloka Mahāthera\] for
  *sammā diṭṭhi*.**

- **When you are here now, just don’t think to abide by *jhāna*, but try
  to overcome the five hindrances, then the mind will settle easily.**

- **Now you should concentrate to attain *jhāna*.**

- **You must do things slowly and thoroughly.**

- **In time to come when practicing *mettā bhāvanā*, pleasant feelings
  may arise. Look at it with equanimity, they are just feelings, all
  feelings are impermanent, suffering, not mine, and conditionally
  arisen.**

- **Equanimity is most important - not emotional feeling**[62]**.**

- **Don’t worry, it will come.**

<!-- -->

- **Regarding *bojjhaṅga* \[factors of enlightenment\] - this is towards
  true enlightenment. Not the way of the
  <span class="mark">\[Hindu\]</span> yogi, \[which is\] *micchā diṭṭhi*
  \[wrong view\] way.**

<!-- -->

- ***Bojjhaṅga* takes place when you are doing *bhāvanā*.**

- **Don’t try to have theoretical explanations, but instead
  experience.**

- **Don’t stick to an attainment, go on.**

- **You must change the *kammaṭṭhāna* in order to tackle each
  defilement.**

- **When going on *caṅkamana*, control the thoughts, so that it sticks
  to one place. At that time *lobha* (greed)*, dosa* (hate) *and moha*
  (delusion) are reduced <span class="mark">and sense of ‘I’ is going
  away towards *Nibbāna*. </span>**

- **Sometimes one is enthusiastic, sometimes one’s strength is going
  down. This will be the situation throughout life. So don’t worry— as
  long as the conditions are good, you may work as hard as possible, but
  not overdoing, always the middle way.**

**6 March 1995**

- Don’t rely on anybody or any person or anything that is external. They
  can separate at any moment and are all unreliable. But depend on your
  own mind that is developed and the All Enlightened One as your guide.

- Observing the physical and verbal actions you do as a layman is known
  as sīla; when a monk, you go further to observe thoughts, and to bring
  them into control (mental actions), that is Ariya *sīla*.

- The physical and verbal actions also derive from the mental state
  (first verse in *Dhammapada—* Dhp 1).

- First look after your own welfare**—**it is not selfish. Bringing
  yourself to selflessness then you can serve others in the highest way
  without selfish motives.

- First Right View (*sammā-diṭṭhi)* \[theoretical foundation\][63], then
  the five hindrances[64] should be checked and overcome; then
  *Satipaṭṭhāna*.

- *Suttas* 117, 125, 106 \[of *Majjhima Nikāya*\]—*Mahācattārīsaka
  Sutta* (MN117; the great forty); *Dantabhūmi Sutta* (MN125; the grade
  of the tamed); *Āneñjasappāya Sutta* (MN106; the way to the
  imperturbable).

- Reading all the *Suttas*, memorising them, there is no point.

- Practice and experience. This is important.

- When we see the 32 parts of the body, and not letting any worldly
  thought arise, one will know that one is directing towards overcoming
  suffering or *Nibbāna*.

**8 March 1995**

- All my blessings are there for all your endeavors.

- Never let the mind be disturbed by a certain situation or
  circumstance.

**9 March 1995**

- You must be firmly established in the Dhamma as a bhikkhu when you go
  out, where you will face all sorts of situations, must think of the
  *Sutta* regarding the Simile of the Saw—*Kakacūpama Sutta* (MN 21) and
  reflecting that our *kamma* is behind us.

- First *sīla* and then *jhāna* and then entering the path**—**
  *sotāpanna, sakadāgāmī, anāgāmī, arahant.*

- Until arahantship there is this “I” conceit in a very subtle way.

- All lay people are like children, they need a father figure, but when
  the children grow up, they do not need one anymore. So don’t think of
  a particular person outside. Rely always on the All Enlightened One
  and the Dhamma that you have already mastered as your guide. Whenever
  effort is made and certain bliss is connected to that, take that as a
  strength to go on and develop.

- Do not teach the Dhamma for any worldly gain and so on.

<!-- -->

- **Go on with compassion for beings with less understanding.**

<!-- -->

- First give support to people who are close to you. But don’t try to
  convert or go behind \[them\], but with your own example**—**and if
  they are interested, you may help them accordingly. You may send to
  your friend in Cyprus the *Kālāma Sutta* (AN3.65; to the Kālāmas). If
  \[they\] are more interested, a list of BPS \[Buddhist Publication
  Society\] books may be provided, and maybe pointing to particular
  books. Not \[to\] suggest this is Buddhism.

- Don’t have any ties. Try to reduce ties as much as possible.

- You must understand this situation. We have not always acted in a
  *kusala* way in the past. Never \[to be\] displeased.[65]

- When unwholesome thoughts arise, don’t react with aversion or hate or
  fear, if reacting that way, it means one is directing the mind in that
  direction, therefore it increases.

- Don’t stick to an attainment or turn back from \[putting\] effort.[66]

- *Suttas* to refer—*Anamatag<span class="mark">ga S</span>aṁyutta*
  (SN15; connected discourses on without discoverable beginning);
  *Mahācattārīsa<span class="mark">ka</span> Sutta* (MN117; the great
  forty); *Vitakkasaṇṭhāna Sutta* (MN20; the removal of distracting
  thoughts) deal with thoughts.

- Doctors are very busy, more diseases, so more activity to cope up
  with, and they are generally highly considered by the society for
  everybody is keen about good health. But in the Dhamma it is all
  mentioned: the root cause for sickness—that is immorality[67].

- It is difficult to maintain *jhāna*, so you should do the second best
  by reading the *Suttas* etc.

<!-- -->

- Live in the present, every pleasant present moment will bring
  happiness in the future, so never let the mind be displeased.

- Look at everything as a *bubble and mirage* (Dhp 170). There is no
  meaning in all this phenomenon.

- There is no ‘I’, there is no ‘me’.

- Daily make sure all activities are done in such a way where there
  hasn’t been any harm done to anybody. Go to bed early, before sleeping
  practice *mettā* *bhāvanā*. Do the activity with full concentration,
  slowly. And on *poya* days you may retire in a suitable place and
  wait. All depends on your practice.

- All external support may come to an end finally. It is our *kamma*
  behind us, we have not always acted in a wholesome way.

- All relatives and family have a lot of *saddhā* due to past lives.

<!-- -->

- **You may give support to your family, relatives, and all others, not
  by trying to convert them, but by your example. It is the best way.**

<!-- -->

- You practice in solitude and go \[in\]to the world, in order to fully
  establish in the practice of *Ambalaṭṭhika Rāhulovāda Sutta* (MN61;
  advice to Rāhula at Ambalaṭṭhika) in the lay life, so that a sound
  basis and a foundation is established.

<!-- -->

- **Based on the way we look from inside; the world is formed from the
  outside.**

<!-- -->

- Anyway, we cannot change things outside.

- When going on *caṅkamana*, see the suffering we have to go through to
  maintain this meaningless process, coming in contact with disturbing
  sense impressions and the food when we are eating, the danger in it
  for it is a thing that magnifies the cause for this process that is
  rushing toward old age, sickness, death, this craving. But one should
  be happy that we know the root cause for this suffering, it is this
  craving.

- Building houses and so forth for the body etc.

<!-- -->

- When disturbing thoughts come, *Satipaṭṭhāna* practice will be good.

*Ven. Ñāṇavīra, Ven. Ñāṇamoli, and Ven. Ñāṇavimala at the Island
Hermitage, Polgasduwa — young Western monks who, having seen the horrors
of the Second World War, turned away from worldly life to follow the
ancient path of the Buddha (1955).*

**Reflection 04: Seeing Through the Snare of Sensuality**

The Buddha taught the spiritual seeker to look beyond appearances—to see
the true nature of experience as it is. Beings, he said, are trapped in
an illusion they don’t recognize and don’t know how to break free from.
What the world takes as beautiful, has an inherent foul nature; what it
calls lasting is impermanent; what it delights in as pleasure is bound
up with suffering. Not understanding this duality, one is perpetually in
this suffering loop, never free from it.

For Möller (later Ven. Ñāṇavimala), these words were no longer distant
teachings. They were the shape his own life had taken. The seemingly
secure world he had built in Sri Lanka collapsed overnight, and he was
stripped of all control. From that darkness, the Buddha’s words felt
like a breath of fresh air—a light piercing the fog of illusion. He
began to see for himself the danger the Buddha had warned of: that to
mistake the unstable for the stable is to invite suffering again and
again.

To be free of that danger requires dismantling the web of false
conditioning woven by craving and ignorance. He undertook this with
endurance and clarity—an unrelenting commitment to simplicity,
renunciation, and truth—a striving that would come to define his life as
a monk.

For decades, Ven. Ñāṇavimala wandered barefoot across Sri Lanka,
carrying nothing but his bowl, robes, shoulder bag and other necessary
requisites.[68] He accepted only what was absolutely necessary for
survival<span class="mark">—</span>and even that, sparingly. He once
told Shanaka, “You rest only in hospitals,” a stark reflection of his
own uncompromising resolve. It wasn’t an exaggeration.

Years later, when Ven. Gnanaloka himself began undertaking *cārikā*, he
met doctors who had treated Ven. Ñāṇavimala during bouts of severe
illness. They remembered him staying only until he could stand again;
then, without announcement, he would leave—on foot, alone, with no
destination beyond the next forest or village. For him, rest was not a
right but a concession to necessity, and even then, only briefly
granted.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala would often say that “life, even the life of a bhikkhu
is full of *dukkha*: sickness, old age, and the continuous effort just
to find sustenance.” The body, he reflected, is merely a fragile sack of
thirty-two foul parts. “If one vomited what was consumed, one would
recoil in disgust. Even before it enters the stomach, it is offensive.
Let it sit a day or two, and it rots. Yet the world clings to this
form—this perishable thing—mistaking it for pleasure.”

The flower that delights the eye is already wilting; the skin that seems
radiant today will soon wrinkle and fade. Even delight itself grows
weary with time. What the world praises as beauty is only impermanence
briefly disguised.

Seeing this, Ven. Ñāṇavimala understood sensuality not as something to
chase, but as delusion wearing the mask of sweetness. Reflecting on the
snare of romantic attachment, perhaps the most potent expression of
sensual desire he said:

“Whatever momentary happiness is there, it is a temporary intoxication.
But far more suffering is connected with it.”

He spoke simply, but the truth struck deep.

Desire, aversion, and delusion—these are the fires that keep us bound to
discontent, always seeking, never still. He came to see that freedom lay
not in belief, but in understanding the chain of arising and ceasing—how
every flame of craving depends on its fuel, and when the fuel is gone,
the fire ends.

This clarity had not come overnight. Long before he donned the robe,
even as a young layman in Germany, he had glimpses of it.

Once, there was a beautiful young woman from his hometown who was
devoted to him. One day, he saw her speaking warmly with another man and
in that instant, jealousy struck him like a sword. The pain was sharp,
but the insight that followed was sharper still. The trap was clear—how
attachment binds the heart, how longing turns quickly to anguish.

He ended the relationship quietly, moved by a painful recognition that
such attachment would only lead to suffering. That moment was an early
glimpse of the turning away that would one day deepen into renunciation.

For Shanaka, this inner steadiness didn’t come easily. He would later
describe his school years as a “terrible time”—a period of raging
hormones and intense temptation.

Growing up in Lesotho among expatriate families, he was surrounded by
Western ideals. Some classmates were sexually unreserved; others
experimented with drugs. While drugs held little interest for him, the
allure of Friday night discos—the dancing, the laughter, the
intoxicating company of girls—was a constant pull.

Though he longed to join them, he was too shy to ask his father for
money. That hesitation—mingled with a sense of shame—kept him away from
habits that might have veered him off course.

Even then, traces of a deeper potential flickered beneath his outward
rough character. At sixteen, he had a quiet intuition that life was
about to change—that by eighteen, something decisive would turn. And it
did. But the first signs came not in temples or scriptures, but in the
ordinary tests of youth.

There was a British girl he had a crush on. The interest was mutual; she
even sent a friend to pass a message of affection. Yet when his own
friend urged him to respond, his reply was unusually philosophical for a
teenager: “I don’t want to play with the world until I know about it.”

That restraint was from an unspoken sense that the world operated
according to a certain order, and he didn’t want to mess with it until
he understood its rules. This moral instinct—a quiet wish to act rightly
without knowing why—was a kind of *sīla*, he believes, that created a
respect from even those girls.

That same instinct followed him into early adulthood. But conscience,
when untested, is only theory.

Having just turned eighteen, before his first real spiritual experiences
or any encounter with Ven. Ñāṇavimala, he found himself facing a test of
another kind. While playing club cricket, he associated with older
teammates who often went to the pub after matches for beers. One day,
they started chanting:

“Shanaka is going to drink today!”… “Shanaka is going to drink!”

The pressure was overwhelming. The glass was placed in front of him, and
for a moment, his mind wavered. Perhaps just a sip, a thought whispered.
It’s only once… why not?

And then—through the noise—came a clear voice. One of the players’ wives
turned toward him and said:

“Shanaka, stick to your principles.”

Her words cut through the confusion. In that instant, his thoughts
cleared, and conviction returned. His hand stilled beside the glass.

He said nothing, but the act itself stayed with him. It showed him how
easily one could slip, and how quietly one could stand firm.

Even then, Shanaka’s sense of *sīla* was more a matter of conscience
than insight—a kind of moral pride, a wish to show he had the willpower
to resist what others yielded to, mixed with a sense of shyness.  
  
However, something within him was quietly preparing for change, though
he could never have imagined it unfolding how it did.

What began shortly after as a spontaneous spiritual insight was soon
forged into true discipline under the steady influence of Ven.
Ñāṇavimala.

As the years passed, that quiet guidance grew firmer. Nearing
twenty-five, Ven. Ñāṇavimala perhaps sensed that Shanaka stood at a
threshold—the age when the world begins to pull one outward, offering
paths that promise comfort, but bind the heart more tightly. He would
gently remind him to be firm in his direction, knowing that the world
would soon expect other commitments.

Around this time, Shanaka took a job at an auditing firm, a decision
that met with his teacher’s quiet disapproval. He remarked to Shanaka,
almost in passing:

“I tell you to go this way,” he gestured one direction, “but then you go
that way,” turning his hand the other way.

It was said in such passing, the words seeming to drift away as he
turned to another topic. Yet, for Shanaka, they did not drift at all.
The words landed with an immense weight. For Shanaka this passing remark
carried the force of a reprimand. It was the only time Ven. Gnanaloka
recalls he felt him express visible disapproval at him.

During this time, some assumed Shanaka had let go of the idea of
ordaining. They speculated that he would settle down and start a family.
But Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s influence lingered in him like a quiet echo. Back
in the lay world, surrounded by temptation, Shanaka began to understand
the true scope of his teacher’s path. It was one thing to admire
simplicity from afar; another to live amidst desire and remain unmoved.

Following his teacher’s guidance he resolved to practice sense
restraint, particularly by averting his gaze from the powerful allure of
feminine beauty—not out of disrespect, but as an act of inner
discipline. He meditated during lunch breaks, even in the office
restroom, and kept his interactions with women brief and professional.
It was an immense effort to steady a mind conditioned by countless lives
of desire.

Yet this outward composure often hid a fiercer inner reality. The fire
of lust still burned greatly. This became painfully clear at twenty-six,
during a pilgrimage to the sacred Ruwanwelisaya *stūpa* in Sri Lanka.

Midway through the journey, his mind was suddenly overwhelmed by a storm
of lustful thoughts—as if a force from outside himself had descended
upon him. The assault was so intense that he was forced to turn back. He
never reached the holy site that day.

What he didn't yet know: the intensity itself was a sign. When
breakthrough nears, the storm becomes fiercest.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s uncompromising counsel echoed in him:

“You must see a tremendous need to turn away from the *kāma-loka* — the
world of sensual pleasure.”

Yet the mind does not submit to willpower alone. It must be met
wisely—its habits understood, not fought against.

For this, Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s advice was: “When unwholesome thoughts
arise, counter them with *asubha saññā*”:

This practice is about seeing the truth of the body beyond the
superficial appearance that the world values so highly. It means
investigating its inherent foul nature by seeing it as the Buddha
taught—a fragile assembly of internal organs, tissues, and fluids,
thinly veiled by a layer of skin; an assembly subject to decay and
unworthy of attachment. This reflection reveals how the mind falsely
takes the body as its own—an attachment born of deception, which
perpetuates seeking delight in food and the human form. Seeing this, the
grip of identification with the body weakens, the fever of lust cools,
and a wise, liberating dispassion arises—finding its worth in wisdom,
not in fleeting appearances.

Then Ven. Ñāṇavimala added:

“If that fails, read the Dhammapada[69]. Trace the root. See where the
attachment lies. Know that it stems from ignorance—from clinging to what
is not truly yours.”

He urged the careful watching of the mind—to learn to recognize the root
before it overpowers. Ven. Ñāṇavimala would say:

“Whenever you delight in pleasant feeling, know that delusion is close
by.”

Ven. Ñāṇavimala would tell Shanaka again and again—throughout the day to
bring one’s mindfulness to reflect on one’s conduct. He directed him to
the *Ambalaṭṭhika Rāhulovāda Sutta* (MN61; Advice to Rāhula at
Ambalaṭṭhika)—the Buddha’s counsel to his own son. This practice is to
hold a mirror to body, speech, and mind—look before you act, watch while
acting, and review afterward. If unwholesome actions have occurred,
honestly recognize it and resolve to let it go.

Ven. Gnanaloka remembered meeting a rishi in India who carried the
essence of this very principle: each day, he would write down every
unwholesome thought that had arisen in him on the wall of his cave.

Over time, the writings grew fewer.

Then eventually, they became zero.

“Purified mind brings purified action,” Ven. Ñāṇavimala used to say. The
mind’s activities, when recognised honestly without judgement, begin
purifying themselves.

For his first solitary retreat, Shanaka chose the secluded Kalugala
Forest Monastery, a sanctuary nestled in the hills of Sri Lanka’s
Kalutara District near the dense Sinh<span class="mark">arāja</span>
rainforest. It was the ideal setting to put his teacher’s guidance into
practice. Before departing, Ven. Ñāṇavimala gave him one essential
instruction for his protection:

“Be well-grounded in the Four Protective Meditations before heading to
the jungle.”

They were:

- *Buddhānussati* \[Recollection on Buddha’s
  qualities\]<span class="mark">—</span>to uplift and steady the mind,

- *Mettā* \[Loving-kindness\]<span class="mark">—</span>to guard against
  ill will,

- *Asubhasaññā* \[Perception of foulness\]<span class="mark">—</span>to
  weaken sensual craving,

- *Maraṇānussati* \[Recollection of death\]<span class="mark">—</span>to
  cultivate urgency and clarity.

These were not rituals to be recited. They were armor. A toolkit for the
mind in solitude. And he was right. Almost immediately upon entering the
forest, lustful thoughts rose unexpectedly.

Shanaka came to see sense restraint not as submission, but as a quiet
freedom—a peace of mind untouched by the pull of the senses.

Yet even as this understanding deepened, Shanaka found himself torn
between lay life and the monastic path. He looked to his teacher for an
answer. Once, he shared a simile that perfectly encapsulated his
approach:

“Like a sailor when crossing a river, slowly, not losing the other
shore, direct the boat slowly, gradually cutting the stream. Similarly,
you should direct your life.”

For Shanaka, with practice and his teacher’s guidance he gradually
became confident in how to skillfully navigate his life, adjusting
according to the conditions and choosing the right tools at the right
moment to meet and overcome the current of sense desires.

His teacher taught this art not with many words, but with his very life.
In that silent, steadfast example, the answer shone brighter than any
spoken word. And in its light, his student found the courage to follow.

*Handwritten notes by Shanka Dharmakeerti, capturing teachings and
reflections from his time with Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera (1995).*

**- Chapter 05 -**

**“Always Be With Thoughts Of Mettā”**

**(April 1995 – July 1995)**

**14 April 1995**

> \- <span class="mark">Sutta No. 107—</span>*Gaṇakamoggallāna Sutta*
> (MN107; to Gaṇaka Moggallāna)—where, when doing other work one must
> also practice. Not just to always stay in seated meditation.
>
> \- When not well, keep the mind calm, practise *Buddhānusati* and make
> the mind happy. Calm the mind with a meditation object.
>
> \- Daily reflect on the three ways of conduct and, before sleeping,
> practice *mettā* *bhāvanā*. That is *kamma* which will give *vipāka*
> (change the situation).
>
> **Daily reflect on the 5 contemplations** (Maharagama Vat Pirith)[70]
>
> \- First <span class="mark">Sīl</span>a (three ways of conduct), Right
> View (Word of the Buddha), *Satipaṭṭhāna* (Overcoming the hindrances),
> *Jhāna*, *vipassanā*
>
> \- When going on *caṅkamana* \[walking meditation\], if a worldly
> thought arises, consider it as “it is not mine”.
>
> \- This; three ways of conduct, is it difficult?[71]

**15 April 199<span class="mark">5</span>**

**-** Know the way you should try to attain *Jhāna* (the way is
described in *Suttas*; *Gaṇakamoggallāna Sutta* (MN107; to Gaṇaka
Moggallāna) and *Dantabhūmi Sutta* (MN125; the grade of the tamed) of
*Majjhima Nikaya*.

> **-** When meditating, certain pleasant, unpleasant or sensual
> thoughts connected to the past may arise. What is past is not
> important. The present is important.
>
> **-** You can notice how moment to moment objects are always changing.
>
> \- The present moment or situation due to the past way of conduct is
> something we cannot brush aside, but we may act in such a manner that
> no greed, hate or delusion may arise and that is *kamma*.
>
> \- In the past for many births, we have depended on feelings for our
> happiness.
>
> \- Death is nothing but breaking of this present body which never
> belonged to us and the mind getting a new base to get on.
>
> \- The art of living in the present; it takes time to get established
> in that; not by days or weeks, but years.

**17 April 1995**

> \- Control of talking and thinking, *Suttas*; *Kāyagatāsati Sutta*
> (MN119; mindfulness of the body), *Mahāsuññata Sutta* (MN122; the
> greater discourse on voidness) and *Cūḷasuññata Sutta* (MN121; the
> shorter discourse on voidness) of \[*Majjhima Nikāya*\].
>
> \- Talk only on Dhamma, otherwise be quiet.
>
> \- Thinking and reasoning should stop a bit.
>
> \- No point knowing, preaching and so forth many *Suttas*, if one is
> not meditating, then one is not *Dhammavihāri* \[*Paṭhamadhammavihārī
> Sutta* (AN 5.73; one who dwells in the Dhamma)\].
>
> \- It is good to know many *Suttas* thoroughly and meanwhile meditate.
>
> \- There is no point looking for external support. The whole practice
> is dwindling away but make sure what is inside; the state of mind is
> kept undisturbed and pure.
>
> \- When you are old you have to depend on others, more bondages.
> Therefore, more suffering.
>
> \- In the library, you may find the *Suttas* in Pāli and an English
> translation. You may read both.

**20 April 1995**

> \- Whenever there are inclinations (in the sensual world) don’t try to
> fight them, it is impossible, but divert the mind to thoughts of
> Dhamma or recite certain verses.
>
> \- Watch the mind and keep it purified.
>
> \- Anyone who delights in feelings, delights in suffering.
>
> \- In the beginning read certain *Suttas*, but as you practice and go
> on you may read more accordingly. Don’t just go to reading more
> *Suttas*, practice is important.
>
> \- Generally, a teacher should be one who has entered the path.

**13 May 1995**

> \- Start with Right View (*sammā-diṭṭhi)* \[by\] read\[ing\] the Word
> of the Buddha \[by Venerable Ñāṇatiloka Mahāthera\] etc.
>
> \- Tradition is so dangerous. That’s why you should stick to the
> *Suttas*[72].
>
> \- When going on caṅkama even as a Bhikkhu, you have to go on
> *piṇḍapāta*. All the difficulty. When no illusion.

**14 May 1995**

> \- Read *Suttas*; *Sallekha Sutta* (MN8; effacement)*, Sammādiṭṭhi
> Sutta* (MN9; right view)*, Mahācattārīsakasutta* (MN117; the great
> forty).
>
> \- When you see *Paṭiccasamuppāda* you see Dhamma.
>
> \- *Ambalaṭṭhika Rāhulovāda Sutta* (MN61; advice to Rāhula at
> <span class="mark">Ambalaṭṭhika</span>)
>
> \- Read *Khandha Vagga* (SN22; Connected Discourses on the Aggregates)
> and *<span class="mark">Saḷāya</span>tana Vagga* (SN35; The Connected
> Discourses on the Six Sense Bases).
>
> \- *<span class="mark">Saḷāya</span>tana Vagga* (SN 35) is an
> expansion of *Khandha Vagga* (SN 22) through the six sense doors. The
> *Suttas* and so on are similar.

- **You may talk on the Dhamma to whoever shows interest, but you must
  do this with your example. You may do with means that is suitable to
  them tactfully.**

> **- Whether someone has entered the path and how far he has progressed
> one cannot say. Only an Arahant knows for certain when he comes to
> that (that is his arahantship). This sort of questioning is
> meaningless.**

**- You must look after your welfare first. This is done by
*Satipaṭṭhāna*.**

- **Our teacher is the All Enlightened One**

*- Aṅguttara Nikāya* 6.85—*Sītibhāva Sutta* (AN6.85; coolness)

> \- You must deal with the different *nīvaraṇās* \[hindrances\] with
> suitable objects of meditation.
>
> \- Reading of new *Suttas* should be done with practice.
>
> \- Working toward a certain worldly position etc. they are all to do
> with feelings. But all that ultimately comes to an end by old age and
> death.
>
> \- Always health is taken over by sickness, youth by old age and life
> by death.
>
> \- Sickness may come suddenly; we do not know what *kamma* we have
> done. The sickness of the body cannot be overcome but the sickness of
> the mind can be. It is very important to purify the mind from *lobha*
> (greed), *dosa* (hate), *moha* (delusion). The best conditions for
> recovery from physical sickness is *mettā* *bhāvanā*, high *puñña
> kamma* and *upekkhā* etc.
>
> \- Even if difficult situations come, you mustn’t let the mind get
> disturbed. Mind should abide blissfully every time.

**15 May 1995**

> \- All your efforts are not in vain. They will follow you like your
> shadow.

**17 May 1995**

> \- You must learn to accommodate with the place or environment to
> create harmony but not at the expense of *akusala kamma*. That you
> should know.
>
> \- There is happiness in married life and in renounced life. There is
> a difference. What is this married life? It is an illusion, an
> intoxication. It is due to not knowing things as they are. Take the
> example of Venerable Mahākassapa. How both stay together and departed
> on separate ways. That is possible.[73]
>
> \- Even if someone is kind and restrained, but it is an obstacle to
> stop this *saṁsāra*.
>
> \- Do your studies also properly. You may retire from time to time as
> you do know[74].
>
> \- Simile of the <span class="mark">sailor</span>, very important,
> crossing the river.
>
> \- You may feel this is right and that is right.
>
> \- You mustn’t enforce on others. But show with your example.
>
> \- Always \[*be*\] with thoughts of *mettā* etc.

**18 May 1995**

> \- Arahants look at all things with *upekkhā*.
>
> \- Some arahants with *abhiññā* and the Buddha had the ability to see
> the attainments of others.
>
> \- Until you come to arahantship one doesn’t know. So it is good to
> have or continue to develop *saddhā*.
>
> \- You would not always have \[quiet\] suitable surroundings. I had it
> only twice in life[75].
>
> \- You must make use of all situations to come to your goal. Obstacles
> should be made use of. You have to get involved in certain activities,
> that is unavoidable. Therefore, when you are engaged in such activity,
> direct your full concentration to that.
>
> \- Regarding the *Suttas*, certain things omitted by the Buddha means
> the person the Buddha talks to has already cultivated what is omitted.
> Sometimes that and things are omitted because certain preliminary
> things have to be cultivated.
>
> \- Your studies; they will also come to an end. It might come to an
> end suddenly.
>
> \- Though we have lived in a good way this life, we don’t know what we
> have done in the past births. So certain painful *kamma vipāka*, we
> should learn to bear it up with equanimity.
>
> \- *Mettā* *bhāvanā* and *Buddhānussati*.
>
> \- You must do *dāna*, *sīla* and *bhāvanā*. Never neglect *bhāvanā*.
> Daily.
>
> \- Due to past *kamma* certain urges may come. That you must take
> care.
>
> \- Not letting the mind go to the future and the past, sticking to the
> presently arisen Dhammas with perfect knowledge.

**19 May 1995**

> \- *Khandha Vagga* (SN22; Connected Discourses on the Aggregates) and
> *Saḷāyatana Vagga* (SN35; The Connected Discourses on the Six Sense
> Bases)—Very important.
>
> \- *Attadatthaṁ paratthena* (look after your welfare etc. Dhp 166)

- **His last words: Dhamma and the Vinaya is our teacher.** [76]

\- May you return safely.

> \- Always this path is against the stream. So, you should cut across
> like the <span class="mark">sailor</span>. You must sometimes
> accommodate,[77] but direct it slowly to the goal.

\- May you go home safely.

\- Don’t get upset by any situation. Our *kamma vipāka* has to take
place.

> \- Don’t have a fixed idea about the future or how you get about. So
> just get on peacefully moment to moment.

\- All your efforts are not in vain.

\- The highest way you can respect the Buddha, Dhamma and Saṅgha is
through the practice.

**28 May 1995**

> \- You may concentrate on the foundation—not thinking of *jhāna* etc.
> nor to be a scholar, but as the practice goes on you may refer further
> to *Suttas* and could categorize them under ‘*sīla*’, ‘*samādhi*’ and
> ‘*paññā*’.
>
> \- *Iṣṭa Devatā* \[guardian celestial being\] is there so you have
> nothing to worry.
>
> \- Studies: You may do your duty with maximum effort on your part, so
> that you would have no inner remorse or conflict concerning your duty,
> but the result should be expected with equanimity.
>
> \- You may learn Pāli when you see the necessity for it.
>
> \- Certain verses you may recite and memorise in Pāli, that which
> appeal to you.
>
> \- Mirror of the Dhamma[78], daily reflection on
> <span class="mark">death</span> etc.
>
> \- May you put brick by brick etc.
>
> \- Mustn’t be negligent and also mustn’t be too eager, but the middle
> way.
>
> \- Hope your effort and understanding will continue.
>
> \- Not letting the mind go to the future and the past, sticking to the
> presently arisen Dhammas with perfect insight as to its true nature of
> impermanence, suffering and therefore not mine. And at that time no
> *lobha*, *dosa* and *moha* (no craving, clinging—*paṭiccasamuppāda*),
> then the mind is directed to *Nibbāna*.
>
> \- “***Nibbānam paramam sukham*” \[*Nibbana* is the ultimate
> happiness/bliss\]**

**26 June 1995**

> \- *Gaṇakamoggallāna Sutta* (MN107; to Gaṇaka Moggallāna), *Dantabhūmi
> Sutta* (MN125; the grade of the tamed), *Sekha Sutta* (MN53; the
> disciple in higher training), *Ariyapariyesanā Sutta* (MN26; the noble
> search).
>
> \- *Saṁyutta Nikāya*, *Khandha Vagga* and *Saḷāyatana Vagga*—some you
> may memorise.
>
> \- *Mahāsatipaṭṭhāna Sutta* (DN22; foundations of mindfulness)
>
> \- We are always going to either the past or the future. But if we
> stick to the present, you would then experience *anicca*. That which
> is *anicca* is *dukkha*. That which is *anicca* and *dukkha* is not
> mine.
>
> \- A bhikkhu has no attendant. When you go on *cārikā* you are quite
> alone, if you are not well, the hospital is there free of charge.
>
> \- *Rāhulovada Sutta*
>
> \- Don’t think of <span class="mark">this</span> personal services,
> but think of this highest form of merit[79].
>
> \- *Buddhānussati*
>
> \- *Maraṇasati*
>
> \- *Asubha bhāvanā* (already in *Satipaṭṭhāna*, when you are
> practicing there is no ignorance, no craving, no *lobha*, *dosa*,
> *moha*).
>
> \- *Saṁyutta <span class="mark">Nikāya </span>*(Read *Khandha* and
> *Saḷāyatana Vagga*).

**29 June 1995**

> \- *Saṁyutta* <span class="mark">22</span> and 35
> onwards—(*Khandhavagga* (SN 22; Connected Discourses on the
> Aggregates) and *<span class="mark">Saḷāya</span>tanavagga* (SN 35;
> Connected Discourses on the Six Sense Bases).
>
> – No *avijjā* no *<span class="mark">saṅkhār</span>a*, no
> *<span class="mark">saṅkhār</span>a* no *bhava,* no *jāti* no old
> ag<span class="mark">e, n</span>o sickne<span class="mark">ss,
> n</span>o death. What doesn’t arise doesn’t pass away.[80]
>
> **- Read *Satipaṭṭhāna*. Practice it. The *nimittas* are there for
> *samādhi* in *Satipaṭṭhāna*.**
>
> **- Must attain *jhāna*. It is the suitable condition for entering the
> paths.**
>
> \- You may read *Mahāparinibbāna Sutta* (DN16; the Great Passing, the
> Buddha’s last days). Where it is mentioned, how you respect the
> Buddha, Dhamma and Saṅgha in the highest way, is only through the
> practice. The other thing is, though you hear that a certain teacher
> is famous or popular, compare it with the Dhamma and the Vinaya. So
> the Dhamma and the Vinaya is our teacher.
>
> \- *Ambalaṭṭhika Rāhulovāda Sutta* (MN61: Advice to Rahula at
> Ambalaṭṭhika) is very important. After the *vandanā* \[worship\].
>
> \- Nobody can make another practice or attain. They can only show like
> the Buddha. They are only *Dhamma kāya*.[81]
>
> \- Read certain passages, verses and ones that appeal to you, memorise
> them or recall them.

**8 July 1995**

> \- Solitude is most necessary for the understanding to arise. As when
> there are distractions through the sense doors, the required
> concentration used to investigate the Dhamma is disturbed.

**11 July 1995**

> \- Formations will never be complete and perfect. So you must learn
> not to be dissatisfied, but to get on with inner peace and *upekkhā*.
> And that *upekkhā* too you should reflect its true nature, it is
