> consciousness, it rises and passes too. Seeing it in this way you
> continue up until arahantship. This inner peace (*upekkhā*) you should
> see as it is, without delusion.

- **\[Whether\] I am there or not make sure you retire for the
  *poya*.**[82]

- **And before you sleep, make sure you reflect and do *mettā* *bhāvanā*
  or in other words go to sleep with a raised or elevated mind.**

- **When you have attained the path, try your best to establish others
  who are first connected to you by birth, and more beings as
  possible.**

- **Make sure while you’re doing your studies that you retire from time
  to time.**

**12 July 1995**

> \- Face facts with confidence (in general in all situations).
>
> \- *Saḷāyatana*, *Khandha*, *Ānāpānasati*, *Bojjhaṅga* \[*Salāyatana
> Vagga* (SN35; The Connected Discourses on the Six Sense Bases),
> *Khandha Vagga* (SN22; Connected Discourses on the Aggregates),
> *Ānāpānasati Sutta* (MN118; mindfulness of breathing), *Bojjhanga
> Saṁyutta* (SN46)\]
>
> \- When you are with other people, don’t be separated, but with
> compassion direct them by your example.
>
> \- Always associate and be close to people and places that have an
> understanding for this Dhamma.

*<span class="mark">Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera giving a brief Dhamma
discourse to lay devotees who had come to offer dāna at Polgasduwa
Island Hermitage. Even in brief encounters, Ven. Ñāṇavimala seemed to
see deeply into a person’s nature, offering words that met them with
quiet precision and compassion (1994).</span>*

**Reflection 05: The Protection of Mettā**

For Ven. Ñāṇavimala, compassion was not a simple sentiment, but wisdom
itself—a seeing that cut through illusion and a courage that met death
with a pure heart. A quality forged, it seemed, across many lifetimes.
As a young man in Germany, working in the farm, he battled with an
internal struggle—that ploughing the land he was killing tiny creatures.
Later on he would understand this sensitivity, one born from a deep
karmic learning.

He sometimes spoke of past-life recollections that began to occur after
his practice of mindfulness (*Satipaṭṭhāna*) had deepened. These were
not meditative experiences in the usual sense, but spontaneous
memories—appearing like dreams, though he remained conscious of
them.[83]

One such recollection concerned a past life in which he was a soldier.
In the heat of battle, he found he could not bring himself to kill. In
that moment of hesitation, an enemy horseman’s lance killed him. He
remembered the scene with startling clarity.

He later got to know in this life that a monument commemorating that war
event stood in that very place in Germany. From his recollection he came
to understand that it was because of his family at the time that he was
put into that situation, and the event was a source of his deep
rejection for harming in his most recent life.

As a layman he was interested in Hinduism, yoga and was a strict
vegetarian. He viewed Mahatma Gandhi as a role model. Later, during his
internment camp, he refused to eat meat and nearly died until his
Buddhist friends encouraged him to accept the meat for sustenance. He
recovered—and from that experience came a new appreciation of the
Buddha’s Middle Path.

As a monk, his perspective deepened further. “Everything that takes
place in this world entails killing,” he would say. He taught that all
existence in *saṁsāra*, even the very act of survival, inadvertently
involves harm—the growing of rice kills insects, and everyday acts such
as walking kills unseen beings. The highest compassion, he explained,
was not about perfecting one’s diet, but about ending rebirth
itself—extinguishing the very ‘self’ that must be sustained at the
expense of other life. He taught that one’s intention was paramount;
taking delight in tastes was a greater harm than the act of eating meat
itself. If one had an attachment to the taste of meat, he would advise,
one should make a purposeful effort to give it up for that reason.

He carried this compassion into his alms round, mindful not of what was
offered, but placing the needs of the giver foremost. When a wealthy lay
devotee once wanted to continue offering specially prepared delicacies
after the initial offering, he responded “Please give an opportunity for
poor people also to offer alms.”[84] He was especially attentive that
the poor had the chance to give, understanding—through his deep sense of
*kamma*—that their present hardship was but the unfolding of past
causes, and that through the merit of generosity now, they could help
shape brighter conditions to come.

His compassion remained consistent, even when concern for his health
became a test of it.

Later on, whilst at Polgasduwa Hermitage, Shanaka and other devotees,
concerned for his ill health, once made a special arrangement for
particular nutritious food packets to be offered to him. He refused,
stating simply, “This is not ascetic” and insisted on taking what was
normally being given to everyone.

This deep, inner quality of goodwill, was not just a philosophy; it was
a tangible force. It manifested as a protective energy, a quiet
fearlessness that could be felt by all beings, human and animal alike.
This was the quality that Ven. Gnanaloka would later come to rely on in
the remote jungles, where he directly experienced its power.

In 2007, Ven. Gnanaloka spent six months in complete silence at
Bambaragasthalawa—a remote cave within Kumana National Park (Yala East).
What struck him most about the place was not just its seclusion but its
absolute stillness. Even the animals seemed to respect the silence. The
ancient cave, once used by meditating monks during the height of
Buddhism in Sri Lanka, had likely not been inhabited by humans for
centuries. Now, it belonged to the bears and leopards who ruled the
jungle.

The nearest village was 15 to 20 kilometres away. A few concerned
devotees had arranged for a *kapakaru* (lay attendant) to offer the
daily meal. But Ven. Gnanaloka made it a point never to see or speak
with him. If there was an urgent message, it would be left in writing
near the alms bowl. Otherwise, noble silence prevailed.

One night, while lying on his side in the cave, he heard a low, rhythmic
sound. At first, it felt like a dream. Then it grew clearer—deep, heavy
breathing. Without rising, he reached toward the lamp and adjusted the
wick. As the light spread across the cave, a wild bear came into
view—just a few feet in front of him.

Instinctively, he began chanting:

“*Virūpakkehi me mettaṁ — Mettaṁ Erāpathehi*...”

(from the *Khandha Paritta*, AN 4.67–68)[85]

The bear turned and fled—vanishing into the forest like a dissolving
shadow.

Years earlier, Ven. Ñāṇavimala had shared similar encounters. While
meditating in a cave at Kudumbigala, a bear once entered and stood on
its hind legs, claws raised. Ven. Ñāṇavimala calmly recited the *Khandha
Paritta*. As he continued, the bear lowered its paws and walked away. On
another occasion, while walking through a national park, he came face to
face with a bear. He lowered his gaze, radiated *mettā*, and when he
looked up again, the bear was gone. When someone asked, “Bhante, what if
the bear had attacked you?” he replied:

“Well, then I would have died with a pure heart.”

As a young man, Shanaka once recalled Ven. Ñāṇavimala telling him:

“Recite the *Khandha Paritta* daily and act with *mettā* to all beings.”

Those simple instructions may have saved his life more than once.

Ven. Gnanaloka remembered another incident at Kudumbigala Forest
Monastery[86], deep in the jungle, seated in meditation, surrounded by
trees, insects, and venomous snakes, he remained in stillness for hours.
Then, a sensation—something heavy on his lap. He ignored it. Only later,
when lifting his robes, did he discover a snake coiled across his legs,
perhaps seeking warmth. As he gently moved, the snake raised its head
and met his gaze. There was no fear—only a quiet recognition, almost
like meeting an old friend. The snake turned, looked back a few times,
and slowly disappeared into the forest.

He realised then: this was the power of *mettā*.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala used to say:

“Develop this heart of *mettā* in the highest way to all beings. This
will be the greatest service to all beings from you.”

And from all that could be seen, he lived by this principle in every
moment.

He often spoke of *mettā-ceto-vimutti*—the liberation of the heart
through loving-kindness—not as a technique, but as a way of being.

To speak of compassion is one thing. To embody it when death stands
close is another. But this was the way Ven. Ñāṇavimala walked—not once,
but again and again—with unwavering courage, turning principle into
lived truth and encouraging others to do the same.

*Handwritten notes by Shanka Dharmakeerti, capturing teachings and
reflections from his time with Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera (1995/1996).*

**- Chapter 06 -**

**“Restrain, Abstain And Endure”**

**(Dec 1995 – Feb 1996)**

**3 December 1995**

- All these blessings and merit that you have accrued—may you achieve
  your goal.

<!-- -->

- **You may practice as mentioned in *Sutta* 107, 117, 125 of
  *Majjhima*** \[*Gaṇakamoggallāna Sutta* (MN 107; to Gaṇaka
  Moggallāna); *Mahācattārīsaka Sutta* (MN117; the great forty);
  *Dantabhūmi Sutta* (MN125; the grade of the tamed)\] and
  *Ambalaṭṭhikarāhulovāda Sutta* (MN61; advice to Rāhula at
  Ambalaṭṭhika), **if you are keen to enter the path, then
  *Kha<span class="mark">ndha</span> Vagga*** (SN22; Connected
  Discourses on the Aggregates) and *Saḷāyatana Vagga* (SN35; The
  Connected Discourses on the Six Sense Bases). **This effort you make
  in this direction will be the highest form of merit. And will be the
  only way to repay parents, relatives and ultimately to all beings. It
  will be a blessing.**

<!-- -->

- As a layman, *Suttas* mentioned to Mahānāma[87].

- Know when you \[return\], you would have to accommodate a bit. You may
  not find people with an understanding for this. In that case, you
  would not try to convince them or convert them, but with the heart of
  *mettā*, you should also keep away from them and should not follow
  their inclinations.

- You will truly be able to help your relatives and everyone else only
  if you purify yourself. Because from a purified mind, purified actions
  follow.

<!-- -->

- *Bhava Sutta* (SN45.164; becoming)

<!-- -->

- *“Iti kho bhikkhave dhammā ca dhamme abhisandenti Dhamma ca dhamme
  paripūrenti a<span class="mark">pārā pāraṅgamanāyā’ti</span>”*

*“*Thus, monks, phenomenon flow on to phenomenon (*Dhamma*), and
phenomenon fulfil

phenomenon in the journey from here to the goal”

*Cetanākaraṇīya Sutta* (AN11.2; volition)

**4 December 1995**

- <span class="mark">\[Quote from Ven. Ñāṇavimala\] “Regarding
  *Saddhātissa*, it may be true that we would have been together. But
  one must stick to the present. As the present makes the future. One
  must control the mind. They are just thoughts (past and
  future).</span> This name seems to be very familiar, we would have
  been together in past lives. **But you must stick to the All
  Enlightened One’s words and advice. As in *Mahāparinibbāna Sutta*
  (DN16; the Great Passing), where the Buddha said, *‘After my passing
  away, that your teacher will be the Dhamma and the Vinaya’.”*** [88]

<!-- -->

- \[*Quote from Ven. Ñāṇavimala*\] “I also got something after 20 years
  in the Saṅgha. There is *kamma* behind \[this\]”. \[*Personal Note:*\]
  (Maybe an attainment etc.)[89]

- *Ariya sīla* is observed when one is practicing *Satipaṭṭhāna*.

- You can’t fall into negligence because you have a lot to work on. That
  is to overcome the hindrances (five of them) and to attain *jhāna*.
  And that state of peace and so as to see its true nature of *anicca*,
  *dukkha* and *anatta*, because it is also a state of consciousness.

- *Mātugāma Sutta* (SN37.1; women)[90]

- Sutta 39 and 40 in *Majjhima* leading to *jhāna*—*Mahāassapura Sutta*
  (MN39; the greater discourse at assapura); *Cūḷaassapura Sutta* (MN40;
  the shorter discourse at assapura).

- A Gradual Training in 107 and 125 in *Majjhima*—*Gaṇakamoggallāna
  Sutta* (MN107; to gaṇaka moggallāna); *Dantabhūmi Sutta* (MN125; the
  grade of the tamed).

- You must not try to preach and so you must center on yourself. Based
  on your pure state of mind, purified action will flow. To benefit both
  you and all beings.

- You cannot be negligent as it is so uncertain. The *sāsana* nearly
  disappeared twice in Sri Lanka. It can disappear again. So we do not
  know what will happen tomorrow. So make haste right now.

- To get *saṁvega* (think of the endless past what we had to go
  through), e.g. the tears more than the four oceans etc. and what is
  ahead of us in Dīgha 26—*Cakkavattisīhanāda Sutta* (DN26; the lion’s
  roar on the wheel-turning monarch).

- Dīgha 27—*Aggañña Sutta* (DN27; on knowledge of beginnings)

**5 December 1995**

- *Ambalaṭṭhikarāhulovāda Sutta* (MN61; advice to Rāhula at
  Ambalaṭṭhika): You do things and follow actions that benefit both
  parties or that which reduces *lobha* (greed)*, dosa* (hate) *and
  moha* (delusion).

- The way you reflected in the past \[how\] the present things are
  formed; for example, you have acted with ‘I’ conceit etc. Therefore,
  now \[there occurs\] the present manifestation of the body and so on.
  As time goes on you will understand it deeply. Nothing from outside
  could help you to see things in reality. But the mind \[itself\]
  should be slowly established and trained to reflect \[on things\] as
  not mine and so on.

- You must make plans for the near future (e.g. going to the station in
  the morning) and quickly come back to the present and must practice
  *sati sampajañña*. This should be the way throughout.

<!-- -->

- **Past inclination would manifest, but one must delight in overcoming
  those inclinations and coming to mastery of them, in the present life.
  It is a great difficulty to overcome attractions to people \[with
  whom\] one was connected to in past lives**[91]**.**

<!-- -->

- Whatever momentary happiness there is, it is \[merely\] a temporary
  intoxication. But far more suffering is connected with it. Therefore,
  overcome it by developing the opposite faculties \[5 *indriyas*—5
  spiritual faculties\] and \[take\] delight \[in them\]. For example,
  Ven. Nanda when he was an Arahant, a *devī* \[female celestial being\]
  from the *deva loka* \[celestial realm\] who had once been his consort
  appeared. However, he replied that, as an Arahant, he had no such
  connections[92].

<!-- -->

- *Majjhima Sutta* 131 (*Bhaddekaratta Sutta**,*** MN131) practice like
  that. Stick to the present.

- When you go to certain temples or meditation centers (Remember
  *Mātugāma*)—*Mātugāma Saṁyutta* (SN 37; linked discourses on females).

<!-- -->

- **Try to avoid such contact \[with the opposite sex\] as far as
  possible.**

<!-- -->

- **Don’t look at them.**

- **If you have to look at them, don’t talk.**

- **If you have to talk, then think of the *asubha*.**

<!-- -->

- When you have attained the first *jhāna*, you should go to the second
  and so on until one reaches *Nibbāna*. Never should one stick to even
  good states, however, as a Bodhisattva, the Buddha was never satisfied
  with good states).

- Certain verses or *Suttas* that had an impact, isolate them and repeat
  it over and over again.

<!-- -->

- Further qualities to be developed when one is established in 5
  \[spiritual\] faculties (*indriya*), *saddhā, viriya, sati, samādhi,
  paññā* \[*Paṭhamavibhaṅga Sutta* (SN48.36; Analysis (1)\].

There are six qualities to be grown - *Mahānāma Sutta* (AN6.10; to
Mahānāma)

1.  Recollect the Tathāgata—Happiness should develop in connection to
    repeated recollection.

2.  Recollect the Dhamma

3.  Recollect the Saṅgha

4.  You should recollect your own virtues

5.  You should recollect your own generosity (*cāga*)

6.  You should recollect the *devas*

**6 December 1995**

- Daily practice of a bhikkhu is to overcome the five hindrances and to
  develop the five *indriya*.[93]

- And we come back to *Ambalaṭṭhika Rāhulovāda Sutta* (MN61; advice to
  Rāhula at Ambalaṭṭhika) *(Tīṇi Sucaritāni—three ways of good conduct)*
  *ariyakanta sīla*[94], this is accomplished when you practice
  *Satipaṭṭhāna*—*Mahāsatipaṭṭhāna Sutta* (DN22; the foundations of
  mindfulness).

- You should act in the way of *Mahāsuññata Sutta* (MN122; the greater
  discourse on voidness). That is verbal and mental actions.

<!-- -->

- *Majjhima* 131—*Bhaddekaratta Sutta* (MN131; a single excellent night)
  is very important, because it is not worth following the past (past
  lives etc.) and having future hopes and definite plans etc.

- Visions and dreams and so on, one makes a note of it but does not
  follow them. Some may be right, some maybe not. But should always and
  totally rely on the words of the Buddha.

- And regarding attainment; how can anyone know unless declared by the
  Buddha or an Arahant with *abhiññā*? It was forbidden for bhikkhus to
  declare their attainment, how much so laymen. And what’s the point of
  speculating? What one should ensure is that one is acting in the
  present in accordance with a way that brings one closer to a state of
  peace and certainty regarding the Dhamma.

- <span class="mark">\[Quote from Ven. Ñāṇavimala\] “For example, there
  was a yogi who \[believed\] they were an *Anāgāmi*, and I said that
  they would go mad if they continued to think in that way. A year or so
  later, it happened that they \[did indeed\] go mad.”</span>[95]

<!-- -->

- In *Majjhima* 105—*Sunakkhatta Sutta* (MN105; to Sunakkhatta), how
  many came and declared in front of the Buddha their final attainment.
  Among them some were true and some were not.

- It is peaceful when one practices the Dhamma \[in the present
  moment\].

- Now when you \[return\] to the layman’s life, it is as if you are
  going on *piṇḍapāta*: you are going to give and this is the highest
  giving. First you may establish them in *dāna*, *sīla,*
  *bhāvanā*—*Sigālovāda Sutta* (DN31; advice to Sigāla). Furthermore you
  can \[guide them\] to establish them in Buddha, Dhamma, Saṅgha. For
  those who are further interested in attaining the path: \[the\]
  *Suttas* taught to *Mahānāma* and
  *<span class="mark">Anāthapiṇḍika</span>* and *Rāhulovāda* *Sutta*,
  *Mettā* *bhāvanā* (as mentioned by Ven. Ñāṇamoli).

<!-- -->

- If you are unable to practise the three ways of conduct perfectly, do
  not have remorse; what is past is past. However, must make a
  determination to avoid it in the future.

- You must encourage others in *paviveka* (solitude)—for laypeople, at
  least on the *poya* days. As for yourself, try to retire \[into
  solitude\] as soon as possible, 2 or 3 days before and after the
  *poya*.

- The way you act and direct the mind in that environment is very
  creative and constructive. One must constantly concentrate on one’s
  practice and only by one’s example that one encourages others.

- Attaining the *Jhānas* step by step training the *Suttas* 107 and 125
  of *Majjhima*—*Gaṇakamoggallāna Sutta* (MN107; to gaṇaka moggallāna);
  *Dantabhūmi Sutta* (MN125; the grade of the tamed) and then to attain
  to the path, *Khandha* and *Saḷāyatana Saṁyuttas*.

- In any case, daily one must make it a point even if it is in the night
  for this *bhāvanā*[96].

<!-- -->

- **One must not overload with intellectual grasping of the *Suttas*.
  One picks out one or two and bring the mind to that actual nature of
  function mentioned in the *Suttas*.**[97] **This is very important!**

<!-- -->

- To get *saṁvega* (spiritual urgency)*:* Dīgha 26—Cakkavattisīhanāda
  Sutta (DN26; the lion’s roar on the wheel-turning monarch);
  *Anamataggasaṃyutta* (SN15; connected discourses on without
  discoverable beginning).

- And never be satisfied with good states.

<!-- -->

- You must establish others in only what you yourself are established in
  and what you clearly see and have experienced.

- When some people (laymen) recite the *gāthās* etc. in their shrine
  room, certain *devatās* (blood relatives) come and impart certain
  information. They have a wider field of knowledge. E.g. when Reverend
  Sir went on *cārikā* in Minneriya even how he was offered a lunch
  pack.[98]

- All the merit that you have accrued and the past background in the
  Dhamma etc. will come to fruit, even though you have to deviate a bit
  from suitable conditions for the practice presently.

- Do you remember *Makhādeva Sutta* (MN83; King Makhādeva) in
  *Majjhima*, the noble practice started by Makhādeva which led to the
  Brahma world \[being\] stopped at the point of king Nimi.

- The Buddha warns how not to be the last in the *Ariyan* lineage
  \[noble lineage\].[99]

> **7 December 1995**

- When you use all that is available then it spoils the Saṅgha.

<!-- -->

- That is what has happened in the temple(s).

- **What you should do is restrain or abstain and endure.**

- This is the Way.

- The bhikkhu’s attitude is to leave things.

- When he endures, he does not feed on craving.

<!-- -->

- To like solitude, it is necessary to have *kamma* in the past. Or it
  should be cultivated. Because it is necessary to strive in solitude to
  experience certain things that cannot be reasoned out, like *jhānas*
  and *vipassanā* knowledge.

- *Sutta* 9 in *Majjhima*—*Sammādiṭṭhi Sutta* (MN9; right view)

<!-- -->

- Like Raṭṭhapāla and others, like the one who was married, the wife
  kept the child in front of him, because he was an arahant, there is no
  effect.[100] It is *acintiya* \[inconceivable\]
  <span class="mark"></span>at thought level, to think how arahants are
  thinking. (They are looking at the whole process from outside). As
  they are considering as not mine and not I. That’s why most of the
  arahants are silent.

<!-- -->

- You must consider yours’ and others’ welfare. You have already done it
  with your relatives . It’s the beginning.

- But never forget your welfare which is most important. And it is the
  first priority! “*Attadatthaṁ paratthena”* (*Attadatthatthera Vatthu*,
  Dhp 166).

**8 December 1995**

- **If you follow another religion, that means you never got a true
  understanding in the Dhamma.**

***Important:***

- **Some may decide to be *Anagārika* and so on.**[101] **But one must
  consider this may create *asmi-māna* \[egocentric conceit\], that I am
  better than others or different to others etc. It is important out of
  respect and gratitude to the Buddha that one accommodates, and has the
  Buddha as one’s constant aim. For three years I was an *upāsaka* and I
  was quite alright like that, but I reflected how I got all that
  opportunity, it is because of the Saṅgha. It is always good not to
  separate oneself from the outside framework. Always respecting the
  Buddha, Dhamma and Saṅgha.**

<!-- -->

- By entering the Saṅgha, one encourages the Saṅgha and brings unity.

<!-- -->

- **Less you need to support yourself as a bhikkhu or as a layman, the
  better it is.**

<!-- -->

- Always must not worry what changes from outside. But must know even if
  things that seem to be pleasant are connected with feelings. Anyone
  who delights in feelings, delights in suffering. So, one must be happy
  that one is constantly following the way that learns to delight in the
  Dhamma, that is ultimately developed beyond feelings.

- Always developing equanimity, see even that the highest state of mind
  is connected with consciousness. For consciousness needs an object and
  depends on the body, so it is *anicca, dukkha* and *anatta*.

<!-- -->

- To do all these it takes some time. So, you have to see it as a layman
  or *bhikkhu* that one first reduces
  *<span class="mark">saṅkhār</span>as* that is coming up till *jhāna*.
  So, it is a gradual process of reducing
  *<span class="mark">saṅkhār</span>as* and overcoming them.

- Whenever you notice that you are delighting in some kind of pleasant
  feeling, know that there is delusion at that time. From time to time
  the attraction to the opposite sex will come. You should know that for
  many lives we have given into this strongest fetter. It is not easy to
  overcome overnight, only by this *asubha* that one sees the delusion
  behind it. And one overcomes it immediately. And again *Rāhulovāda
  Sutta*, for my welfare and for others, and should firmly stick to the
  learning to reflect before, while and after all actions.

<!-- -->

- When meeting people from other religions, you should instruct them
  (mentioned by Venerable Piyadassi) and encourage them to follow their
  religion.[102] All religions talk about *dāna, sīla* and *bhāvanā*.
  But in this Dhamma there is this *Satipaṭṭhāna*.

- All religions and their founders are ones who reached a certain high
  state. But it lasts only for some time and collapses again. Whether it
  is Islam, Hinduism, Christianity, Judaism.

- The western countries are materially driven, each one wanting to
  advance more than the other. Then war begins. And then when everything
  collapses, they go to the churches etc. But when things become
  alright, again they get back to building. This is because their
  spiritual experience is based on *micchā diṭṭhi* (wrong view).

<!-- -->

- When you think how the *arahant* thinks, it is not possible to
  understand, but if we just consider he experiences things from a
  distance.

<!-- -->

- There is happiness in worldly life and the suffering of the renounced
  life. But the suffering of the renounced life is better than the
  happiness of the worldly life. When I went on *cārikā*, I was
  completely independent; but do not think it was easy. There was
  suffering and it is like that until death. No formation is complete or
  satisfactory. And all that we experience, that which we have done or
  created, so making sure the mind is always directed to a state (where
  one will be free from all this suffering forever) without creating
  disharmony or conflict with the surrounding that one is in.

- You must not try to be a scholar, and going on preaching etc. like in
  the temples, are one activity after the other—more *saṅkhāras*.
  Satisfying the *dāyakas* \[lay devotees\] etc. and losing sight of the
  essential practice, which in return makes the Saṅgha deteriorate.

- The *Suttas* *Majjhima* 131, 19 ,20, 107, 117, 125—*Bhaddekaratta
  Sutta* (MN131; a single excellent night); *Dvedhāvitakka Sutta* (MN19;
  two kinds of thought); *Vitakkasaṇṭhāna Sutta* (MN20; removal of
  distracting thoughts); *Gaṇakamoggallāna Sutta* (MN107; to Gaṇaka
  Moggallāna); *Mahācattārīsaka Sutta* (MN117; the great forty);
  *Dantabhūmi Sutta* (MN125; the grade of the tamed)

- When you get back \[home\], *Mahānāma Suttas*.[103]

- This way of activity (mentioned in Mahānāma *Suttas*) gives a chance
  to glimpse the ultimate at that level, as a layman.

- 

**9 December 1995**

- **When you direct your mind to a certain sphere, then as if by force,
  you get support and response in that direction. For example: when
  someone is trying to climb a mountain, he begins to meet people who
  are on the same path and they help each other to come to the goal.**

<!-- -->

- One must learn in all one’s activities to stick to the present.
  *Majjhima* 131 (*Bhaddekaratta Sutta*, MN131)

- One must not try to think whether one has attained the path or one
  will attain the path. But must stick to the practice as advised by the
  Buddha, not sticking to a person, etc.

- Practice according to the *Suttas* 107,117,125 (*Gaṇakamoggallāna
  Sutta*, MN107; *Mahācattārīsaka Sutta*, MN117; *Dantabhūmi Sutta*,
  MN125) leading to the *jhāna*. And if one wants to enter the path
  *Khandha* and *Saḷāyatana Saṁyutta<span class="mark">s</span>*. This
  is almost the noble eightfold path.

- Seeing always the causes of this suffering and removing it. It is
  removing the *nīvaraṇas.*

- There is always the danger that one may become a scholar. In the
  beginning, the first arahants heard only one *Sutta*. You may make
  note of certain *Suttas* that had an impact. And may repeat them. Only
  as the practice progresses that you may refer to more *Suttas*.

- You must try your best to help others by your example. When you turn
  away. And when they see that, it is an encouragement for them to do
  the same.

- When you don’t think of a person or an individual, but as a whole, for
  example as the Mahā Saṅgha, that is spiritual. One must regularly
  develop the delight connected to Buddha, Dhamma, Saṅgha.

- *Purāṇa kamma* is the present body and what is experienced with it.
  And making sure in the present one reflects wisely the presently
  arisen phenomenon, so that future arising would not take place.

- **May you be successful in all your endeavors (last words).**

**11 December 1995**

- From *sati* it is possible to attain *jhāna*.

- As you go on, one day you should determine to learn the Dhamma in the
  exact words of the Buddha, that is in <span class="mark">Pāli</span>.
  There is no other language to \[better\] describe the ideas expressed
  by the Buddha, except Pali. The other thing is when the translator
  translates with his defiled mind the meaning undoubtedly changes.

- You may read the *Thera Gāthās* (Th; verses of the elder monks) to get
  inspiration. Like this you can add some knowledge that fits to the
  main practice.

- What is most important now is to establish these practices so that
  they become a habit. In all conditions.

- As soon as you concentrate and see every experience from the point of
  view of the Buddha’s teaching, all difficulties and suffering vanish.

- Then one looks at it from a distance, as if not mine etc. It goes up
  to the end (arahantship), the practice until one is continuously
  experiencing from that point of view. You see they are just processes
  and phenomena. When you stick to this awareness certainty should come
  and doubt should vanish.

- May you be a help for many beings. It starts at home. All who have a
  body will go through, some time or the other, the suffering connected
  to it. May they all come to the utter release from this suffering. May
  you go ahead of all of them, that will be a great help.

<!-- -->

- **Most of life is created by sex. Most of the time we meet people who
  were connected to us from the past. One is totally blinded \[by\]
  illusion when one is sexually attracted. And \[once\] involved,
  \[mental\] bondages are made only after all that is done. One
  \[should\] see the suffering connected \[with it\] and involved in it.
  So one must take care.**

<!-- -->

- In the Dhamma when help is possible to be given, you may do so but not
  at personal expense, otherwise one may get on, making all these things
  fall off[104], because what we see, hear, etc. there is *kamma* behind
  it, but they are just feelings, perception “not mine” “not I”.

- You may discuss with your parents, relatives, telling them clearly and
  definitely your intentions.

- Later on, when you are definitely certain you may act like
  Raṭṭhapāla—*Raṭṭhapāla Sutta* (MN 82; on Raṭṭhapāla). But you should
  act in such a way that will create harmony, and not disharmony.[105]
  And especially your relatives have been useful and helpful from a
  young age. And they are connected to you from past lives. So, you have
  to help each other.

**12 December 1995**

- Make sure you develop the mind now, not to worry about what is ahead
  and what has happened. But learning to observe the present with skill.
  Try to get established in this until you are here. Then you will be
  able to face anything with an undisturbed mind. And when you try to
  avoid foreseen activities and bondages, restraining your inner
  inclination, remember this is *kamma*. And when you are fully
  directing the mind, things would change from outside.

<!-- -->

- **Very Important: You must learn to get along alone. Because what is
  ultimately to be cultivated, goes beyond outside access and
  intervention, and in any case the outside situations change too. I too
  had support for five years and was left alone. Then I have to go
  through the *Suttas* by myself. There is *kamma* behind all these
  things. You may meet certain people who would give support at
  different levels. You must be like a clever sailor or a mountaineer,
  not going straight through but criss-cross, never losing the aim.**

<!-- -->

- For your uncle: *Sigālovāda Sutta* (DN31; advice to Sigāla), *Mettā*
  *Bhāvanā*, *Buddhism in a Nutshell* by Nārada Thera.

<!-- -->

- You may discuss it with your parents and relatives. Prestige is not
  important. In any case all this material gain ultimately brings
  disaster. And you may tell your future aim. And you may openly tell
  the difficulties faced when attending the classes etc.

<!-- -->

- Even to enter the Saṅgha, there is *kamma* behind it. So one must try
  to support the bhikkhus to get on undisturbed.

<!-- -->

- It is impossible that you spend the whole time in *pīti* and
  happiness.

<!-- -->

- **Regarding women, act in the way Venerable Ānanda was advised to
  do**—***Mahāparinibbāna Sutta* (DN16; the Great Passing).**[106]

- **When you go to the classes and when you practice restraint etc. you
  are practicing the Dhamma.**

**13 December 1995**

- We may meet again or not or these conditions may come or will not
  come. But what is important is that you direct to the Dhamma in
  whatever condition one is in. That this state of mind is undisturbed.
  One lives in the present, that itself is a happiness. And making the
  conditions more suitable to do that undisturbed and continuously in a
  harmonized way. Because when the objects change very fast it is
  difficult to practice this.

<!-- -->

- The Hindus and so on, when their teacher dies, the pupils \[seek\] out
  where he is \[re-\]born and bring him back to teach. The yogis, no
  doubt, work very hard. They consider that which they \[strive\] for as
  permanent, but it is \[still\] within consciousness and therefore a
  formation. All formations are *anicca* (impermanent), *dukkha*
  (suffering) and *anatta* (non-self). They have *asmi-māna*
  (ego-conceit), but the bhikkhus do not.

- Many arahants at the time of the Buddha were previously in the
  Brahmaloka[107] or one who has cultivated *jhāna*. When they met the
  Buddha they easily attained *jhāna* and arahantship. In any case they
  experienced what they have done (the *kamma*) to come back to the
  human world (*manussaloka*).

- You may not be able to sit in *bhāvanā*[108] all the time. But you may
  go a step below, and contribute towards the concentration by reading
  the *Suttas*, reciting striking stanzas and memorizing etc.

- The Buddha as Bodhisatta (Makhādeva) arose many times in the
  Brahmaloka, but came back, still a *puthujjana* (common worldling).
  Until he understood the *paṭiccasamuppāda* (dependent origination).
  Then he became the *Dhamma Rāja* (king of Dhamma).

**14th - 16th February 1996**

# *Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera seated near the monks’ dining hall at Polgasduwa Island Hermitage. He generally discouraged taking photographs, reminding those who asked: “Why do you want to see this foul body? See the Dhamma.” (circa mid–late 1990s).*

# 

# 

# 

# **Reflection 06: Soft like Butter, Hard like Diamond**

Venerable Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera embodied a profound paradox—infinitely
gentle in spirit, yet unbreakable in will. To some, he appeared as a
saintly monk, radiating serene benevolence; to others, he was a fierce
ascetic whose unyielding determination allowed no compromise on the path
to liberation. As Ven. Gnanaloka would later reflect, his teacher was
utterly “intense in the Dhamma,” yet that intensity was always held
within a soft, warm smile—a contrast captured perfectly by the way he
would describe him: “soft like butter, hard like diamond.”

He did not shape his teachings to please, but to awaken. Sometimes his
words were tender, at other times sharp; sometimes his silence carried
more weight than speech. Yet always they were directed precisely to the
person before him. Whoever came to him was urged to walk with urgency,
as though time were slipping away. For many, this intensity was too much
to bear. Drawn by the aura of a saint, they often found themselves
unsettled by the fierceness of his path. With Ven. Ñāṇavimala, there was
no idle talk, no wasted time. It was practice, practice, and more
practice.

A senior monk who had completed over ten rains retreats once came to pay
respects. Shockingly, Ven. Ñāṇavimala treated him almost as a novice,
advising that he should remain under the guidance of a teacher until
maturity was reached—Ven. Gnanaloka felt awkward, even wondering if Ven.
Ñāṇavimala had misjudged him.

Yet as they left the kuti, the monk turned to Ven. Gnanaloka and said
quietly, “He knows everything about me.”

What seemed harsh was, in truth, a precise instruction, spoken exactly
as it was needed.

At other times, the lesson came without words. On one occasion, a doctor
was brought by lay supporters who thought he might be of help. As if
thinking Ven. Ñāṇavimala was just a frail old man with little awareness,
the doctor addressed his words to Ven.
Mettavih<span class="mark">ārī</span>, one of the attendants, rather
than Ven. Ñāṇavimala himself.

At one point he made a direct remark to him: “Everyone thinks you’re an
arahant, but I don’t consider like that”

Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s response was not in words. He remained in silence, his
neutral gaze resting with a quiet softness, a trace of a smile upon his
face.

Nothing in him shifted—no trace of irritation, no hint of pride.

For Shanaka, however, it was very different. Hearing his teacher spoken
of so carelessly stirred a surge of anger.

“I wanted to throw him out of the kuti,” he later admitted.

To be near Ven. Ñāṇavimala was often to feel as though one had stepped
into a kind of force field—an atmosphere of stillness where the mind
naturally became settled. But in that instant, it was as if he had been
pushed outside it.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala seemed to grow distant, and even the Buddha image nearby
to the side appeared to recede.

The moment came like a vision, exposing with clarity what had truly
arisen in his own heart: a thought of anger and ill will. Where his own
mind was inflamed, Ven. Ñāṇavimala sat utterly unmoved by either praise
or disrespect. The contrast was unmistakable.

Even in matters of requisites, Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s attitude remained the
same—gentle yet unbending. During his student years in Cyprus, Shanaka
had worked for the first time in his life, laboring for seven days.
After returning back to Sri Lanka, he went to a shop with his earnings
and carefully selected the best robe, alms bowl, and other requisites he
could find, preparing a complete *Atapirikara*—the traditional offering
of a monk’s eight basic requisites. He brought it to his teacher with
deep devotion, expecting Ven. Ñāṇavimala to accept it.

Smiling at the memory, Ven. Gnanaloka later recalled, “I can still
picture myself standing before him in his room. When I offered the
*Atapirikara*, he didn’t accept it!” To Shanaka’s determined insistence,
his teacher’s response was gently “Offer it to the Saṅgha”.

It was an answer Shanaka would hear many times. Though he was among Ven.
Ñāṇavimala’s closest attendants, there was never special treatment. This
was a level of asceticism that, even after decades of his own practice,
Ven. Gnanaloka felt he could never match. “Even for a glass of water, he
didn’t ask,” he reflects. His teacher seemed to exist in a state of
perfect mindfulness. A quiet, unwavering attention infused his every
action. The smallest gesture. The simplest word. Nothing, it seemed, was
ever done heedlessly.

Yet this firmness was never coldness. On rare occasions, Ven. Ñāṇavimala
would accept offerings—but always out of compassion, and seemingly from
a deep karmic sensitivity. Once, a young boy mentally disturbed had come
to meet him and offered him a simple packet of Nestomalt \[powdered
malted food drink\]. Another time, some of Shanaka’s friends from India
placed their offering before him: a dozen bananas, unceremoniously
wrapped in newspaper. To the quiet amusement of the onlooking Shanaka,
Ven. Ñāṇavimala accepted both without hesitation, seeming to know that
for these individuals, such an opportunity might never arise again, that
this act of merit carried a weight they could not otherwise gain. For
them, his acceptance was an act of kindness. For others, his gentle
refusals became lessons in detachment.

His responses were never predictable. He would usually dismiss requests
for photographs. Shanaka’s grandmother learned this first-hand when Ven.
Ñāṇavimala was on *piṇḍapāta* stopping by her house. When she asked to
take a picture, his response was a teaching on detachment; “Why do you
want to see this foul body? See the *Dhamma*.”

Yet on one occasion, when a foreign visitor who was due to leave
Polgasduwa that day requested some photographs, to Shanaka’s surprise,
he agreed. Then Ven. Ñāṇavimala turned toward Shanaka with a hint of
amusement and said, “It’s not a movie.” The act of allowing, so
different from his usual refusals, was a key insight for Shanaka. It was
a glimpse into a compassion that was not bound by rigid consistency, but
one that responded with wisdom to what was needed in the moment—whether
a sharp lesson in detachment or a simple act of kindness. As Ven.
Gnanaloka later reflected: “Even for the smallest things, I would ask
him what to do—because I knew the answer would always be different.”

As he reflected on these seemingly contradictory responses, Ven.
Gnanaloka came to see that they were not inconsistencies, but the
actions of a mind that had gone beyond rigid rules. The source of this
freedom was his very way of seeing the world, a perspective Ven.
Gnanaloka was given a rare glimpse of on another occasion. Once, Ven.
Ñāṇavimala remarked that “life is just a dream,” and with those words,
he laughed. It was not amusement, but a glimpse of how he saw through
the play of existence.

For Ven. Gnanaloka, so accustomed to Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s quiet composure,
the very quality of this rare laughter stood out in contrast.

This same freedom, which gave his practice its unbending strength, was
also the source of a simple, human tenderness. Perhaps the most touching
glimpse of this came just after Shanaka’s ordination. He had gone into
the ceremony as a layman in jeans and a t-shirt, with a long beard. Ven.
Ñāṇavimala, bedridden at the time, had been unable to attend. After the
ceremony, the newly named Venerable Gnanaloka went to see his teacher,
his head and face now clean-shaven, dressed in the saffron robes for the
very first time. As he stood before the master, Ven. Ñāṇavimala simply
gazed at him for some moments, as if pondering. Then, his very first
words were not about the Dhamma, but about his appearance. He remarked,
“With your beard gone, you’re looking different.” In that moment, it was
not the fierce ascetic speaking, but more like a spiritual father to his
son.

For over sixteen years, Ven. Gnanaloka saw this paradox again and again.
Ven. Ñāṇavimala never clung, never favoured, never softened the path to
make it easier. Yet behind his firmness there was always compassion. To
many, this intensity was too much, and they drifted away. But for those
who stayed, the lessons were unforgettable.

*Handwritten notes by Shanka Dharmakeerti, capturing teachings and
reflections from his time with Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera (1996).*

**- Chapter 07 -**

**“Stick To The Exact Words Of The Buddha”**

**(Feb 1996 – April 1996)**

**20 February 1996**

**Practice step by step from 1 to 7 below:**

1\. *Mahānāma Sutta* (AN8.25; Mahānāma)

2\. *Gaṇakamoggallāna Sutta* (MN107; to Gaṇaka Moggallāna)

3\. *Dantabhūmi Sutta* (MN125; the grade of the tamed)

4\. *Mahācattārīsaka Sutta* (MN117; the great forty)

5\. *Ambalaṭṭhika Rāhulovāda Sutta* (MN61; advice to Rāhula at
Ambalaṭṭhika)

6\. *Khandha Saṁyutta* (SN22; connected discourses on the aggregates)
and *Saḷāyatana Saṁyutta* (SN 35; connected discourses on the six sense
bases) (22 onwards; 50 to 55 in *Saṁyutta*)[110]

7\. **kālaṁ āgameyya (waiting for one’s time/death)**[111]

- *Salla Sutta* (SN36.6; the dart)

- ***Khantī* \[Patience\]**

> *“Khantī paramaṁ tapo titikkhā, Nibbānaṁ paramaṁ vadanti Buddhā;*
>
> *Na hi pabbajito parūpaghātī, Na samaṇo hoti paraṁ viheṭhayanto.”*

“Forbearing patience is the highest austerity. *Nibbāna* is supreme, say
the Buddhas.

He, verily, is not a recluse who harms another. Nor is he an ascetic who
oppresses others.”

(Dhp 184)

> \- Develop this heart of *mettā* in the highest way to all beings.
> This will be the greatest service to all beings from you.

> \- *Majjhima Nikāya*: *Cūḷagosiṅga* *Sutta* (MN31; the lesser

> positions. So that one tears away from the *kāmaloka* (the sensual
> realm) altogether. When you consider that, turning away happens
> naturally.
>

> (MN117; the great forty) that leads to *jhāna* and then *Saṁyutta* 22
> to 50–55 to attain *sotāpanna*, if you continue to practice like this,
> at latest, at the moment of death, one will attain the path.[112]

- **You won’t be able to take the ten precepts when you are a layman,
  but you can take eight precepts (*atthaṅgika uposatha* *sīla*) and the
  *ājīvaṭṭhamaka sīla* (eight precepts with right livelihood as the
  eighth)**[116]**.**

**2 March 1996**

**Remember the potter Ghaṭīkāra—*Ghaṭīkāra Sutta* (MN81; with
Ghaṭīkāra). Consider it carefully (he was an Anāgāmi, yet a layman).**

- What arises passes away. What arises by causes they pass away. The
  more one begins to look into the state of mind and begins to consider
  things as they are—*Paṭiccasamuppāda*—the more one begins to look in
  this essential way as a regular practice, that begins to grow and in
  this way, lot of unwholesome states come to an end. And Right View
  (*sammā-diṭṭhi)* sets in more firmly.

- May you by your example and advice, help all beings to tread on the
  path.

- Do everything with *sati-sampajañña* (mindfulness and clear
  comprehension) and also *Ambalaṭṭhika Rāhulovāda Sutta* (MN61; advice
  to Rāhula at Ambalaṭṭhika).

- Stick to the exact words and advice of the Buddha[117].

- This way one will be under the best guidance.

- Whatever you do as a layman—a career etc.—it should be ultimately
  directed to the welfare of all beings.

**3 March 1996**

- Daily you may reflect on *kālaṁ āgameyya* (waiting for one’s
  time/death) even as a layman.

- You may continue to take *ājīvaṭṭhamaka sīla* \[eight precepts\] until
  life lasts. A lot of people do, and it will be a great support.

**<span class="mark">27 March</span> 1996**

- *Sutta* 19, 20 *Majjhima*—*Dvedhāvitakka Sutta* (MN19; two kinds of

  thoughts) for Dhamma thoughts and Dhamma speech in *Majjhima*
  122—*Mahāsuññata Sutta* (MN122; the greater discourse on voidness).

- Don’t build up any bondages with anybody or anything except with the
  word of the Buddha \[*Suttas*\].

- In your kuti, you might have a place for *caṅkamana* \[walking

  The *<span class="mark">Ādīnava</span>* \[disadvantages or

  *Udāna*—*Uposatha Sutta* (Ud 5.5; uposatha).

  mind through loving-kindness\], it will strengthen the faculties.[118]

**Go through:**

5\. *Kālaṁ āgameyya* \[waiting for one’s time/death\]

**28 March 1996**

- Greek mythology originates from Hinduism. They had contact with the
  East. E.g. China and India, etc. So most of the myths are
  \[influenced\] from Hindu\[ism\].

- <span class="mark">Even Christ had beginnings from the East. Though he
  could not fully grasp the Dhamma as he had a Jewish background
  \[rooted in Abrahamic beliefs in a Creator-God\]. He was highly
  saintly, though he allowed drinking wine and killing
  animals</span>[119]<span class="mark">.</span>

- When old inclinations appear, one must deal with them tactfully. It is
  impossible to fight them.

- The one who captured Troy, he had to cross a stretch of ocean. People
  warned him telling him that on the one side there were abysses and
  only death could come if you turn to that side. And on the other side
  there are nymphs alluring, but they are flesh eating. So he told his
  fellow sailors to cover their ears with wax and to tie him to the mast
  and if he tries to loosen himself, then to tighten further. This was
  to be done until that stretch was passed. In the case of the bhikkhu,
  it is the practice of *Satipaṭṭhāna*.[120]

<!-- -->

- **The other is how the cowherd beat the cows when they ate the
  harvest. When they decided to eat again, they recalled the painful
  experience. So, they never went there—*Vīṇopama Sutta* (SN35.246; the
  simile of the lute). Similarly, you should think of *kāma ādīnava*
  (the drawbacks of sensuality) and *nekkhamma ānisaṁsa* (the advantages
  of renunciation).**

<!-- -->

- *Satipaṭṭhāna*—the *asubha* (of the *kāmaloka*) and then
  *ānāpānasati*.

<!-- -->

- MN107, 117—How to \[achieve\] *jhāna*—*Gaṇakamoggallāna Sutta* (MN107;
  to Gaṇaka Moggallāna); *Mahācattārīsaka Sutta* (MN117; the great
  forty) + *Saṁyutta* 22 to 55—*Khandha Saṁyutta* (SN22; connected
  discourses on the aggregates) to *Veḷudvāravagga (*SN55).

- Marvels of the ocean—*Pahārāda Sutta (AN8.19; to Pahārāda)*. How to be
  a true representative of the Buddha, Dhamma and Saṅgha.

- You should accommodate yourself to the place and situation to create
  harmony.

- Saṅgha is also a formation. It lasted 2500 years, the Buddha foresaw
  the decline of the *sāsana*. That is why Vinaya was brought as an
  outside formation to support the longevity of the *sāsana*. In the
  beginning the bhikkhus were allowed to live as they wish, as most of
  them used to attain arahantship as soon as they entered \[the order\].

<!-- -->

- 1\. *Itivuttaka* (along the stream what one meets, the dangers)

- <span class="mark">2. *Dhammapada padīpa*, *Dhammapada* (Dhp; verses
  of the Buddha)</span>

- In the night, try to sleep with a fresh, peaceful mind so that one
  will avoid unwholesome impressions throughout the night. And it will
  be highly *puñña* (meritorious action) supporting one’s future.

**30 March 1996**

**3 April 1996**

  according to that nature of things. So the practice should be always
  directed to *sampajañña*, and then to *jhāna* and attaining the path.

**9 April 1996**

  and means to deal with them. For ones who are a little advanced, the
  *Mahāparinibbāna Sutta* (DN16; the Great Passing).

- As time goes on you will get the ability to help others with a more
  pure state of mind, and then you will know what is appropriate for
  them and how to use your thoughts.

- You don’t have to write a big letter, you can just write “best wishes”
  only. \[regarding writing to parents and friends\]

- Ultimately, one must prepare oneself for *kālaṁ agameyya* \[waiting
  for one’s time/death\] . For that, you slowly reduce the feelings,
  making them finer. Because after all, it is nothing but feelings,
  perception, consciousness, *pañcupādānakkhandha* (five aggregates
  subject to clinging).

**12 April 1996**

- You would find nothing from outside that is more helpful than what you

- It is sad to say that the essence of the practice is almost lost

  *kilesas* are mentioned in detail.

- Any situation, keep the inside calm, never be disturbed, this is the
  very practice of the Dhamma.

- The body is not the Arahant, it is unthinkable.

<!-- -->

- We must go in stages, first raise the mind to higher states or stages
  from the grosser level with all its gross defilements, that is the
  realm of devas, in that state there is not this kind of body. But one
  must know that all formations are *anicca*, *dukkha*, *anatta*. So
  while in this human state one can do the highest reflection and
  already transcend the whole situation of forming by attaining the
  path, etc.

- Never let the mind ponder and go back to past events etc.
  <span class="mark">Try to bring it to the present, and that is
  *kamma*</span>[123]<span class="mark">.</span>

- Always from *āmisa* to *nirāmisa sukha*[124]. Never the other way.

  (MN122; the greater discourse on voidness).

  to 55 etc. This is done in stages first fulfilling the noble eightfold

#

# 

# 

# 

# 

# 

# 

# 

# 

# *Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera seated at the monks’ dining hall, Polgasduwa Island Hermitage—the exact place where he first lost consciousness before being assisted by Shanaka (circa mid–late 1990s).*

# 

# 

# 

# **Reflection 07: Final Lessons on Detachment**

Even as illness and age pressed upon him, Ven. Ñāṇavimala remained
unmoved, like a rock standing firm against wind and storm. Whether in
the solitude of forests or the sterility of hospital corridors, his
practice did not concern itself with altering the outer world but with a
steady, inward response to it through reflection and equanimity.

From early on in his monk’s life at Polgasduwa Island Hermitage, this
became evident. As the hermitage’s disciplined character began to
change, attracting a different kind of monastic and lay visitor, many of
the senior monks felt it was no longer conducive to their practice and
departed one by one. Ven. Ñāṇavimala quietly remained.

“For karmic reasons,” he once said, “we receive a particular place and
teacher. To remain there also offers support for the rest.” He held that
leaving should not be done hastily, but in consultation with the Saṅgha.
He stayed for a decade.

When he finally began his wandering life (*cārikā*), it was not from
dissatisfaction, but as a natural unfolding of the path.

After those long years of wandering, his health began to decline. The
toll of *cārikā* and advancing age showed themselves most starkly after
he returned firstly to Siri Vajiraramaya temple and then to Polgasduwa
Hermitage.

There, severe prolonged diarrhea rapidly diminished his weight and
vitality.

Characteristically, he made nothing of his condition, maintaining his
daily practice of receiving alms at the *dāna sālā*[125] and when
special requests were made giving any Dhamma advice or blessings. To
outside eyes, this decline of physical condition was a time of hardship;
but Shanaka sensed something altogether different—a palpable dispassion,
and a detachment from the body that preserved his radiance even as his
strength faded.

Ven. Gnanaloka described one such occasion as a layman at Polgasduwa
where Ven. Ñāṇavimala first lost consciousness:

“On that particular day I was washing items in the *dāna sālā* when
alarmed attendants and monks rushed to tell me of Bhante’s condition. I
found him unresponsive, his head drooping and both hands composedly
clasped in his lap.

Yet remarkably his face retained its moonlike radiance—that glow never
dimmed, creating an unforgettable image. All present were hesitant to
intervene, as he had never before allowed anyone to assist him in such
an intimate manner.

I reached for Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s belt, quickly pulled out the keys, and
ran to his kuti. I found the medicinal honey he kept and ran back with
it. Placing it on his tongue, I remember being struck by the radiance
that still seemed to emanate from his unconscious body. I could not tell
whether it was the honey, passage of time, or something else, but
suddenly he regained consciousness and spoke the following in
<span class="mark">Pāli</span>:

‘*Vayadhammā saṅkhārā <span class="mark">…</span> uppādavayadhammino;
uppajjitvā nirujjhanti, tesaṁ vūpasamo sukho*.  
(All conditioned things are impermanent *<span class="mark">…</span>*
they are of the nature to arise and pass away; having arisen, they
cease. Relief from them is pleasant.)’

‘*Sabbe saṅkhārā aniccā ti yadā paññāya passati, atha nibbindati dukkhe
esa maggo visuddhiyā*.  
(When one perceives with wisdom that all conditioned things are
impermanent, then one turns away from suffering. This is the path of
purification.)’

Then he said, ‘This foul body, just lift this up.’

At that moment, I realized that his consciousness had faded while
reflecting on these verses, and it was those same reflections that
brought him back. This was my first time physically assisting him. It
was a moment of mixed emotions—on one hand bearing witness to the
helpless nature of the human body of someone so remarkable, but on the
other a most joyous experience at the rare privilege of being by his
side during this time. It was both the saddest and happiest day of my
life.”

Doctors would periodically come and check up on Ven. Ñāṇavimala at the
hermitage, and would often leave with sense of admiration. Once, a new
doctor came to the hermitage to perform a medical check-up. Unfamiliar
with Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s character and perhaps overzealous to treat his
declining health, he was met with a simple remark:

“There is no medicine for old age.”

While his mind met old age with such clarity, his body’s decline soon
became undeniable. Worn from years of austere wandering, now demanded a
level of care he had long resisted. Yet even here his practice revealed
itself—not as resistance to aging and sickness, but as a direct meeting
of them. His independence was never outwardly; it was an inner resolve.

In line with his austerity, he was admitted to a government hospital and
whilst there he remained the same: inwardly composed, outwardly simple.
His preference for public over private care was not incidental; it
reflected his lifelong consistency. For one who had rejected comforts, a
hospital ward was no different from a kuti or a forest glade—it was
merely another setting for practice.

Shanaka, who now became one of his close attendants, visited regularly,
and Ven. Ñāṇavimala would sense his presence before even seeing him.
Recognising the sound of his footsteps in the corridor, he would softly
call out, “Shanaka, Shanaka,” to Venerable
Mettavi<span class="mark">hārī</span>, who was the other close attendant
there.

Ven. Gnanaloka recalls those hospital days vividly:

“I possessed great faith but little experience in caring for anyone. Yet
I remained present, offering a kind of protection that I could.”

Even during these times, Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s independence was apparent. He
had warned Shanaka: "Don't be in the hands of the devotees." He lived by
this principle. After being hospitalized he accepted assistance, but
never gave favor to any attendant. More often, seeing there was another
attendant available, he would instruct Shanaka to return to his
practice. The lesson was subtle but unmistakable: service too must not
become a form of clinging.

Ven. Gnanaloka later reflected: *“*There was no difference between the
first day I met him and the last day I met him.*”* Outwardly he could be
close and kind, but inwardly he maintained a steady dispassion, balanced
with a universal compassion that never leaned into intimacy.

The same steady discipline carried into his mindfulness. Even within
hospital walls, he seemed to erase the distinction between day and
night. Ven. Gnanaloka recalls how at 2 a.<span class="mark">m,</span> he
would engage in walking meditation in the hospital corridors, quietly
pacing every two hours. Even when lying down, there seemed to be a
watchful awareness that carried with him.

One day Shanaka was doing walking meditation on the balcony of the
hospital ward, as Ven. Ñāṇavimala quietly watched. When Shanaka
returned, Ven. Ñāṇavimala asked him:

“Did you meet your friend… your thoughts?”

Then, after a pause, he added with a soft smile,

“Don’t overdo it. Take some rest.”

It was how he spoke of wandering thoughts, those passing visitors of the
mind. He had once advised Shanaka not to forcibly chase them away, but
to meet them gently as if they were a guest, and let them pass on their
own. Then the underlying tendency to aversion finds no ground to grow.

What stayed with Shanaka were moments like these—the peculiar way of
teaching hidden in simple exchanges.

As his teacher’s health declined, those small exchanges took on a deeper
light. Ven Ñāṇavimala later needed to stay at Siri Vajiraramaya temple
as it was easier to access medicines and appropriate care. During this
period, a striking incident left a lasting imprint on Ven. Gnanaloka.

He recalls entering Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s room and offering to help adjust
his body in bed. He replied with an uncharacteristically abrupt “No!”

Then, after a pause, he asked, “Did anything touch you?”

Shanaka was confused until he realized that stool had involuntarily
passed from Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s frail body. The moment this was
understood, Ven Ñāṇavimala said with striking clarity:

“Whether it’s inside or outside, it doesn’t make a difference.”

The body, the Buddha taught, is just elements (*dhātu*)—solidity,
fluidity, heat, and motion. Inside, outside. All the same. Nothing to
claim as ‘mine.’ Just nature unfolding.

Ven. Gnanaloka recalls “he was looking at his body with that reality,
really meaning it. You could feel it.” At first, Ven. Ñāṇavimala
objected, stating he would wait for the other attendants. But when
Shanaka remained quietly beside him, he consented.

What stood out through the years was not hardship, but the steadiness of
a life wholly given to the Dhamma. The Dhamma was his refuge—its truth
held firm where pain could not reach.

Since the first day he got a chance to physically assist Ven. Ñāṇavimala
at Polgasduwa, a thought remained with Ven. Gnanaloka: a single day of
his teacher’s existence on earth offered more worth than the entirety of
our lifetimes, for his very presence was a rare light that shone upon
both the Buddha’s Dispensation (*sāsana*) and the world. How fortunate
to encounter one who walked the spiritual path with uncompromising focus
and complete dedication. While many pursue this spiritual journey with
admirable knowledge and sincerity, Ven. Ñāṇavimala stood apart—a life
that was not an interpretation of the Dhamma, but its living expression.

**Shanaka’s draft letter to his family**

*(date unknown; likely written during the period described in Reflection
07).*[126]

“My reverence to both of you and love to Malli \[younger brother\].

I received both postcards. Reverend Ñānavimala fell extremely sick. He
showed extreme weakness of body in the last few days, but in spite of
all this he manages everything on his own. One thing is that I had the
privilege of supplying him with fruit juices. There is much to narrate
about these past few days. I would leave that for the future.

As to my classes, please do not worry. I was planning to register at
Aquinas Borella \[a popular private higher education institution\], as
the courses there start late. CBS \[another higher education
institution\] of course starts very early. Reverend Ñāṇavimala might be
admitted at any time to the Bhikkhu Ward \[National Hospital\]. If that
would take place in the near future. I would return with him. I must
stress that all this weakness and sickness, that I mentioned is
concerning his body only but that of his mind I’m sure I haven’t to
mention. He is surely a living example of this Aryan way. You must not
have any worries about me. Already I feel a great confidence as to the
success of my actual intentions in my heart.”

*Handwritten notes by Shanka Dharmakeerti, capturing teachings and
reflections from his time with Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera (1996 – 1998).*

# *Bone relics of Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera, later enshrined in memorial stūpas at Polgasduwa, Parappaduwa, and Pannapitiya; some were also offered to close lay disciples. His final breath was described as peaceful and fully controlled—the stillness of one who had truly seen the Dhamma.*
