# 

# 

# 

# 

# **Reflection 08: Breath, Consciousness, and the Final Moment**

There were rare moments when Ven. Ñāṇavimala sensed that the conditions
were right—when Shanaka’s mind was settled and receptive—that he would
offer that he would offer teachings on the deepest truths: the
conditioned nature of consciousness. It was during one of these quiet
intervals, in the cool shade beside the library at Siri Vajiraramaya
temple, that he posed a question to Shanaka:

In the final moments, at physical death, is the arahant[135] able to
keep the attention on the breath or does it go elsewhere?

This was no idle question. Behind it lay the profound mechanics of
consciousness (*viññāṇa*) and the nature of rebirth.

In the Buddha’s teaching, this question is particularly significant
because it touches on the ultimate goal of the path: the complete end of
suffering (*dukkha*). This isn’t just everyday unhappiness, but the
deep, underlying stress that exists in every life, whether seemingly
fortunate or not—the stress of being subject to loss, the pain of
unfulfilled desire, sickness, aging, and death.

Ending rebirth is the only final solution, because even a life in a
peaceful heavenly realm is temporary. As Ven. Ñāṇavimala had once
remarked:

“Even the Brahma world[136] is suffering. One may re-arise even in the
hell realms.”

Therefore, to break this chain for good, one must see it clearly. This
means understanding what binds the process of consciousness, turning it
into the crucial link that connects one life to the next.

To appreciate the weight of Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s question, one must first
understand consciousness within the context of the Dhamma. There is the
body composed of flesh and bone, but there is a difference between a
body with consciousness and one without. Without consciousness, it is
merely a physical object. With consciousness, there is knowing, tied to
one of the six sense bases: eye, ear, nose, tongue, body, or mind.

Take the ear faculty: there is the ear and there are sound waves. Yet
consciousness does not necessarily arise. Only when there is the ear and
sound is brought into attention do the conditions align for
ear-consciousness to arise. When it arises, this consciousness is a
‘knowing-ness’ associated with that particular sense faculty. It is not
an independent entity or ‘soul’ existing on its own. The meeting of the
three: ear, sound, and ear-consciousness is ‘contact’ (*phassa*). From
contact, feeling (*vedanā*) arises: pleasant, unpleasant, or neutral.
Alongside feeling, perception (*saññā*) may arise, recognizing and
designating what is present—for example, ‘this is a sound’ or ‘this is a
table.’ Perception also judges the feeling: for example, “this is
pleasant,” “this is unpleasant.” Such judging is itself a form of
perception. From here, pleasant feeling inclines toward desire and
greed, unpleasant toward aversion and hatred, and neutral toward
delusion.

*Saṅkhāra*[137] is the Buddha’s term for these reaction processes, and
they are born from ignorance (*avijjā*). Ignorance, here, is the very
blindness to these processes: not knowing their impermanent and
suffering nature, their cause, their cessation, or the path to their
end. A mind veiled by this ignorance rushes toward these burning
processes, continually getting hurt, drawn to them, as Ven. Ñāṇavimala
warned, “like the moths who fly into the flames.” These processes
constitute the accumulation of *kamma*—willed actions—which manifest
through mind, speech, and body as *mano-kamma, vacī-kamma*, and
*kāya-kamma*. Moment by moment, across the six sense faculties, this
continuously drives the ongoing accumulation of *kamma* and the
consequences they produce.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala taught that at the last moment of death, an object
appears according to one’s accumulated *kamma*, becoming a condition for
consciousness to re-arise. He would illustrate this by giving the
example of a virtuous businessman whose mind in this life had been
conditioned by business—whose thoughts habitually turned to trade and
transactions. Those habitual desires become the *kamma* that fuel the
final-moment object—the condition for consciousness to re-arise. Then
that person may be reborn in a life with favorable conditions for doing
business, such as being born to parents who are supportive of business.
Particular conditions follow from how one occupies the present moment,
he would say.

But what of the arahant—the one who has fully uprooted greed, hate and
delusion, without traces of craving and *kamma*?

Ven. Ñāṇavimala explained that the arahant, in that final moment, can
maintain attention solely on the breath without attachment. The breath
is simply known as it is—nothing more. Because there is no *kamma*,
there is no object - a thought - to distract attention. Because there is
no craving one doesn’t get distracted. Even the breath itself is not
attached to. With conditions absent for consciousness to arise, no
rebirth occurs. Ven. Gnanaloka recalls how powerfully his teacher’s
words resonated: “As Bhante described it, I remember imagining it
happening to me and a great peace came over my mind just from the
reflection.” “It’s deep,” Ven. Ñāṇavimala would warn Shanaka.

This explanation was given during his first self retreat with Ven
Ñāṇavimala and was the first time he came across the *Khandha Vagga* and
*Saḷāyatana Vagga*. "I was surprised that so many *Suttas* are there by
the Buddha and I became quite absorbed with it" he recalls. He
discovered in their pages the confirmation—and the unfolding meaning—of
his teacher’s words.

These texts contain the Buddha’s core analysis of experience: the
*Khandha Vagga* deconstructs our sense of ‘self’ into five aggregates
(*pañcupādānakkhandhā*), while the *Saḷāyatana Vagga* is an extension of
*Khandha Vagga* to explain experience of the world through our six
senses. As Ven. Gnanaloka reflects on these very aggregates, “Craving
acts like a glue—it binds consciousness to form[138], feeling,
perception and volitional formations, but when that glue is removed by
wisdom, the process cannot exist.”

This insight was not just philosophical. It had a practical and
liberating result.

Even modern neuroscience, with all its instruments, still struggles to
define consciousness. It looks to the brain to trace how neural
processes might give rise to consciousness. But the ancient teachings
had already revealed that consciousness does not arise randomly, nor
from matter alone, but due to specific causes—and that these causes
could be transcended.

Ven. Gnanaloka further related his understanding to the one who is one
stage below final liberation - the anāgāmi[139]. In contrast to the
arahant, the anāgāmi has cut off the lower fetters but may still harbor
subtle lust, conceit, restlessness, and ignorance. Even lacking gross
desire for material existence, the anāgāmi retains subtle attachment as
well as *kamma* towards the higher formless existences. This becomes the
object that conditions the consciousness to move on, which is why the
anāgāmi is reborn in a higher, formless Brahma plane; the subtle
attachment has not been completely uprooted, unlike the arahant. Thus,
at the moment of physical death, attention is not able to remain
exclusively on the breath but gravitates to the object of subtle
attachment, which is that of the equanimous *samādhi*.

Before the insights on consciousness and liberation began to unfold, his
teacher had introduced him to the *Suttas* even from their very first
meetings.

In one of these early encounters, when Shanaka described the experience
of noticing arising and passing away, Ven. Ñāṇavimala directed him
toward the *Satipaṭṭhāna* *Sutta*. The *Satipaṭṭhāna Sutta*, which lays
out the four foundations of mindfulness—of body, feeling, mind, and
Dhammas—holds a central place in Buddhist practice today. As passed down
to him by his teacher, Ven. Gnanaloka clarifies that the application of
real *Satipaṭṭhāna* happens when one is free from the five hindrances
(*nīvaraṇas* - sensual desire, ill-will, sloth and torpor, restlessness
and remorse, and doubt)*.*

He explains that this is not a matter of forceful suppression, but of
cultivating the opposite qualities of the mind - the five *indriyas*
(faith, energy, mindfulness, concentration and wisdom). Ven. Ñāṇavimala
once commented to Shanaka that when he was finding reasons for needing
to leave the Island Hermitage, it was because these five *indriyas* were
not properly cultivated in a balanced way, and if they were he would
just stay on. Ven. Ñāṇavimala further remarked that monks who base their
practice more solely on the Vinaya (monks code of conduct), without the
five *indiryas* being properly cultivated in a balanced way, this
becomes a reason why they disrobe.

Wisdom is a critical component in the *indriyas*. Wise reflection
(*yoniso manasikāra*) is fundamental to this, belonging to
*Dhammānupassanā*, the contemplation of Dhammas, within the framework of
*Satipaṭṭhāna* practice. Yet, many discourses the Buddha delivered, such
as those in the *Khanda Vagga* and *Saḷāyatana Vagga* also serve to
activate this quality of contemplation. To see the arising and passing
of the five aggregates and recognize their impermanent nature. Ven.
Gnanaloka elaborates, “This insight goes beyond simply noticing that
something appears and later disappears, or that a feeling arises and
subsides. This is just an immediate noticing of impermanence. On a
subtle intuitive level, however, one sees that the very causes and
conditions that create these phenomena are themselves impermanent. In
this deeper insight, one can naturally clearly see the impermanence
(*anicca*) nature.” Since *Dhammānupassanā* is present in all the
foundations of mindfulness, Ven. Ñāṇavimala encouraged systematic
reading of various *Suttas*, allowing deeper understandings to emerge
naturally. This broader approach reveals the full richness of the
teaching, rather than focusing narrowly on one aspect such as the body.

Ven. Gnanaloka remembers an incident from his layman days when the
*Suttas* revealed their practical power in a most personal way.

One day Ven. Ñāṇavimala looked at Shanaka and, quite unexpectedly,
remarked, “My thoughts went to your cousin.” Since he didn’t usually
comment on worldly affairs, the remark struck Shanaka as unusual. His
cousin, an Air Force pilot was only a year apart in age to Shanaka and
they had grown up together more like brothers than cousins. As children
they shared toys, and as young men his cousin had confided in Shanaka.
The bond was deep, stronger even than the one he shared with his own
brother. After Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s remark, the following day a telegram
letter arrived from home calling him to come back urgently to Colombo,
without explanation. When he arrived, he found the house full of
grieving relatives— his cousin had gone missing in action and was
presumed dead.

Rather than react immediately, he quietly made his way through the crowd
to his room at the back, his mother following after him, anticipating
his sorrow. Sitting down, he turned his mind inward to the reflections
presented in *Saḷāyatana* discourses: ‘*The ear is impermanent, sounds
are impermanent, ear-consciousness is impermanent; what is impermanent
is suffering and thus non-self*.’ Hearing the distressing news, he saw
it was beyond his control—merely an arisen sound wave reaching his ear.
He began to witness his ear faculty as if from a distance as a separate
thing, and sound as a separate external occurrence, both momentary and
conditioned. The sense of “*this belongs to me, this I am, this is
myself*” then quieted. In clearly seeing this as an impersonal process,
sadness did not take hold. The mind did not shut the experience out but
saw its true dependent and impersonal nature. His mother, deeply moved
by his unshaken calm, worshipped him. Later, the full, tragic story came
to light: his cousin’s plane had been shot down over the northern war
zones[140] by LTTE[141] rebels.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala would say, “arahants see everything from a distance.”
What Shanaka was only beginning to glimpse was a form of this detached
awareness—observing experiences through this framework of causes and
conditions—an approach that reveals how the *Suttas*, when read with the
right spirit, are not just concepts but can give a direct experience of
these truths.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s advice was that “one must not overload with
intellectual grasping of the *Suttas*”. Neither rushing the reading as
too simple nor over intellectualizing them as academia or abstract
philosophy—both extremes block what the *Suttas* are meant to reveal.
Each *Sutta* provides a means for reflection (*kammaṭṭhāna*), by keeping
the mind steady on the reflection, without going too far into wanderings
of the past or future. Ven. Gnanaloka offers simple guidance: “Read as
if the Buddha were speaking directly to you. Cultivate a childlike
openness. For this to happen, one can choose to cultivate *samādhi*
separately, bringing the mind to the present moment and then read the
*Suttas*. Don’t aim for some imagined ‘*magga-phala*’ experience. Let
the insights unfold by themselves.” Ven. Ñāṇavimala taught the *Suttas*
serve as “signposts”: practical guides pointing the way forward on the
Dhamma path.

Ven. Gnanaloka observed that when yogis got a powerful insight from
contemplating the *Suttas*—like seeing that “this is not mine, not I”
they would sometimes cling to it. “Keep reading,” he would say, “don’t
stop there—because that’s the very reason it happened in the first
place. Otherwise, they may get stuck in that or just trying to get that
equanimous *samādhi* feeling again”. He adds that “it happened to me
too”.

This practice of returning to the teachings is essential. The *Suttas*
are not meant for a single, superficial encounter; they are living
instructions. In the Buddha’s time, it was through this very process of
sustained, repeated reflection that disciples awakened to the Dhamma,
often attaining final liberation.

Such reflection, done with a receptive and investigative mind, could
lead to subtle forms of support. He remembered on one occasion whilst
Ven. Ñāṇavimala was speaking about impermanence, Shanaka found himself
deeply focused on that, silently catching that insight himself. At that
moment, Ven. Ñāṇavimala would say, “just wait like that,” watching and
simply waiting as if a form of mind-to-mind communication or internal
exercise was taking place.

That subtle influence, he came to understand, was not confined by
distance or space.

At another time, while staying in the Himalayas, reading and reflecting
quietly in his kuti, Ven. Gnanaloka felt an influence—a blessing
perhaps—from Ven. Ñāṇavimala, reaching him across the great distance
supporting that insightful contemplation. Reflecting on these
experiences, Ven. Gnanaloka felt they were not a coincidence, but a kind
of connection on a ‘Dhamma-plane’. He reminds fellow practitioners
“don’t think you’re alone”, affirming Ven Ñāṇavimala’s advice said to
him—if the qualities are aligned, support comes, even from those beings
afar but on the same Dhamma-plane. Ven. Ñāṇavimala passed away not long
after this experience, while Ven. Gnanaloka was still practicing in the
Himalayas.

Ven. Upasama, another close attendant disciple of Ven. Ñāṇavimala,
witnessed Ven Ñāṇavimala during these last days take his final breath,
describing it as being taken “peacefully… in (a) fully controlled
manner!”[142] In his telling, the moment spoke for itself.

Ultimately, it all pointed to one truth. The final breath, the last
moment of attention, the condition that determines whether consciousness
re-arises or ceases—it all depends on how one has perceived the Dhamma.
Whether craving still binds, or whether wisdom has finally cut the
thread.

*Handwritten notes by Ven. Bambalapitiye Gnanaloka Thero, capturing
teachings and reflections from his time with Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera
(2000).*

**- Chapter 09 -**

**“Give A Finger To Māra,**

**He Takes A Hand”**

**(Jan 2000 – May 2000)**

\[Shanaka Dharmakeerti ordained as Venerable Bambalapitiye Gnanaloka on
19th December 1999 these Notes were written Post Ordination\]

**11 January 2000**

- Make sure the main practice is well established concentrating on the
  necessary task. Later when the need or the necessity is felt you may
  add the required knowledge for that stage. (Avoid being a scholar or a
  meditator without *paññā*).

<!-- -->

- Establish in *Mettā* *Bhāvanā* as told in *Aṅguttara—Mettā Sutta*
  (AN8.1; loving-kindness) and then *Khanda* and *Saḷāyatana*
  *Saṁyutta—Khandha Saṁyutta* (SN22; connected discourses on the
  aggregates); *Saḷāyatana Saṁyutta* (SN35; connected discourses on the
  six sense bases)

- Slowly start in the nearby area *piṇḍapāta* and slowly extend to
  further areas.

- Know the branches of Galduwa and Wathurawila, so you could easily find
  your way to those places when going on *cārikā*.

<!-- -->

- **When you make the determination to live according to the teachings
  of the Buddha, that is *kamma*. Going on *cārikā* is very hard, you
  will fall sick. But then only there is experience of *dukkha* in
  *Kāmaloka* (the sensual realm).**

<!-- -->

- Otherwise if you get used to the place and all its comforts, being
  looked after by the *dāyakas*. It is dangerous. Think of the first 60
  arahants, how they died and where they passed away.[143]

- First you will have to go with the teacher. Progress in the Dhamma is
  not at once, you must allow time.

- When in the jungle, don’t get frightened or excited when faced with
  animals because they perceive the state of mind. Recite the *Khandha
  Paritta*—*Ahirāja Sutta* (AN4.67; snakes) daily and act with *mettā*
  to these beings.

- **If the monk you are under (teacher’s senior pupil) does not have an
  understanding of the actual practice, then you might have to part from
  him. This must not be done at once. When such things come, you must
  call for a Saṅgha meeting. Because it is not by chance you get this
  and that one as your *upajjhāya* or *ācariya*. There is a certain
  *kamma* behind it.**

**23 January 2000**

- When you are striving and living according to the Dhamma, merit is
  made and devas in the higher world will give protection as they like a
  person who can give merit. They will follow, don’t forget that.

- Though things have changed outside, if you are determined to live
  according to the Dhamma, that will not be in vain.

- Though things have changed in Ceylon from outside, still in the hearts
  of the people the true idea of the monks is not lost.[144]

**4 May 2000**

- When you give a finger to Māra, he takes a hand. So be careful of this
  *kāmasukha (sensual pleasure)*. If some bhikkhus continue in this way,
  in the course of time, as generally people look for a leader, they
  would follow[145].

> *Ekāsanaṁ ekāseyyaṁ, eko caraṁ atandito; eko damayam attānaṁ, vanante
> ramito siyā*
>
> He who sits alone, lies down alone, walks alone, in diligent practice,
> and alone tames himself should find delight in living in the forest.
>
> (Dhp305)

# 

> *Ven. Bambalapitiye Gnanaloka and Ven. Pannipitiye Upasama attending
> to Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera at Parappaduwa Island Hermitage—though
> his body had grown frail, his heart radiated vast compassion; each
> morning, his blessings flowed outward in waves of mettā embracing all
> beings (circa 2000s).*

# **Reflection 09: In the Footsteps of a Master**

When Venerable Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera lay bedridden in his final years at
Parappaduwa Island Hermitage, his attendants would meet him each morning
for a blessing before their alms round. As Ven. Gnanaloka recalls, “He
would put us in a *mettā* state… In his bed, you would know that he’s
having a kind of vision. You could see that he is seeing something.
That’s very, very clear.”

These visions shifted from day to day, and his blessings became a vast
field of loving-kindness (*mettā*). He would encourage his attendants to
join him in spreading *mettā*—beginning with the local villagers and
gradually extending to all beings. He also began to include spreading it
to the “youth who have gone astray in the North”. His usual mention was
to “the fisherman who would serve” referring to the daily offering they
would get from them in their alms round.

For three consecutive days, however, a curious new phrase entered the
blessing. After the usual verses, he would say, “Even those who are
misguided in the North… they will also serve.”

The repetition of this final phrase was strange, and to Ven. Gnanaloka,
it seemed misplaced, almost puzzling—perhaps a moment of confusion
brought on by age.

The North was the heartland of the civil war, where Buddhist monks were
often singled out by the LTTE for their saffron robes. There are records
of monks being dragged from buses and shot[146], temples bombed[147],
and rural communities living in fear of sudden massacres[148]. It was
also the very region where Shanaka’s own cousin, an Air Force pilot, had
been killed in the conflict.

But two years later, Ven. Gnanaloka, found himself deep in rebel-held
territory, standing barefoot as a senior LTTE leader, a member of the
Liberation Tigers of Tamil Eelam, the main separatist group in Sri
Lanka’s civil war, removed his cap and gently placed a handful of
*vadai* (savoury fried dumplings) into his alms bowl.

It had all begun at Parappaduwa, where Ven. Gnanaloka was serving his
teacher. Inspired by the stories of Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s solitary
wanderings, he felt moved to take up the ancient practice of *cārikā*
himself. It was a path of radical uncertainty, a practice of meeting
each moment without a plan. This was an approach Ven. Ñāṇavimala
embodied completely. During those days, when Ven. Ñāṇavimala was asked
where he was going, his reply was simple: “I haven’t decided yet. I’ll
decide when I get to the corner.”

It was this spirit of profound, present-moment freedom that so inspired
the young disciple.

When Ven. Gnanaloka asked his teacher for permission, Ven. Ñāṇavimala
gave him a resolute blessing: “Don’t worry. Your guardian *devas*[149]
are behind you.” That single line, spoken with such quiet certainty, was
more than a promise; it was a reflection of the Dhamma’s protective
power, and it was enough to carry him across hundreds of miles.

His teacher had often spoken of a principle woven through the ascetic
life: that the pure virtue of a wandering monk naturally attracts the
support of unseen beings, or *devatās*. “Those who are practising,” Ven.
Ñāṇavimala would say, “*devatās* will follow”—drawn to virtue to gain
merit. He spoke from decades of experience. There were times when a
particular food would come to mind, and then appear before him in ways
that seemed beyond coincidence. Once, arriving unannounced in a remote
village near Minneriya National Park where food was scarce and no one
could have known he was coming, an elderly woman offered him a carefully
prepared meal, she said, a *devatā* appeared in her evening prayers,
instructing her that a monk would arrive the next morning[150].

Yet Ven. Ñāṇavimala would always add a caution: wishes fulfilled in such
ways could stir further desire. A practising monk must guard his mind
with the utmost care.

And so Ven. Gnanaloka departed. Ven. Gnanaloka had no itinerary. Just a
direction—north—a rudimentary map, and the inner compass of *sati*
(mindfulness). After seeking the blessing of his preceptor[151], Madihe
Paññāsīha Mahā Nāyaka Thero, who gently told him “Do it without falling
ill. This is the time to try such things,” he began walking. He began
the journey on April 9th, 2003, and it would take him a month to reach
the sacred northern islet of Nāgadīpa. To reach his destination,
however, was no simple task. While Nāgadīpa and the Jaffna peninsula
were under government control at the time, he first had to pass through
vast areas of the Vanni region, which were still administered by the
LTTE. In total, the journey there and back would cover over 1,200
kilometers on foot, much of it through this rebel-held territory.

He would walk 15 kilometers in the early morning and another 15 in the
late afternoon. With *Sati* and *Samādhi* as his companions, the body
did not complain.

When he reached Anurādhapura, he rested for a week, asking a few lorry
drivers about the conditions ahead. He knew that entering
rebel-controlled territory would mean stepping into uncertainty, perhaps
even death. But as he later shared, “I didn’t have an army to protect
me—only the teachings of the Buddha, *mettā*, and the understanding that
this body is not mine.”

He carried with him the contemplation from the *Khandha Vagga*—the
teaching on the five aggregates of clinging. Form, feeling, perception,
volitional formations, consciousness—that is all that takes place. And
he saw those he would meet the same way: not soldiers or rebels, but
aggregates arising and passing away, like his own. Just nature meeting
nature.

He would recall: “The *khandhas* are universal, this craving is
universal, whoever decreases this craving through wisdom—with that,
dispassion will come, and they can be liberated. I feel it is a
potential everybody has. In this way I had a lot of *mettā.*”

Before crossing the last government military checkpoint, he sought out a
monk to purify his *sīla*[152] and prepare for the possibility of death.
Thankfully, a forest monk from the Waturavila tradition, fluent in
Tamil, helped him and even went to the extent of preparing a note for
alms rounds and taught him the sacred Tamil phrases: “*Subam, śānti,
samadānam*” (auspiciousness, peace, generosity).

The soldiers at the final government outpost tried to stop him[153]. It
was a plea, not an order. “Is there active conflict?” he asked. “No,”
they replied. And with that, the Monastic Rules permitted his
passage.[154]

On the other side, he was met not with bullets, but with kindness. A
senior leader, who spoke English, arranged a travel pass, offered food,
and even shielded him from publicity. Others came—militants, villagers,
children—drawn by the sight of a lone monk walking barefoot in silence.
Their questions were often simple. “*Sapada*?”—have you eaten?

He moved through areas without human habitation, across forests and dry
plains, where the only shelter was the Dhamma. When militants approached
him with suspicion, a kind stranger would often appear at just the right
moment, offering reassurance or safe passage.

A month into his journey, he reached the northern islet of Nāgadīpa and
prepared to begin the long walk back south. While staying in a makeshift
shelter built by sailors near the Dambakolapatuna Navy Camp, his mind
turned to his cousin—the Air Force pilot whose plane had gone down
somewhere in these northern waters in 1995. When the camp commander
arrived with the daily meal, Ven. Gnanaloka asked if he happened to know
anything about the crash.

The commander’s reply was stunning. Just a month earlier, local
fishermen had pulled a large piece of wreckage from their nets—it was
the engine of the lost aircraft. The spot where they found it, deep in
the ocean, was a mere three kilometers from where he now sat.

The path had not only led him through a war-torn land; it had, without
intention, brought him to the place tied to the one Ven. Ñāṇavimala had
once sent blessings to, before word of the passing had ever reached him.
He stayed an extra day. With the support of the fishermen, he went out
in a boat to the exact location and performed a blessing over the
water—a quiet, unplanned resolution.

The journey, it seemed, was a series of such unexpected moments, some of
quiet grace, others of sudden danger.

Once, a motorcycle came hurtling toward him, its rider intent on
intimidation. It veered away only at the final instant. Later, with a
soft smile, Ven. Gnanaloka said, “I was ready to die. It seemed he
wasn’t.”

There was another moment, deeper into rebel territory, when LTTE’s Chief
of Police Balasingham Nadesan arrived, inspected him, and then asked a
question that seemed to come from somewhere deeper than protocol: “Will
we ever get peace?”

Ven. Gnanaloka replied, “Peace is inside, not outside.”

“I never saw them as terrorists,” he would later say. “I felt as if I
were meeting my own children.”

This feeling was never clearer than in an encounter at Kilinochchi—a
place then known as the most heavily secured region in the North, the
heart of LTTE control. Some had quietly warned him, “Not to pass through
that and go somehow another way. If you do, do not linger there.”

Small children would peek from behind trees as he passed, their eyes
wide with curiosity at this strange sight—a monk walking alone,
barefoot, unafraid. Was he mad? How could he not be frightened? The
villagers watched too—they seemed to feel it. He had no fear.

At the checkpoint, a young cadre at a security checkpoint tasked with a
body search approached, his eyes filled with a conflict between his duty
and his devotion. He looked at Ven. Gnanaloka, and told him “Seniors are
watching me, Swamī, so I need to make physical contact with you.” He
then closed his eyes to perform the search. As he did, Ven. Gnanaloka
recalls, “I have never seen so much devotion in anyone, Sinhalese or
otherwise.”[155]

At another checkpoint when a militant asked “Are you not afraid? We are
LTTE”, his answer was quiet and unshaken: “As long as I have this
(meditative) power, I’m not afraid.”

As Ven. Gnanaloka turned south to leave, LTTE cadres followed on
motorbikes, eager to speak with him one last time. “We have no conflict
with your thoughts,” they told him.

But one scene remains etched in memory. In a village along the way back,
families had gathered on either side of the road. Their voices rose
together: “Why are we suffering, Sadhu? Why are we suffering?”

There were no words he could give. Only his presence, only *mettā*.

Decades earlier, Ven. Ñāṇavimala had taken up the practice of *cārikā*
for more than twenty-five years. When asked why his teacher had walked
for so many years, Ven. Gnanaloka would remember how he would talk about
it: “You are faced with the real truth of the *kāma loka*, then you can
see the suffering of it—and you get that real feeling of wanting to come
out of it.”

*Kāma loka*: the world as filtered through our six senses. Not just
pleasure, but all of it—the clinging, the loss, the endless wanting.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala had traversed the island south to north, east to west
three times over—walking into every province, every terrain, every kind
of human struggle.

Every kind of uncertainty.

During the Tamil insurgency of the early 1970s, Ven. Ñāṇavimala had been
on *cārikā* when he was surrounded by armed rebels who suspected him of
being a spy. They dragged him into the jungle, threatening to kill him.
Ven. Ñāṇavimala asked for a few moments to prepare his mind. Sitting on
a rock, he closed his eyes and entered deep meditation. When he was
ready, he simply raised one arm—his signal to be shot.

But no shot came. When he opened his eyes, the forest was empty. His
would-be executioners had vanished. He rose and continued walking.

“*Dhammo have rakkhati Dhammacārī*”—the Dhamma protects those who live
by the Dhamma.

As Ven. Gnanaloka reflects now, it is not his own courage or clarity
that carried him through, but the example of a teacher who had walked
that path for decades—not for what he would gain, but for what he could
give. A blessing offered wherever he walked.

For Ven. Gnanaloka, the journey fulfilled a deep aspiration. Yet he came
to see that physical extremes do not reveal the Dhamma. For that, one
needs meditative seclusion with the Buddha’s words.

His teacher had been clear: *cārikā* was not for everyone. It depended
on one’s *kamma*. You don’t walk to find the Dhamma. You walk because
you’ve found it.

But even then, one must remain unbound—even from the practice of
*cārikā* itself. The essence was freedom, not the form.

This journey, then, was never his alone. It was part of a lineage—a
living echo of one who said little, lived simply, and yet transformed
lives, one silent act at a time.

*Handwritten notes by Ven. Bambalapitiye Gnanaloka Thero, capturing
teachings and reflections from his time with Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera
(2001).*

**- Chapter 10 -**

**“Find A Way To Bring Many To This Path”**

**(July 2001)**

**14 July 2001**

> \- \[*Personal Note:*\] All blessings for creating the opportunity for
> many to follow the Noble path. (Regarding a separate *sīmā* and the
> safeguarding of purity of the Saṅgha).[156]
>
> \- To do everything with the advice, blessings and approval of Mahā
> Nāyaka Thero.
>
> \- Create separate *cārikā* bhikkhus.[157]
>
> \- Many will get an impression in the future from what you do in the
> morning (*piṇḍapāta*) and some will want to join.
>
> \- As the *Kasībhāradvaja Sutta* (SN 7.11; with Bhāradvāja the
> farmer), this applies especially to you, when you happen to discourse
> to others you must discourse to them from their framework or language,
> your idea or Dhamma ideas it will create an impression.
>
> \- You must find a way to bring many to this path.
>
> \- Create separate *cārikā* *piṇḍapāta* bhikkhus.
>
> \- (Bhikkhus in mind) recruit young ones from the age 20 who are
> especially coming from good families, who can make determination on
> the essential goal.

# 

*Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera on piṇḍapāta (alms-round): Revered as the
Mahā Kassapa of the modern era, he was a legendary cārikā bhikkhu who
traversed Sri Lanka barefoot, north to south and east to west, covering
its 65,000 km² multiple times for over 25 years. (Location and date
unknown)*

**Reflection 10: A Seed Foreseen, A Path Unfolding**

In the quiet seclusion of Polgasduwa Island Hermitage, Ven. Ñāṇavimala
Mahāthera spent over a decade steeped in disciplined training under the
guidance of eminent monastic scholars—Ven. Ñāṇatiloka, Ven. Ñāṇapoṇika,
and Ven. Ñāṇamoli. These were monks who didn’t just study the Pāli
Canon—they carried it in their blood. And among the original teachings
of the Buddha, Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s roots of renunciation took hold deeply.

But the Island Hermitage was only a beginning. After eleven years, Ven.
Ñāṇavimala stepped beyond the boundaries of monastery walls and began a
life of ascetic wandering—*cārikā*—wandering with no fixed abode, no
possessions, and no dependence on even the smallest comfort. For more
than twenty-five years, he walked the length and breadth of Sri Lanka
barefoot, moving silently through village and forest, without agenda or
proclamation. Those who encountered him felt something rare—a life lived
so simply that its very presence made the Dhamma visible.

Among the many who quietly watched and listened was the young Shanaka,
he had been drawn to meditation from a young age. Reserved by nature, he
lived as a recluse even before his ordination. He rarely engaged in
small talk, even among fellow monastics, and had no aspiration for
recognition or teaching others.

Yet his teacher saw otherwise.

As a novice, Ven. Gnanaloka was once told, unexpectedly, “Your devotees
are waiting for you.” At the time, it sounded more poetic than
prophetic. Still, over the years, Ven. Ñāṇavimala would quietly prepare
him—not with formal instruction, but with passing remarks that hinted at
something more to come.

“When you happen to discourse to others,” he once said, “you must
discourse to them from their framework or language. Your idea or Dhamma
ideas—it will create an impression.”

Then, in 2001, what had once been subtle suggestions crystallized into a
clear mission from his teacher:  
“You must find a way to bring many to this path. Create separate *cārikā
p<span class="mark">iṇḍa</span>pātika* bhikkhus.”

It was a rare and weighty instruction—especially for one who had spent
his life resisting social contact, avoiding even casual interactions
with other monks. But the words were delivered without pressure, as if
Ven. Ñāṇavimala knew the moment would come by itself.

That moment arrived years later, not in a monastery, but high in the
Himalayas.

In 2015, while attempting to climb Mount Kailash, Ven. Gnanaloka faced
sudden, life-threatening altitude sickness. With no access to oxygen or
proper medical help, he began gasping for breath. As his body weakened,
there was no panic—only a deep internal stillness. He turned inward, not
to fight death, but to meet it with clarity.

And in that space between life and death, a troubling thought arose:  
Had he shared the Dhamma he had received?

This internal conflict was magnified by his private aspiration: he had
vowed to share the Dhamma he had received only after no trace of greed,
hate or delusion was left in his heart.

He realized then that sharing need not wait for perfection. One doesn’t
need to master the entire ocean to offer a cup of water. That
realization—drew him out of his long seclusion.

It wasn’t a desire to teach, but a sense of duty that emerged. Slowly,
almost reluctantly, he began to guide others—offering retreats, sharing
insights, and gently encouraging those ready to walk the path. In time,
he even began to support others in renunciation, nurturing a new
generation of monastics inspired by the same wandering spirit.

Though his own instincts had always leaned toward quietude, it was clear
the turning point had been foreseen—not by him, but by the one who had
trained him.

Looking back now, the threads are unmistakable. From cryptic blessings
to direct instructions, Ven. Ñāṇavimala had not only planted the seed
but somehow glimpsed its flowering. Looking back, he sees the
transformation in his own life as no accident—but as something quietly
shaped and anticipated long before anyone else noticed.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala did not speak of destiny. His way was to place the right
word in the heart of one who was ready and then wait, patiently, as life
brought it to fruition.

*At Polgasduwa Island Hermitage stands a small memorial stūpa,
enshrining the relics of Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera—bearing silent
witness to a life lived beneath the open sky, serene and steadfast on
the ancient path.*

**Epilogue — The Sage**

At the time of the Buddha, many who listened to the Dhamma were moved to
renounce worldly life and follow the ancient path of the sages.

In Venerable Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera, the spirit of the ancient path came
alive again—a life of quiet renunciation, lived beneath the open sky.
His footprints joined those of countless seekers, from the forests of
India to the hermitages of Sri Lanka and beyond. Wherever such lives are
lived, the way of the sage endures—serene, selfless, and unbroken
through time.

The verses below, spoken by the Buddha, encapsulate the essence of that
path—the life of the *muni*, the silent sage. They remind us that the
Dhamma is not a doctrine to be held, but a way to be lived—here and now,
in this very life.

May these words awaken in the reader the same resolve that inspired
countless seekers—to live by the Dhamma, to see clearly, and to walk the
way of the sage.

“Peril stems from intimacy,

dust comes from an abode.

Freedom from abode and intimacy:

that is the sage’s vision.

Having cut down what’s grown,

they wouldn’t replant,

nor would they nurture what’s growing.

That’s who they call a sage wandering alone,

the great seer has seen the state of peace.

Having appraised the fields and measured the seeds,

they wouldn’t nurture them with moisture.

Truly that sage who sees the ending of rebirth

has left speculation behind and is beyond reckoning.

Understanding all the planes of rebirth,

not wanting a single one of them,

Truly that sage freed of greed

need not swim,

for they have reached the far shore.

The champion, all-knower, so very intelligent,

unsullied in the midst of all things,

has given up all, freed in the ending of craving:

that’s who the attentive know as a sage.

Strong in wisdom,

with precepts and observances intact,

serene, loving absorption, mindful,

released from chains, kind, undefiled:

that’s who the attentive know as a sage.

The diligent sage wandering alone,

is unshaken by blame and praise—

like a lion not startled by sounds,

like wind not caught in a net,

like water not sticking to a lotus.

Leader of others, not by others led:

that’s who the attentive know as a sage.

Steady as a post in a bathing-place

when others speak endlessly against them,

freed of greed, with senses stilled:

that’s who the attentive know as a sage.

Steadfast, straight as a shuttle,

horrified by wicked deeds,

discerning the just and the unjust:

that’s who the attentive know as a sage.

Restrained, they do no evil,

young or middle-aged, the sage is self-controlled.

Irreproachable, he does not insult anyone:

that’s who the attentive know as a sage.

When one who lives on charity receives alms,

from the top, the middle, or the leftovers,

they think it unworthy to praise or put down:

that’s who the attentive know as a sage.

The sage lives refraining from sex,

even when young is not tied down,

refraining from indulgence and negligence, freed:

that’s who the attentive know as a sage.

Understanding the world,

the seer of the ultimate goal,

the unaffected one,

having crossed the flood and the ocean,

has cut the ties, unattached and undefiled:

that’s who the attentive know as a sage.

The two are not the same,

far apart in lifestyle and conduct—

the householder providing for a wife,

and the selfless one true to their vows.

The unrestrained householder kills other creatures,

while the restrained sage

always protects living creatures.

As the crested blue-necked peacock

flying through the sky

never approaches the speed of the swan,

so the householder cannot compete

with the mendicant,

the sage meditating secluded in the woods.”

*Muni Sutta* (Snp 1.12; the Sage)

**Appendix**

## **Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s Suggested Readings for the Path towards Enlightenment**

The *Suttas* given below form a suggested two-week self-retreat
curriculum based on guidance given by Venerable Ñāṇavimala. It is
designed for practitioners who wish to undertake a short, focused period
of solitary practice at home or in a quiet place. Follow the sequence in
order: the early texts build a firm foundation, and later texts support
concentrated insight practice. Use them with restraint — prioritise
direct practice over intellectual study.

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

**I. The Foundation for All Practitioners**

This section contains the essential starting points repeatedly
emphasized by Venerable Ñāṇavimala for establishing a solid foundation
in the Dhamma.

For Right View (*Sammā Diṭṭhi*):

• The Word of the Buddha by Ven. Nyanatiloka Mahāthera: Consistently
recommended as the first and most essential guide.

• *Sammādiṭṭhi Sutta* (MN 9): The Discourse on Right View, which
Venerable Ñāṇavimala advised reading “again and again.”

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

**II. The Direct Path to Nibbāna: A Step-by-Step Guide**

The following is a specific, sequential curriculum that Venerable
Ñāṇavimala gave to Ven. Gnanaloka on February 20, 1996, describing it as
a direct, step-by-step path that was shared to Ven. Gnanaloka is a way
to “lead to Nibbāna.” This precise sequence is preserved here exactly as
it was given. Ven. Gnanaloka himself doesn’t have experience with this
path, but he relates that Ven. Ñāṇavimala had shared this based on
insights he has clearly had and feels it is an important sequence.

Step 1: The Foundation for a Noble Disciple

• *Mahānāma Sutta* (AN 8.25)

Step 2: The Gradual Training of the Mind

• *Gaṇakamoggallāna Sutta* (MN 107) & *Dantabhūmi Sutta* (MN 125)

Step 3: Understanding the Path in Full

• *Mahācattārīsaka Sutta* (MN 117)

Step 4: The Central Importance of Virtuous Conduct

• *Ambalaṭṭhika Rāhulovāda Sutta* (MN 61)

Step 5: The Development of Liberating Insight

• *Khandha Saṁyutta* (SN 22) & *Saḷāyatana Saṁyutta* (SN 35)

Step 6: Broadening the Foundation

• *Devadūta Sutta* (MN 130): The Divine Messengers, also a key text for
contemplating mortality.

• *Sāmaññaphala Sutta* (DN 2): The Fruits of the Homeless Life.

• Further Sequence on Purification: The *Suttas* from MN 7 to MN 10
(*Vattha, Sallekha, Sammādiṭṭhi*, and *Satipaṭṭhāna*) were also noted as
another important sequence to follow.

Step 7: The Final Contemplation

• *Kālaṁ Āgameyya* (Awaiting One’s Time): This refers to the final
practice of meeting sickness and death with wisdom. The key *Suttas* for
this contemplation are:

o *Salla Sutta* (SN 36.6): The Dart of Feeling.

o ***Paṭhamagelañña Sutta*** (SN 36.7) & *Dutiyagelañña Sutta* (SN
36.8): The Discourses to the Sick.

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

**III. Another Approach to Nibbāna: A Sequence on Purification**

As noted, Venerable Ñāṇavimala also taught that the sequence of *Suttas*
from *Majjhima Nikāya* 5 to 10 is another complete path.

• *Anaṅgaṇa Sutta* (MN 5), *Ākaṅkheyya Sutta* (MN 6), *Vattha Sutta* (MN
7), *Sallekha Sutta* (MN 8), *Sammādiṭṭhi Sutta* (MN 9) and
*Mahāsatipaṭṭhānasutta* (MN 10)

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

**IV. The Development of Insight (Vipassanā & Dhammānupassanā)**

With a mind calmed and steadied through concentration, one can then
develop liberating insight by contemplating the true nature of
experience. As highlighted in Reflection 8, this is the heart of the
contemplation of phenomena (*Dhammānupassanā*).

• *Khandha Saṁyutta* (SN 22): The Connected Discourses on the
Aggregates, for deconstructing the sense of “self.” (Includes the *Bhāra
Sutta*, SN 22.22, on the burden of the aggregates).

• *Saḷāyatana Saṁyutta* (SN 35): The Connected Discourses on the Six
Sense Bases, for understanding how all experience arises.

• *Bhaddekarattasutta* (MN 131): The Discourse on One Fine Night, which
provides a powerful practice for focusing on the present moment.

## **Venerable Ñāṇavimala’s Walking Contemplations (*Caṅkama Bhāvanā*)**

On numerous occasions, Venerable Gnanaloka (then Shanaka) accompanied
his teacher from his hut to the monastery dining hall and back. During
these walks, Venerable Ñāṇavimala would often verbalize the sequence of
his *caṅkama* (walking meditation) contemplations. The following
phrases, memorized by Venerable Gnanaloka, offer a rare glimpse into the
structured, moment-to-moment reflection of a master.

The practice unfolds in stages, beginning with arousing faith and
culminating in the direct contemplation of Dependent Origination.

### **1. Brightening the Mind**

The practice begins by cultivating faith and confidence in the Buddha,
reflecting on his boundless qualities, reciting a Pāli Gāthā or
recalling the great practitioners of the past who walked this very path
to liberation.

### **2. Grounding in the Present Moment**

After brightening the mind with faith, one returns to the present by
reflecting on the impermanent nature of all phenomena.

> *“Yadatītaṁ pahīnaṁ taṁ, appattañca anāgataṁ.*
>
> *Paccuppannañca yo dhammaṁ, tattha tattha vipassati”*
>
> *“The past is left behind, The future is not yet reached.*
>
> *But those phenomena that are present, He clearly sees wherever they
> arise”*
>
> \[This verse is extracted from *Bhaddekaratta Sutta* (MN 131; a single
> excellent night)\]

### **3. Contemplating the Body & Cultivating Awareness**

Next, one detaches from the body by mindfully recognizing its nature and
cultivates awareness in every posture.

> *“Kāyo anantādīnavo āvāso,*
>
> *Sabba roga rukkha mūlo”*
>
> *“This body is a dwelling full of endless faults,*
>
> *the root of all disease and suffering.”*
>
> \[Not a direct quote; likely inspired by *Girimānanda Sutta* (AN60)
> and *Apadāna* (PTS 2.467)\]
>
> *“Gate ṭhite bhāsite tuṇhībhāve sampajānakārī bhikkhu.”*
>
> *“A Bikkhu acts in full awareness when walking, standing, talking, and
> keeping silent.”*
>
> *“Gacchanto gacchāmi”*
>
> *“When walking, one knows: ‘I am walking.’”*
>
> \[Paraphrased from *MahāSatipaṭṭhāna Sutta* (DN22; the foundations of
> mindfulness)\]

### **4. Reflecting on Impermanence**

While walking, the contemplation deepens by examining the conditioned
and transient nature of the body and all formations.

> *“Āhārasambhūto kāyo*
>
> *Āhāra<span class="mark">ṁ</span> anicca<span class="mark">ṁ</span>
> dukkha<span class="mark">ṁ</span> anatt<span class="mark">aṁ</span>*
>
> *Kāya<span class="mark">ṁ</span> anicca<span class="mark">ṁ</span>
> dukkha<span class="mark">ṁ</span> anatt<span class="mark">aṁ</span>*
>
> *Sabbasaṅkhāra<span class="mark">ṁ</span>
> anicca<span class="mark">ṁ</span> dukkha<span class="mark">ṁ</span>
> anatt<span class="mark">aṁ</span>”*
>
> *“This body is sustained by food.*
>
> *Food is impermanent, subject to suffering, and not-self.*
>
> *The body is impermanent, subject to suffering, and not-self.*
>
> *All conditioned things are impermanent, suffering, and not-self.”*
>
> \[Not a direct quote; likely inspired by *Puttamaṁsa Sutta* (SN 12.63;
> son’s flesh)

### **5. Aspiring to Be Like the Sages**

> Reflects on calming the body, speech and mind, practicing restraint,
> understanding impermanence, and following the path of the wise ones.
>
> *“Santa kāyo santa vāco*
>
> *Santavāsu samāhito*
>
> *Vantalo<span class="mark">kā</span>miso bhikkhu*
>
> *Tādisā samaṇā mama*
>
> *Santa indriyā santa manasā*
>
> *Santa<span class="mark">ṁ</span> nesa<span class="mark">ṁ</span>
> gata<span class="mark">ṁ</span> chinna<span class="mark">ṁ</span>*
>
> *Okita cakkhu mitabhāṇi*
>
> *Tādisā samaṇā mama”*
>
> *“Calm the body, calm the speech.*
>
> *Composed in peaceful dwelling.*
>
> *Like such sages, I, too, aspire to become one who is liberated from
> the world.*
>
> *Calm the senses, calm the mind.*
>
> *Their path is calm, their attachments cut off.*
>
> *They have restrained vision, they do not look far or close; they see
> just enough.*
>
> *Like such sages, I, too, aspire to become one who is liberated from
> the world.”*
>
> \[Not a direct quote; likely inspired by Sayings of the Buddha,
> *Dhammapada* Verse 378\]
>
> *“Kāya<span class="mark">ṁ</span> purāṇa<span class="mark">ṁ</span>
> kamma<span class="mark">ṁ</span>,*
>
> *Abhisaṅkhata<span class="mark">ṁ</span>
> abhisañcetayita<span class="mark">ṁ</span>,*
>
> *Anicca<span class="mark">ṁ</span> nirodha
> dhamma<span class="mark">ṁ</span>.”*
>
> *“This body is old karma.*
>
> *Formed and shaped by prior intentions.*
>
> *Everything is impermanent and destined for cessation, not eternal.”*
>
> \[Not a direct quote; likely inspired by *Natumha Sutta* (SN 12.37;
> not yours)\]

### **6. Contemplating Mundane Dependent Origination**

The reflection then turns to the causal chain that perpetuates suffering
(*saṁsāra*).

> *“Avijjā paccayā saṅkhārā,*
>
> *Saṅkhāra paccayā viññāṇa<span class="mark">ṁ</span>,*
>
> *Viññāṇa paccayā jāti,*
>
> *Jāti paccayā jarāmaraṇa<span class="mark">ṁ</span> sokaparideva
> dukkha domanassupāyāsā sambhavanti.”*
>
> *“With ignorance as condition, mental formations arise,*
>
> *With mental formations as condition, consciousness arises,*
>
> *With consciousness as condition, birth arises,*
>
> *With birth as condition, arise aging and death, sorrow, lamentation,
> pain, grief, and despair.”*
>
> \[Condensed form of dependent origination; extracted from
> *Paṭiccasamuppāda Sutta* (SN 12.1; dependent origination)\]

### **7. Contemplating the Impermanence of All Formations**

Imagine what it’s like to taste the peace of cessation—the stilling of
formations—echoed in the Buddha’s final words.

> *“Vayadhammā saṅkhārā,*
>
> *Uppāda vaya dhammīno,*
>
> *Uppajjitvā nirujjhanti,*
>
> *Tesa<span class="mark">ṁ</span> vūpasamo sukho.”*
>
> *“Conditioned things are subject to decay,*
>
> *Having the nature to arise and pass away,*
>
> *They come into being and then cease.*
>
> *Their cessation is peaceful.”*
>
> \[Combination of the Buddha’s final words and the verse uttered by
> Sakka, Lord of the Gods, following his passing; from the
> *Mahāparinibbāna Sutta* (DN 16, the Great Passing).\]
>
> *“Sabbe saṅkhārā aniccā’ti,*
>
> *Yadā paññāya passati,*
>
> *Atha nibbindati dukkhe,*
>
> *Esa maggo visuddhiyā.”*
>
> *“All conditioned things are impermanent,*
>
> *When one sees this with wisdom,*
>
> *One becomes disenchanted with suffering.*
>
> *This is the path to purification.”*
>
> \[Sayings of the Buddha, extracted from *Dhammapada—*Verse 278)

### **8. Contemplating Transcendental Dependent Origination**

The reflection turns to the liberating sequence that leads beyond
suffering to Nibbāna.

> *“Avijjā nirodhā saṅkhāra nirodho,*
>
> *Saṅkhāra nirodhā viññāṇa nirodho,*
>
> *Viññāṇa nirodhā jāti nirodho,*
>
> *Jāti nirodhā jarā maraṇa soka parideva dukkha domanassupāyāsā
> nirujjhanti.*
>
> *Eta<span class="mark">ṁ</span> santa<span class="mark">ṁ</span>
> eta<span class="mark">ṁ</span> paṇīta<span class="mark">ṁ</span>,
> yadida<span class="mark">ṁ</span> sabba saṅkhāra samatho,*
>
> *Sabbūpadhipaṭinissaggo,*
>
> *Taṇhakkhayo, virāgo, nirodho, nibbānan ti.”*
>
> “*With the cessation of ignorance, mental formations cease,*
>
> *With the cessation of mental formations, consciousness ceases,*
>
> *With the cessation of consciousness, birth ceases,*
>
> *With the cessation of birth, aging and death, sorrow, lamentation,
> pain, grief, and despair all cease.*
>
> *This is the peaceful, this is the sublime: the calming of all
> formations,*
>
> *The relinquishment of all attachments,*
>
> *The destruction of craving, dispassion, cessation, and Nibbāna.*”
>
> \[Condensed form of dependent origination; extracted from
> *Paṭiccasamuppāda Sutta* (SN 12.1; dependent origination)\]

**A Note for the Practitioner**

While this sequence reflects the specific contemplations verbalized by
Venerable Ñāṇavimala, practitioners are encouraged to adapt these
principles—selecting verses from the *Suttas* that resonate with their
own experience—to support mindfulness and insight during walking
meditation.

**Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s Suggested Readings for Upāsakas (lay people)**

Venerable Ñāṇavimala often recognized lay people who displayed a sincere
commitment to practice. To support their efforts, he would sometimes
give them handouts—privately typed or photocopied sheets of his
teachings—as a personal form of encouragement.

**<u>List of Suttas for Upāsakas:</u>**

*paviveka*

fo<span class="mark">ur *Sat*</span>*ipaṭṭhāna, Samādhinimitta* (M 44)

**Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s Suggested Readings for Saṅgha (monks)**

Given by Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera mainly to young monks—newly ordained
or beginning to live independently. He stressed that genuine training
starts with a clear grasp of the Buddha’s gradual path
(*anupubba-paṭipadā*): from virtue (*sīla*) to concentration (*samādhi*)
and finally to wisdom (*paññā*).

MN 117; Complete the Noble Eightfold Path in a specific order to obtain
samādhi. After that *sammāñāṇa*

and *sammāvimutti*

MN 107; MN 39; MN 53; MN 59; MN 27; The gradual training for a bhikkhu

MN 125; The gradual training for *jhāna*.

A 10.61; If you have difficulties at the present level of practice then
take one step back and practice at that level

MN 61; *Sīla* for laypeople—eight precepts for monks—*Pātimokkha* for
ariyas - *ariyakanta sīla*. This *Sutta* is describing a different kind
of sīla which is purity in *kāya*, *vāca* and *mano* (*tīṇi
sucaritāni*). Based on this *sīla*, *pīti* will arise

MN 19 - Dividing the thoughts[158]

MN 7; Reflecting on the Buddha, Dhamma and Saṅgha leads to step by step
progress as follows: *saddhā*,

*pāmujja, pīti, passaddhakāya*[159]*, sukha, samādhi*. This process is
how one comes to *samādhi* with joy and Dhamma-understanding as a basis

MN 40; More or less the same as M7, but based on purity of the twelve
qualities

MN 64; How to practice for *sammāñāṇa* and *sammāvimutti* with *jhāna*
as the basis

**The Source of Wisdom—Canonical References**

This section, which is a work in progress, lists all *Suttas*
(Discourses of the Buddha) referred to in this work, along with brief
summaries adapted from SuttaCentral.net. They represent the wellspring
from which these notes and reflections arise—words that earlier seekers
journeyed far to find, and which today lie open to all who wish to see
for themselves.

## Dīgha Nikāya

### Sīlakkhandhavagga

- DN2: **Sāmaññaphalasutta** (the fruits of ascetic life)

> The newly crowned King Ajātasattu is disturbed by the violent means by
> which he achieved the crown. He visits the Buddha to find peace of
> mind, and asks him about the benefits of spiritual practice. This is
> one of the greatest literary and spiritual texts of early Buddhism.

Mahāvagga

- DN16: **Mahāparinibbānasutta** (the great discourse on the Buddha’s
  extinguishment)

> The longest of all discourses, this extended narrative tells of the
> events surrounding the Buddha’s death. Full of vivid and moving
> details, it is an ideal entry point into knowing the Buddha as a
> person, and understanding how the Buddhist community coped with his
> passing.

- DN22: **MahāSatipaṭṭhāna sutta** (longer discourse of mindfulness)

> The Buddha details the seventh factor of the noble eightfold path,
> mindfulness meditation. This discourse is essentially identical to MN
> 10, with the addition of an extended section on the four noble truths
> derived from MN 141.

- DN23: **Pāyāsisutta** (with Pāyāsi)

> This is a long and entertaining debate between a monk and a skeptic,
> who went to elaborate and bizarre lengths to prove that there is no
> such thing as an afterlife. The discourse contains a colorful series
> of parables and examples.

- DN24: **Pāthikasutta** (about Pāthika putta)

> When Sunakkhatta threatens to disrobe, the Buddha is unimpressed.
> Rejecting showy displays of asceticism or wondrous powers, he
> demonstrates his pre-eminence.

Pāthikavagga

- DN26: **Cakkavattisutta** (the wheel-turning monarch)

> In illustration of his dictum that one should rely on oneself, the
> Buddha gives a detailed account of the fall of a kingly lineage of the
> past, and the subsequent degeneration of society. This process,
> however, is not over, as the Buddha predicts that eventually society
> will fall into utter chaos. But far in the future, another Buddha,
> Metteyya, will arise in a time of peace and plenty.

- DN27: **Aggaññasutta** (origin of the world)

> In contrast with the brahmin’s self-serving mythologies of the past,
> the Buddha presents an account of evolution that shows how human
> choices are an integral part of the ecological balance, and how
> excessive greed destroys the order of nature.

- DN29: **Pāsādikasutta** (an impressive discourse)

> Following the death of Nigaṇṭha Nātaputta, the leader of the Jains,
> the Buddha emphasizes the stability and maturity of his own community.
> He encourages the community to come together after his death and
> recite the teachings in harmony.

- DN31: **Siṅgālasutta** (advice to Sigalaka)

> The Buddha encounters a young man who honors his dead father by
> performing rituals. The Buddha recasts the meaningless rites in terms
> of virtuous conduct. This is the most detailed discourse on ethics for
> lay people.

## Majjhima Nikāya

Mūlapariyāyavagga

- MN2: **Sabbāsavasutta** (all the defilements)

> The diverse problems of the spiritual journey demand a diverse range
> of responses. Rather than applying the same solution to every problem,
> the Buddha outlines seven methods of dealing with defilements, each of
> which works in certain cases.

- MN5: **Anaṅgaṇasutta** (unblemished)

> The Buddha’s chief disciples, Sāriputta and Moggallāna, use a simile
> of a tarnished bowl to illustrate the blemishes of the mind and
> conduct. They emphasize how the crucial thing is not so much whether
> there are blemishes, but whether we are aware of them.

- MN6: **Mahāmālukyasutta** (the longer discourse with Māluṅkya)

> A little baby has no wrong views or intentions, but the underlying
> tendency for these things is still there. Without practicing, they
> will inevitably recur.

- MN7: **Vatthasutta** (the simile of the cloth)

> The many different kinds of impurities that defile the mind are
> compared to a dirty cloth. When the mind is clean we find joy, which
> leads to states of higher consciousness. Finally, the Buddha rejects
> the Brahmanical notion that purity comes from bathing in sacred
> rivers.

- MN8: **Sallekhasutta** (the effacement)

> The Buddha differentiates between peaceful meditation and spiritual
> practices that encompass the whole of life. He lists forty-four
> aspects, which he explains as “effacement”, the wearing away of
> conceit.

- MN9: **Sammādiṭṭhisutta** (the right view)

> Venerable Sāriputta gives a detailed explanation of right view, the
> first factor of the noble eightfold path. At the prompting of the
> other mendicants, he approaches the topic from a wide range of
> perspectives.

- MN10: **Mahāsatipaṭṭhānasutta** (the discourse on mindfulness
  meditation)

> Here the Buddha details the seventh factor of the noble eightfold
> path, mindfulness meditation. This collects many of the meditation
> teachings found throughout the canon, especially the foundational
> practices focusing on the body, and is regarded as one of the most
> important meditation discourses.

Sīhanādavagga

- MN13: **Mahādukkhakkhandhasutta** (the longer discourse on the mass of
  suffering)

> Challenged to show the difference between his teaching and that of
> other ascetics, the Buddha points out that they speak of letting go,
> but do not really understand why. He then explains in great detail the
> suffering that arises from attachment to sensual stimulation.

- MN14: **Cūḷadukkhakkhandhasutta** (the shorter discourse on the mass
  of suffering)

> A lay person is puzzled at how, despite their long practice, they
> still have greedy or hateful thoughts. The Buddha explains the
> importance of absorption meditation for letting go such attachments.
> But he also criticizes self-mortification, and recounts a previous
> dialog with Jain ascetics.

- MN18: **Madhupiṇḍikasutta** (the honey cake)

> Challenged by a brahmin, the Buddha gives an enigmatic response on how
> conflict arises due to proliferation based on perceptions. Venerable
> Kaccāna draws out the detailed implications of this in one of the most
> insightful passages in the entire canon.

- MN19: **Dvedhāvitakkasutta** (two kinds of thought)

> Recounting his own experiences in developing meditation, the Buddha
> explains how to understand harmful and harmless thoughts, and how to
> go beyond thought altogether.

- MN20: **Vitakkasaṇṭhānasutta** (how to stop thinking)

> In a practical meditation teaching, the Buddha describes five
> different approaches to stopping thoughts.

Opammavagga

- MN22: **Alagaddūpamasutta** (simile of the snake)

> One of the monks denies that prohibited conduct is really a problem.
> The monks and then the Buddha subject him to an impressive dressing
> down. The Buddha compares someone who understands only the letter of
> the teachings to someone who grabs a snake by the tail, and also
> invokes the famous simile of the raft.

- MN25: **Nivāpasutta** (fodder)

> The Buddha compares getting trapped by Māra with a deer getting caught
> in a snare, illustrating the ever more complex strategies employed by
> hunter and hunted.

- MN28: **Mahāhatthipadopamasutta** (the longer simile of the elephant’s
  footprint)

> Sāriputta gives an elaborate demonstration of how, just as any
> footprint can fit inside an elephant’s, all the Buddha’s teaching can
> fit inside the four noble truths. This offers an overall template for
> organizing the Buddha’s teachings.

Mahāyamakavagga

- MN31: **Cūḷagosiṅgasutta** (the shorter discourse at Gosiṅga)

> The Buddha comes across three mendicants practicing diligently and
> harmoniously, and asks them how they do it. Reluctant to disclose
> their higher attainments, they explain how they deal with the
> practical affairs of living together. But when pressed by the Buddha,
> they reveal their meditation attainments.

- MN37: **Cūḷataṇhāsaṅkhayasutta** (The shorter discourse on the ending
  of craving)

> Moggallāna visits the heaven of Sakka, the lord of gods, to see
> whether he really understands what the Buddha is teaching.

- MN38: **Mahātaṇhāsaṅkhayasutta** (the longer discourse on the ending
  of craving)

> To counter the wrong view that a self-identical consciousness
> transmigrates from one life to the next, the Buddha teaches dependent
> origination, showing that consciousness invariably arises dependent on
> conditions.

- MN39: **Mahāassapurasutta** (the longer discourse at Assapura)

> The Buddha encourages the mendicants to live up to their name, by
> actually practicing in a way that meets or exceeds the expectations
> people have for renunciants.

- MN40: **Cūḷaassapurasutta** (the shorter discourse at Assapura)

> The labels of being a spiritual practitioner don’t just come from
> external trappings, but from sincere inner change.

Cūḷayamakavagga

- MN50: **Māratajjanīyasutta** (rebuke of Mara)

> Māra, the trickster and god of death, tried to annoy Moggallāna. He
> not only failed but was subject to a stern sermon warning of the
> dangers of attacking the Buddha’s disciples.

Gahapativagga

- MN53: **Sekhasutta** (a trainee)

> The Buddha is invited by his family, the Sakyans of Kapilavatthu, to
> inaugurate a new community hall. He invites Venerable Ānanda to
> explain in detail the stages of spiritual practice for a lay trainee.

- MN54: **Potaliyasutta** (with Potaliya the householder)

> When Potaliya got upset at being referred to as “householder”, the
> Buddha quizzed him as to the true nature of attachment and
> renunciation.

- MN56: **Upālisutta** (with Upāli)

> The Buddha disagrees with a Jain ascetic on the question of whether
> physical or mental deeds are more important. When he hears of this,
> the Jain disciple Upāli decides to visit the Buddha and refute him,
> and proceeds despite all warnings.

Bhikkhuvagga

- MN61: **Ambalaṭṭhika Rāhulovādasutta** (advice to Rahula at
  Ambalatthika)

> Using the “object lesson” of a cup of water, the Buddha explains to
> his son, Rāhula, the importance of telling the truth and reflecting on
> one’s motives.

- MN64: **Mahāmālukyasutta** (the longer discourse with Mālukya)

> A little baby has no wrong views or intentions, but the underlying
> tendency for these things is still there. Without practicing, they
> will inevitably recur.

- MN66: **Laṭukikopamasutta** (the simile of the quail)

> Again raising the rule regarding eating, but this time as a reflection
> of gratitude for the Buddha in eliminating things that cause
> complexity and stress. The Buddha emphasizes how attachment even to
> little things can be dangerous.

- MN67: **Cātumāsutta** (at Cātumā)

> After dismissing some unruly monks, the Buddha is persuaded to relent,
> and teaches them four dangers for those gone forth.

- MN68: **Naḷakapānasutta** (at Naḷakapāna)

> Those who practice do so not because they are failures, but because
> they aspire to higher freedom. When he speaks of the attainments of
> disciples, the Buddha does so in order to inspire.

Paribbājakavagga

- MN75: **Māgaṇḍiyasutta**: (wth Māgaṇḍiya)

> Accused by a hedonist of being too negative, the Buddha recounts the
> luxury of his upbringing, and his realization of how little value
> there was in such things. Through renunciation he found a far greater
> pleasure.

Rājavagga

- MN81: **Ghaṭikārasutta** (with Ghaṭikāra)

> The Buddha relates an unusual account of a past life in the time of
> the previous Buddha, Kassapa. At that time he was not interested in
> Dhamma, and had to be forced to go see the Buddha. This discourse is
> important in understanding the development of the Bodhisattva
> doctrine.

- MN82: **Raṭṭhapālasutta** (with Raṭṭhapāla)

> A wealthy young man, Raṭṭhapāla, has a strong aspiration to go forth,
> but has to prevail against the reluctance of his parents. Even after
> he became a monk, his parents tried to persuade him to disrobe. The
> discourse ends with a moving series of teachings on the fragility of
> the world.

- MN83: **Maghadevasutta** (about king Maghadeva)

> A rare extended mythic narrative, telling of an ancient kingly lineage
> and their eventual downfall.

Brāhmaṇavagga

- MN99: **Subhasutta** (with Subha)

> Working hard is not valuable in and of itself; what matters is the
> outcome. And just as in lay life, spiritual practice may or may not
> lead to fruitful results.

Devadahavagga

- MN105: **Sunakkhattasutta** (with Sunakkhatta)

> Not all of those who claim to be awakened are genuine. The Buddha
> teaches how true spiritual progress depends on an irreversible letting
> go of the forces that lead to suffering.

- MN106: **Āneñjasappāyasutta** (conducive to the imperturbable)

> Beginning with profound meditation absorption, the Buddha goes on to
> deeper and deeper levels, showing how insight on this basis leads to
> the detaching of consciousness from any form of rebirth.

- MN107: **Gaṇakamoggallānasutta** (with Moggallana the accountant)

> The Buddha compares the training of an accountant with the step by
> step spiritual path of his followers. But even with such a well
> explained path, the Buddha can only show the way, and it is up to us
> to walk it.

Anupadavagga

- MN116: **Isigilisutta** (at Isigili)

> Reflecting on the changes that even geographical features undergo, the
> Buddha then recounts the names of sages of the past who have lived in
> Mount Isigili near Rājagaha.

- MN117: **Mahācattārīsakasutta** (the great fourty)

> A discourse on the prerequisites of right samādhi that emphasizes the
> interrelationship and mutual support of all the factors of the
> eightfold path.

- MN118: **Ānāpānassatisutta** (mindfulness of breathing)

> Surrounded by many well-practiced mendicants, the Buddha teaches
> mindfulness of breathing in detail, showing how it relates to the four
> kinds of mindfulness meditation.

- MN119: **Kāyagatāsatisutta** (mindfulness of the body)

> This focuses on the first aspect of mindfulness meditation, the
> observation of the body. This set of practices, simple as they seem,
> have far-reaching benefits.

Suññatavagga

- MN121: **Cūḷasuññatasutta** (shorter discourse on emptiness)

> The Buddha describes his own practice of meditation on emptiness.

- MN122: **Mahāsuññatasutta** (longer discourse on emptiness)

> A group of mendicants have taken to socializing too much, so the
> Buddha teaches on the importance of seclusion in order to enter fully
> into emptiness.

- MN125: **Dantabhūmisutta** (the level of the tamed)

> A young monk is unable to persuade a prince of the blessings of peace
> of mind. The Buddha offers similes based on training an elephant that
> would have been successful, as this was a field the prince was
> familiar with.

- MN130: **Devadūtasutta** (messengers of the gods)

> Expanding on the previous, this discourse contains the most detailed
> descriptions of the horrors of hell.

Vibhaṅgavagga

- MN131: **Bhaddekarattasutta** (one fine night)

> This discourse opens with a short but powerful set of verses extolling
> the benefits of insight into the here and now, followed by an
> explanation.

- MN135: **Cūḷakammavibhaṅgasutta** (the shorter analysis of deeds)

> The Buddha explains to a brahmin how your deeds in past lives affect
> you in this life.

- MN137: **Saḷāyatanavibhaṅgasutta** (analysis of six sense fields)

> A detailed analysis of the six senses and the relation to emotional
> and cognitive processes.

- MN139: **Araṇavibhaṅgasutta** (the analysis of no strife)

> Achieving peace is no simple matter. The Buddha explains how to avoid
> conflict through contentment, right speech, understanding pleasure,
> and not insisting on local conventions.

Saḷāyatanavagga (MN143-152)

Chapter on the six senses

- MN145: **Puṇṇovādasutta** (advice to Puṇṇa)

> On the eve of his departure to a distant country, full of wild and
> unpredictable people, Venerable Puṇṇa is asked by the Buddha how he
> would respond if attacked there.

## Samyutta Nikāya

Naḷavagga

- SN1.4: **Accentisutta** (time flies)

> Time flies, so we should drop the world’s bait.

- SN2: Devaputtasaṁyutta (Linked Discourses on Godlings)

- SN4: Mārasaṁyutta (linked discourses with Māra)

- SN7: Brāhmaṇasaṁyutta (Linked Discourses with Brahmins)

> SN 7.11: **Kasībhāradvājasuttasutta** (with Bhāradvāja the farmer)
>
> A brahmin farmer criticizes the Buddha for failing to produce food,
> merely living off the work of others. But the Buddha shows that his
> true work is spiritual.

SN9: Vanasaṁyutta (linked discourses in the woods)

SN5.1-12: Gaṅgāpeyyālavagga (abbreviated texts on the Ganges)

- SN12.52: **Upādānasutta** (grasping)

> Craving increases when you linger on pleasing things that stimulate
> grasping, illustrated with the simile of a bonfire.

Dutiyavagga

- SN14.15—**Caṅkamasutta** (walking together)

> The Buddha points out that each of the great disciples has a retinue
> of students of a similar character. And the monks in Devadattas’s
> retinue all have evil wishes.

### Anamataggasaṁyutta (SN15)

Linked discourses on the unknowable beginning

Opammavagga

- SN20.10: **Biḷārasutta** (a cat)

> As a cat may suffer from eating too hastily, so that a mendicant may
> suffer by not restraining senses.

SN22: Khandhasaṁyutta

Linked Discourses on the Aggregates—The first fifty

### Bhāravagga

- SN22.22: **Bhārasutta** (the burden)

> The aggregates are the burden, a person carries that burden, craving
> takes up the burden, and letting go puts down the burden.

Khajjanīyavagga

- SN22.81 **Pālileyyasutta** (at Pālileyya)

> Leaving behind the quarreling monks at Kosambi, the Buddha went to
> stay in the empty forest at Pārileyyaka. Ānanda brings some mendicants
> to receive a teaching, and one of them wonders how to see so as to end
> defilements in this very life. The Buddha teaches the 37 practices
> that lead to awakening, and then goes on to show that various ways of
> regarding the aggregates are conditioned phenomena.

SN35: Saḷāyatanasaṁyutta

Gilānavagga

- SN35.74: **Paṭhamagilānasutta** (the sick, first)

> Hearing that a newly-ordained mendicant was sick, the Buddha visited
> him to offer support and Dhamma encouragement.

- SN35.75: **Dutiyagilānasutta** (the sick, second)

> Hearing that a newly-ordained mendicant was sick, the Buddha visited
> him to offer support and Dhamma encouragement.

Yogakkhemivagga

- SN35.104: **Yogakkhemisutta** (sanctuary from the yoke)

> Someone who has given up attraction to the senses has arrived at the
> sanctuary.

Gahapativagga

- SN35.127: **Bhāradvājasutta** (with Bhāradvāja)

> King Udena of Kosambi asks Venerable Piṇḍola Bhāradvāja how even young
> monks can maintain the celibate life. Piṇḍola says it’s because they
> see women as their sisters, mothers, or daughters. If this doesn’t
> work, they meditate on the ugliness of the body. Finally they exercise
> sense restraint.

Saṭṭhipeyyālavagga

- SN35.203: **Bāhirātītādianattasutta** (The Exterior as Not-Self in the
  Three Times)

Sagāthāvagga

- SN36.6: **Sallasutta** (the arrow)

> Both ordinary and awakened people experience the three feelings. The
> difference is that when an ordinary person is stricken with feeling,
> they react, creating more suffering, whereas an awakened person
> responds with equanimity.

- SN36.7: **Paṭhamagelaññasutta** (the infirmary, first)

> A mendicant should await their death mindful and aware. They should
> bear the feelings of approaching death with wisdom and equanimity.

- SN36.8: **Dutiyagelaññasutta** (the infirmary, second)

> A mendicant should await their death mindful and aware. They should
> bear the feelings of approaching death with wisdom and equanimity.

Paṭhamapeyyālavagga

- SN37.1: **Mātugāmasutta** (a female)

> Five things that make a woman either agreeable or disagreeable to men.

Esanāvagga

- SN45.164: **Bhavasutta** (States of existence)

- SN47: Satipaṭṭhānasaṁyutta

- SN 47.20: Sedaka Sutta (at Sedaka)

Saraṇānivagga

- SN55.21: **Paṭhamamahānāmasutta** (with Mahānāma, first)

> Mahānāma the Sakyan expresses his fear that if he dies unmindful he
> may go to a bad rebirth. The Buddha tells him not to fear, as he will
> definitely go to a good place, since he has long practiced the Dhamma.

- SN55.22: **Dutiyamahānāmasutta** (with Mahānāma, first)

> Mahānāma the Sakyan expresses his fear that if he dies unmindful he
> may go to a bad rebirth. The Buddha tells him not to fear, as he will
> definitely go to a good place, since he has the four factors of
> stream-entry.

Puññābhisandavagga

- SN55.37: **Mahānāmasutta** ( (with Mahānāma)

> The Buddha explains to Mahānāma what is a lay follower, what is a
> virtuous lay follower, and a lay follower accomplished in faith,
> generosity, and wisdom.

## Anguttara Nikaya

Rathakāravagga

- AN3.19: **Paṭhamapāpaṇikasutta** (a shopkeeper, first)

> Shopkeepers and mendicants both have to work hard.

- AN3.20: **Dutiyapāpaṇikasutta** (a shopkeeper, second)

> Shopkeepers and mendicants both have to be clever, responsible, and
> well supported.

Devadūtavagga

- AN3.40: **Ādhipateyyasutta** (in charge)

> Practicing with yourself in charge, or with others, or the teaching.

Mahāvagga

- AN3.65: **Kesamuttisutta** (with the Kālāmas of Kesamutta)

> How to navigate among different spiritual opinions.

Samaṇavagga

- AN3.81: **Samaṇasutta** (ascetics)

> The duties of an ascetic.

- AN3.82: **Gadrabhasutta** (the donkey)

> A donkey tries to moo like a cow. It doesn’t end well.

- AN3.83: **Khettasutta** (fields)

> The duties of a farmer are like those of a mendicant.

- AN3.84: **Vajjiputtasutta** (a Vajji)

> What to do if you can’t keep all the rules.

- AN3.85: **Sekkhasutta** (a trainee)

> What exactly is a “trainee”?

- AN3.86:**Paṭhamasikkhāsutta** (training, first)

> Even spiritually advanced people can break minor rules.

- AN3.87: **Dutiyasikkhāsutta** (training, second)

> Even spiritually advanced people can break minor rules.

- AN3.88: **Tatiyasikkhāsutta** (training, third)

> Even spiritually advanced people can break minor rules.

- AN3.89: **Paṭhamasikkhattayasutta** (three trainings, first)

> The three trainings explained, to seeing the truths.

- AN3.90: **Dutiyasikkhattayasutta** (three trainings, second)

> The three trainings explained, to perfection.

Loṇakapallavagga

- AN3.101: **Paṁsudhovakasutta** (a paneer)

> Meditation is like purifying gold. A meditator should progressively
> eliminate more refined corruptions.

- AN4.41: **Samādhibhāvanāsutta** (ways of developing immersion further)

> Developing convergence for pleasure, understanding, mindfulness, and
> ending defilements.

Pattakammavagga

- AN4.67: **Ahirājasutta** (the snake king)

> Spreading loving-kindness to snakes and other creatures.

Nīvaraṇavagga

- AN5.55: **Mātāputtasutta** (mother and son)

> A mother and son, though ordained as monk and nun, have sexual
> relations. The Buddha launches a powerful attack on the dangers of
> sex.

Kakudhavagga

- AN5.94: **Phāsuvihārasutta** (living comfortably)

> Living comfortably by practicing meditation.

Andhakavindavagga

- AN5.113: **Sammāsamādhisutta** (right immersion)

> To enter samādhi, a mendicant must be able to endure sense impacts.

- AN 5.114: **Andhakavindasutta** (at Andhakavinda)

> Five qualities to instill in recently ordained mendicants.

Dīghacārikavagga

- AN5.229: **Paṭhamakaṇhasappasutta** (black snakes, first)
