> Five ways a woman is like a black snake. \[Intended to counter deeply
> rooted male kāma-rāga (sensual desire) by focusing on the danger
> (ādīnava) and arouse a sense of dispassion, not on the inherent nature
> of gender\].

- AN5.230: **Dutiyakaṇhasappasutta** (black snakes, second)

> Five ways a woman is like a black snake. \[Intended to counter deeply
> rooted male kāma-rāga (sensual desire) by focusing on the danger
> (ādīnava) and arouse a sense of dispassion, not on the inherent nature
> of gender\].

Āhuneyyavagga

- AN6.10: **Mahānāmasutta**

> The Buddha teaches his relative Mahānāma that a noble disciple often
> practices the six recollections, which lead to joy and peace.

Anuttariyavagga

- AN6.25: **Anussatiṭṭhānasutta** (topics for recollection)

> The six recollections are a way to escape from greed.

Mahāvagga

- AN6.63: **Nibbedhikasutta** (penetrative)

> A detailed analysis of several central themes, including sense
> perception, feeling, defilements, kamma, etc.

Devatāvagga

- AN6.66: **Arahattasutta** (perfection)

> Six things lead to perfection.

- AN6.73: **Paṭhamatajjhānasutta** (fist absorption, first)

> Six qualities that enable a mendicant to gain the first absorption.

- AN6.74: **Dutiyatajjhānasutta** (first absorption, second)

Sītivagga

- AN6.85: **Sītibhāvasutta** (coolness)

> Possessing six qualities a mendicant attains to coolness.

Mahāyaññavagga

- AN7.47: **Dutiyaaggisutta** (fires, second)

> The brahmin Uggatasarīra has arranged a great sacrifice. On Ānanda’s
> advice, he asks the Buddha how to make a sacrifice beneficial.

- AN7.48: **Paṭhamasaññāsutta** (perceptions in brief)

> Meditating on seven perceptions leads to the deathless, in brief.

Mahāyaññavagga

- AN 7.61: **Pacalāyamānasutta**

> Before his awakening, Moggallāna is struggling with sleepiness in
> meditation. The Buddha visits him and gives seven ways to dispel
> drowsiness, and other important teachings.

- AN7.72: **Aggikkhandhopamasutta**

> It would be better for a monk to embrace a blazing fire than to lie
> with a maiden.

Mettāvagga

- AN 8.2: **Paññāsutta** (wisdom)

> Eight conditions that lead to the arising of wisdom, its growth and
> perfection.

Mahāvagga

- AN8.17: **Paṭhamabandhanasutta** (catching, first)

> A woman imprisons a man in eight ways.

- AN8.18: **Dutiyabandhanasutta** (catching, second)

> A man imprisons a woman in eight ways.

- AN 8.19: **Pahārādasutta** (with Pahārāda)

> Demons delight in the ocean for eight reasons, and likewise the
> mendicants delight in the Dhamma for eight reasons.

Gahapativagga

- AN8.25: **Mahānāmasutta**

> Questioned by his relative Mahānāma, the Buddha explains what makes
> someone a Buddhist lay follower, a virtuous lay follower, and a lay
> follower practicing for the welfare of all.

Gotamīvagga

- AN8.56: **Bhayasutta** (danger)

> [<u>https://suttacentral.net/an8.56</u>](https://suttacentral.net/an8.56?view=normal&lang=en)
>
> The Buddha explains how addiction to sensual pleasures is perilous.

Sambodhivagga

- AN 9.3: **Meghiyasutta**

> Venerable Meghiya, while attending on the Buddha, wants to go off and
> meditate in a forest alone. The Buddha discourages him, but he goes
> anyway. When his meditation doesn’t go well, he returns chastened to
> the Buddha, who teaches him about the benefits of good companions and
> other fundamentals of a balanced spiritual practice.

Sacittavagga

- AN10.60: **Girimānandasutta**

> The monk Girimānanda is sick. The Buddha encourages Ānanda to visit
> him and speak of ten perceptions.

Nissayavagga

- AN11.2: **Cetanākaraṇīyasutta** (making a wish)

> A virtuous person need not make a wish; it is natural for the path to
> flow on.

- AN11.7: **Saññāsutta** (percipient)

> Ānanda asks the Buddha about a deep state of meditation where all
> normal perception has ceased, but there is still perception. The
> Buddha affirms that such a state exists. Ānanda puts the same question
> to Sāriputta, and gets the same answer.

Anussativagga

- AN11.11: **Paṭhamamahānāmasutta** (with Mahanama, first)

> Mahānāma the Sakyan sees that the monks are preparing robes after the
> rains season. Knowing that the Buddha will soon depart, he asks how
> they are to live with their busy lives.

- AN11.12: **Dutiyamahānāmasutta** (with Mahanama, second)

> Mahānāma the Sakyan sees that the monks are preparing robes after the
> rains season. Knowing that the Buddha will soon depart, he asks how
> they are to live with their busy lives.

## **Glossary**

The terms listed below are from the Pāli language—the language of the
early Buddhist scriptures—and provide the English meaning and context
for essential concepts found in this work.

**Ācariya**: A teacher or master.

**Ādibrahmacāriya:** The beginning of the spiritual life or fundamental
good conduct. It refers to the essential moral foundation for higher
practice, including celibacy, which forms the start of the dedicated
path toward enlightenment.

**Adhiṭṭhāna**: Determination or Resolution. It refers to the unwavering
commitment to a course of practice, a virtue, or a specific spiritual
goal.

**Akusala**: Unwholesome, unskillful, or demeritorious actions or
thoughts.

**Anāgāmi**: A “Non-returner”; the third of the four stages of
enlightenment, a being who will not be reborn in the human realm but in
a pure abode.

**Anāryan:** “Ignoble” or “worldling” (puthujjana). A person who has not
yet attained any of the four stages of enlightenment, characterized by
the persistent view of a permanent “self.”

**Anatta**: “Not-self”; the core Buddhist doctrine that there is no
permanent, unchanging soul or self in any phenomenon.

**Anicca**: Impermanence; the doctrine that all conditioned things are
in a constant state of flux and subject to change.

**Arahant**: A “worthy one”; a fully enlightened being who has attained
Nibbāna and ended the cycle of rebirth. An arahant, having uprooted all
delusion, no longer creates new kamma; their actions are instead termed
kiriya (inoperative) and do not produce future results, thus ending the
cycle of rebirth.

**Aranya**: A forest hermitage or monastery.

**Asubha (saññā)**: Contemplation <span class="mark">on the 32 parts
components the body made is up of</span>; head-hairs, body-hairs, nails,
teeth, skin, flesh, sinews, bones, marrow, kidneys, heart, liver,
pleura, spleen, lungs, intestines, intestinal tract, stomach, faeces,
bile, phlegm, pus, blood, sweat, fat, tears, grease, saliva, nasal
mucus, synovium \[oil lubricating the joints\], urine and brain. There
is nothing attractive in nature found in these, hence one sees the
reality of how unworthy of identifying with it is. It is used as an
antidote to lust.

**<span class="mark">Aṭa</span>pirikara**:
(<span class="mark">Sinhala</span>) The traditional offering of the
eight basic requisites for a Buddhist monk, including robes, an alms
bowl, a razor, etc.

**Avijjā:** Fundamental ignorance or delusion. This is not a lack of
worldly information, but a deep-seated misperception of reality.
Specifically, it is the ignorance of the Four Noble Truths, which causes
one to see what is impermanent as permanent, what is suffering as
pleasure, and what is not-self as a self. It is considered the root
cause that drives the entire cycle of rebirth (saṁsāra)

**Bhikkhu / Bhikkhuni**: A fully ordained Buddhist monk (bhikkhu) or nun
(bhikkhu<span class="mark">nī</span>).

**Brahmā-loka / Brahma World:** A very high and long-lived celestial
being (Brahma) or realm (loka) attained through deep meditation. Despite
its peace, it is still part of the impermanent cycle of rebirth
(saṁsāra).

**Buddhānussati**: Recollection or mindfulness of the qualities of the
Buddha; one of the protective meditations.

**Cārikā**: A journey on foot, especially the traditional ascetic
practice of a wandering monk who does not stay in one place for long.

**Dāna**: The act of giving, generosity, or charity; an offering,
especially of food, to monastics. <span class="mark">Dāna</span> hall is
the place where people offer.

**Deva/Devata**: A deity or celestial being, often compared to an angel.
However, they are still mortal, unenlightened, and bound to the cycle of
rebirth (saṁsāra). While the terms are often used interchangeably, deva
is the general term for a god, whereas devatā can refer more
specifically to a localized spirit or guardian divinity.

**Dhamma / dhammas:** A term with two main meanings depending on
context. Dhamma (capitalized) is the Buddha’s Teaching as a whole, or
the ultimate Truth. In meditation, mindfulness of dhammas (lowercase)
refers to contemplating mental objects or phenomena, often grouped into
categories like the Five Hindrances or the Four Noble Truths.

**Dhammapada**: “Verses of the Dhamma”; a beloved collection of the
Buddha’s sayings in verse form.

**Dhātu**: An element or natural property. Refers to the four great
elements (‘solidity’ or earth, ‘fluidity’ or water, ‘heat’ or fire, and
‘motion’ or air) that are the fundamental components of all physical
matter. By seeing that one’s own body is composed of the same elements
as the world outside, this contemplation helps deconstruct the idea of a
solid ‘self.’

**Dosa**: Aversion, hatred, or ill will; one of the three root
defilements.

**Dukkha**: Suffering, stress, unsatisfactoriness; the first of the Four
Noble Truths.

**Nīvaraṇa**: Hindrances that obstruct meditation and wisdom. There are
five: sensual desire, ill will, sloth and torpor, restlessness and
remorse, and doubt.

**Indriya:** Faculties or controlling principles. In the context of
meditation, this most often refers to the five spiritual faculties:
faith (saddhā), energy (viriya), mindfulness (sati), concentration
(samādhi), and wisdom (paññā). The practice involves developing and
balancing these five qualities to lead the mind toward liberation.

**Jhāna**: A state of deep meditative absorption characterized by
profound stillness and concentration.

**Kālaṁ agameyya:** “Awaiting one’s time.” A phrase used to describe the
final stage of a practitioner’s path, referring to the practice of
mindfully and equanimously awaiting death. It is a contemplation of the
body’s inevitable decay, done with an unagitated mind.

**Kamma (Karma):** (Pāli/Sanskrit) The universal law of cause and effect
where intentional actions of body (kāya), speech (vacī), and mind (mano)
determine one’s future experiences. These actions are primarily
categorized by their nature and results:

- *Wholesome (kusala) kamma* refers to actions rooted in skillful
  qualities like generosity and kindness. While skillful, they are
  performed by one who has not yet eradicated the fundamental ignorance
  (avijjā), and thus lead to favorable rebirth.

- *Unwholesome (akusala) kamma* refers to actions rooted in greed,
  hatred, ○ and delusion (*<span class="mark">lobha, dosa,
  moha</span>*), which lead to painful results.

- *Unperturbable (āneñja) kamma* is a specific type of wholesome *kamma*
  related to deep meditative states, leading to rebirth in a formless
  realm.

**Khandha Paritta**: A protective chant from the *Suttas*, recited to
generate loving-kindness towards snakes and other creatures.

**Kuti**: A small hut or simple dwelling for a monk or nun.

**Loba**: Greed, desire, or attachment; one of the three root
defilements.

**Magga-phala:** “Path and Fruition.” A term for the specific,
supramundane moments of consciousness that mark the attainment of the
four stages of enlightenment. The Path (magga) is a single moment of
insight that cuts through specific defilements, while the Fruition
(phala) is the immediate result—the direct experience of the peace of
Nibbāna.

**Mahāthera**: “Great Elder”; a title of respect for a senior monk,
typically with over twenty years of ordination.

**Māra:** The “Evil One” or “Great Deceiver”; a deity who personifies
temptation, illusion, and the forces that bind beings to the cycle of
rebirth (saṁsāra). While depicted as a being who tempted the Buddha,
Māra can also be understood as a metaphor for the internal
defilements—such as greed, hatred, and fear—that a practitioner must
overcome in their own mind.

**Maraṇānussati**: Recollection or mindfulness of death, used to
cultivate urgency and reduce attachment.

**Mettā (bhāvanā)**: Loving-kindness; the meditative practice of
cultivating boundless goodwill towards all beings.

**Moha**: Delusion or ignorance; one of the three root defilements.

**Nibbāna (Nirvana)**: (Pali/Sanskrit) The ultimate goal of Buddhism;
the complete cessation of suffering and the extinguishing of the “fires”
of greed, hatred, and delusion.

**Nīvaraṇa:** The Five Hindrances; a group of five unwholesome mental
states that obstruct the mind during meditation and block the
development of wisdom. They are: sensual desire, ill will, sloth and
torpor, restlessness and remorse, and doubt

**Pacceka Buddha:** A “Silent” or “Private” Buddha. An individual who
attains full enlightenment on their own, without a teacher, during an
age when the Buddha’s teachings are not available in the world, but who
does not teach the path to others.

**Pāli Canon**: The collection of primary scriptures of Theravāda
Buddhism, written in the Pāli language and consisting of three
“baskets”: the Sutta Piṭaka (discourses), the Vinaya Piṭaka (monastic
discipline), and the Abhidhamma Piṭaka (philosophy).

**Pañcupādānakkhandhā:** The “five aggregates subject to clinging.” This
is the Buddha’s analysis of a person’s experience into five components:
form (the body), feeling, perception, mental formations, and
consciousness. According to the teaching, suffering arises from our
clinging to or identifying with these constantly changing processes as a
permanent “self.”

**Paṭiccasamuppāda**: Dependent Origination; the doctrine that all
phenomena arise in dependence on other phenomena, explaining the process
that perpetuates suffering.

**Piṇḍapāta**: The monastic practice of collecting alms food by walking
with a bowl.

**Piritha (Pirith)**: (Sinhala, from Pali: *Paritta*) A collective term
for a set of protective chants or discourses from the Pāli Canon.

**Poya/Uposatha**: (Sinhala/Pāli) A day of religious observance in Sri
Lanka, typically falling on the full moon, when devout lay Buddhists
often observe eight precepts.

**Puthujjana:** A “worldling” or ordinary person who has not yet
attained any of the four stages of enlightenment and thus remains bound
to the cycle of rebirth (saṁsāra). The opposite of a Noble One (ariya).

**Rishi/Isi:** (Sanskrit/<span class="mark">Pāli</span>) A title meaning
a sage, seer, or revered teacher. It is used in Hindu, Jain, and other
Indian spiritual traditions.

**Rūpa:** Form, matter, or physical phenomena. It is the first of the
Five Aggregates of Clinging (pañcupādānakkhandhā). According to the
*Suttas*, all rūpa is composed of the four great elements (mahābhūta):
the properties of earth (solidity), water (cohesion), fire (heat), and
air (motion). It also includes derived form (upādāya-rūpa), which
consists of the five physical sense faculties (eye, ear, nose, tongue,
body) and their corresponding sense objects (sights, sounds, smells,
tastes, and tangible things).

**Samādhi:** Meditative concentration. A key part of the Noble Eightfold
Path, it is the practice of unifying the mind to serve as the foundation
for wisdom (paññā).

**Samatha**: “Calm-abiding” or tranquility. A category of meditative
practices designed to quiet the mind and develop deep concentration
(*samādhi*). Along with insight (*Vipassanā*), it is one of the two
pillars of Buddhist meditation; a calm mind developed through *samatha*
provides a powerful foundation for seeing reality clearly.

**Saṁsāra**: The endless cycle of birth, death, and rebirth,
characterized by suffering and driven by ignorance and craving.

**Saṁvega**: A sense of spiritual urgency or holy dread that arises from
contemplating the dangers of *saṁsāra* (e.g., old age, sickness, and
death). This feeling is a powerful catalyst for dedicated practice.

**Saṅgha**: The community of Buddhist monastics (bhikkhus and
bhikkhunis); can also refer to the community of all awakened or noble
disciples.

**Saṅkhāra**: As a Buddhist term with primarily two nuanced meanings
depending on context, however the essential characteristic is that being
related to conditional phenomena.

1.  As the fourth of the Five Aggregates, define the
    <span class="mark">saṅkhāra</span>-khandha as the six classes of
    volition: volition regarding forms, sounds, smells, tastes, tactile
    objects, and ideas.

2.  As the second link in Dependent Origination (*avijjā-paccayā
    saṅkhārā*), it refers to *kamma*-forming volitional actions—the
    wholesome and unwholesome actions of body, speech, and mind, born
    from ignorance, that propel the cycle of rebirth.

**Sāsana**: The dispensation, teachings, and legacy of a Buddha.

**Sati**: Mindfulness; awareness of the present moment.

**Sīla**: Morality, virtue, or ethical conduct, often undertaken by
observing precepts.

**Sīmā**: Boundary or consecrated area. In monastic law (Vinaya), the
sīmā is the specific, legally established ground where the Saṅgha
(community of monks) conducts formal legal ceremonies like ordinations
(Upasampadā) and the recitation of the Pātimokkha (monastic rules).

**Sutta (Sutra)**: (Pali/Sanskrit) A discourse or sermon of the Buddha.

**<span class="mark">Swāmī</span>:** (Sanskrit) An honorific title from
the Indian subcontinent, meaning “master” or “one who is master of
oneself.” While it is most commonly used for Hindu spiritual teachers,
in Sri Lanka, it is also used colloquially as a term of deep respect for
Buddhist monks, particularly those seen as highly accomplished in their
practice.

**Thero/ Thera**: A title for a senior Buddhist monk, similar to “Elder”
or “Venerable.”

**Upekkhā**: Equanimity; a state of balanced, unshakable peace of mind
that is not swayed by pleasure or pain.

**Vinaya**: The code of monastic discipline for the Saṅgha.

**Vipassanā**: Insight; meditation that cultivates a direct seeing into
the true nature of reality, especially into the three characteristics of
impermanence, suffering, and not-self.

**Yoniso manasikāra**: Wise attention or wise reflection; paying
attention to things in a way that leads to wisdom rather than delusion.

# 

# 

# **Acknowledgement** 

This book has been a journey of faith and perseverance, made possible
only through the collective goodwill of many individuals. It was an
entirely volunteer-based effort that spanned nearly two years, from
November 2023 to October 2025.

Our deepest gratitude goes, first and foremost, to the memory of
Venerable Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera, whose profound life of renunciation is
the very heart of this work. His ascetic, unwavering example continues
to be a source of immense inspiration.

We are profoundly indebted to Venerable Bambalapitiye Gnanaloka Thera.
He not only gave his permission for this project but generously shared
the private, handwritten notes of his teacher, entrusting them to us.
Given his ascetic nature and deep commitment to solitude, meeting with
him was a rare and precious opportunity. Over the course of this
project, a small team of writers was granted several extensive
interviews—some lasting many hours—both in Sri Lanka and during his
travels abroad. During these invaluable sessions, he patiently answered
our questions, clarified ambiguities in the decades-old notes, and
reviewed the reflections to ensure they faithfully preserved the spirit
and meaning of his encounters with his master. We hope this book does
justice to his trust.

This work would not exist without the tireless efforts of a dedicated
team of volunteer editors and reviewers, both lay and monastic, whose
insightful feedback helped to shape and refine this manuscript. We also
extend our gratitude to the devoted supporters who cared for Venerable
Ñāṇavimala during his final years, and to all those who have preserved
his memory and teachings over the decades.

May any merit generated from this work be dedicated to the liberation of
all beings.

**Sri Sambuddha Mission Trust**

The Sri Sambuddha Mission Charitable Trust was established to support
meditation practitioners and propagate the Buddha’s original teachings.
The Trust is dedicated to preserving and sharing the Dhamma with
sincerity and generosity.

Venerable Bambalapitiye Gnanaloka Thera serves as the spiritual advisor
to the Trust and its associated monastery, the Sri Sambuddha Ramaneeya
Ashramaya Forest Hermitage—a serene meditation center nestled in the
picturesque surroundings of Dunumala, Sri Lanka.

The monastery regularly hosts *Saddhammadhara Meditation Retreats* for
both monastics and lay visitors. Following a framework inspired by Ven.
Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera, these retreats emphasize a quiet, self-guided
exploration of the Buddha’s teachings through direct engagement with the
*Suttas*. As Ven. Ñāṇavimala envisioned, the ultimate intention is to
inspire suitable individuals toward the renunciant path.

For those interested in supporting the mission or inquiring about a
retreat, please contact the Trust at:

Phone: +94 76 422 4858 or +94 77 738 7842

Email: srisambuddhamissiontrust@gmail.com

Website: www.srisambuddhamission.org

**Back Cover \[Draft Concept\]**

<span class="mark">For decades, a German-born ascetic walked barefoot
the length and breadth of Sri Lanka, becoming a silent legend. That monk
was Venerable Ñāṇavimala—a master who embodied the Buddha’s teachings in
their purest form. Yet, for a few precious years, he opened his heart to
a devoted disciple who kept a record of his rare and profound
instructions.</span>

<span class="mark">This Is Also In The Mind is that record. These pages
offer a unique glimpse into the living transmission of the Dhamma, told
through the intimate notes and moving reflections of Venerable
Gnanaloka. It is a story of radical simplicity, unwavering courage, and
the timeless path to liberation. More than a book of teachings, it is an
invitation to walk in the footsteps of a wandering ascetic and discover
the unshakeable peace that lies within.</span>

[1] Sinhalese Buddhist lay and civic organizations gained considerable
influence in the late colonial period, alongside the island’s largely
non-violent independence movement led by an educated middle class. This
movement culminated in the transfer of power to local representatives
and the establishment of the Dominion of Ceylon on 4 February
1948—setting the stage for a renewal of Buddhist institutional life
after over three centuries of colonial rule.

[2] They were the only Western-born monks invited to participate in the
Sixth Buddhist Council in Yangon, Burma (1954–1956), in recognition of
their deep knowledge and contributions to Buddhism.

[3] Mahānāyaka theros are high-ranking Buddhist monks who oversee and
regulate the Buddhist clergy in <span class="mark">Theravāda</span>
Buddhist countries.

[4] The Word of the Buddha: An Outline of the Buddha in the Words of the
Pāḷi Canon—Compiled, translated and explained by Ven. Nyanatiloka
Mahāthera.

[5] In the original notes, this discourse is referred to simply as
Rāhulovāda Sutta, though its full title is Ambalaṭṭhikarāhulovāda Sutta
(MN61; advice to Rāhula at Ambalaṭṭhika).

[6] This practice is about the reflection of bodily, verbal and mental
actions, reflecting before, during and after, as a foundational practice
that should be done always.

[7] Primary focus should be given to *Khandhasaṁyutta* section (SN22;
Connected Discourses on the Aggregates). Buddha analyzes the five
aggregates—form, feeling, perception, volitional formations, and
consciousness—as the aggregates subject to clinging, showing them to be
impermanent, suffering, and not-self.

[8] The *Satipaṭṭhāna Sutta* (Discourse on the foundations of
Mindfulness) is a foundational discourse of the Buddha, considered
essential for the development of insight and the attainment of Nibbāna
(Final Liberation). It lays out the four objects for the practice of
Satipaṭṭhāna: mindfulness of the body, feelings, mind, and mental
objects. The two main canonical versions are the shorter *Satipaṭṭhāna
Sutta* (MN10; the foundations of mindfulness) and the longer
*Mahāsatipaṭṭhāna Sutta* (DN22; foundations of mindfulness).

[9] Primary focus should be given to *Saḷāyatanasaṃyutta* section (SN35;
Connected Discourses on the Six Sense Bases). Buddha analyzes the eye,
ear, nose, tongue, body, mind and their objects—as the domain where
contact conditions feeling and craving. He teaches guarding the sense
faculties and seeing impermanence, suffering, and not-self in relation
to the six sense bases.

[10] *Samatha* (calm abiding) meditation stills and steadies the mind.
But when drowsiness intrudes, the Buddha advised the monk
Mahā-Moggallāna to: change the object of meditation, recite/reflect on
Dhamma, rouse the body (rub the ears/limbs), wash the face and open the
gaze, attend to light, walk; if needed, lie down briefly. He later
became a chief disciple, foremost in psychic powers.

[11] Ven. Ñāṇavimala gave directions in the briefest form: “*Majjhima*
\[number\]” names the discourse in the *Majjhima Nikāya* and, when
several are given, the order to follow. The sequence “7, 8, 9, 10” is a
compact progressive training: (MN7) begins by cleansing the mind’s
stains; (MN8) guides the effacement of defilements; (MN9) establishes
right view as the framework; (MN10) sets out the direct path of
mindfulness to be cultivated throughout.

[12] Ven. Gnanaloka clarifies: “Mentally” refers to experiences arising
in meditation (e.g. visions). Regard them like sense-objects: observe
without assigning value or taking them up, and see their impermanence..

[13] Ven. Gnanaloka clarifies, this is referring to removing the mental
defilements (*āsavas*), by the approaches described in the *Suttas*.
This requires a certain degree of knowledge of the *Suttas*.

[14] *Jhāna* provides the clarity and steadiness of mind through which
the Dhamma is directly seen.This seeing is not conceptual knowledge but
insight born of experience.

[15] In Sri Lanka, a *Poya* day is the full-moon Uposatha observance.
Lay Buddhists typically visit the temple or a forest monastery (often in
white) and “take sīla” for the day—usually the Eight Precepts. This
long-standing lay observance deepens study, meditation, and lay-monastic
reciprocity.

[16] Ven. Gnanaloka clarifies this refers to gaining approval from his
father, but being sensitive to how he is feeling as well, not just about
getting formal approval.

[17] Aranya refers to forest hermitages. *Sāsana* denotes the Buddha’s
dispensation—the Dhamma and Vinaya as lived and handed on. The statement
points not only to the impermanence of hermitages and buildings but,
more critically, to decline when practice, study, and the weakening of
virtue.

[18] Ven. Gnanaloka notes this line is a personal note—an
over-explanation of Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s teaching. “Presently arisen
consciousness and components” should be read as the immediate field of
experience—e.g., the breath together with contact, feeling, perception,
and volitional formations—as observed as they arise and pass.

[19] Ven. Gnanaloka notes this sentence is a personal note—an
over-explanation of Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s teaching. In the context of
*lokuttara sammā-diṭṭhi*, when Nibbāna is the object, “past formation”
should be understood as the bodily formation (*kāya-saṅkhāra*)—i.e., the
last breath occurring as a result of past *kamma*. \[For further
context, see Reflection 08.\]

[20] To reach the first stage of awakening (stream-entry), the “four
factors” to keep in mind are noble friendship (*kalyāṇamitta*), hearing
the true Dhamma (*saddhamma-savana*), wise attention (*yoniso
manasikāra*), and practice in accordance with the Dhamma
(*dhammānudhamma-paṭipatti*).

[21] A personal note by Shanaka—reminder of the tug between retreat and
lay duty: once outside the forest monastery, he would have to resume
study and, in time, work—engagements rooted in the sensory world.

[22] Edgar Cayce (1877–1945) was a well-known American psychic who gave
trance readings that purported to reveal individuals’ past lives. In the
book Many Mansions (1952) by Gina Cerminara, it documents Cayce’s work
on karma and reincarnation as a cause for present-life conditions.

[23] Ven. Ñāṇavimala related that, with a developed *Satipaṭṭhāna*
practice, recollections of past lives began to occur. One such
recollection revealed the origin of his lifelong migraines: in a former
life during the time of the Crusades he had been a German soldier
fighting Romans in a German village; a short Roman soldier approached,
and he struck this Roman soldier on the head with a club, killing him.
He noted his own unwholesome act, following this insight on *kamma,* the
migraines subsided. As an additional comment, Ven. Ñāṇavimala would
stress the importance of grounding oneself in *Satipaṭṭhāna*
(mindfulness), stating that the practice provides the necessary mental
balance to confront often overwhelming past life recollections. ‘It was
(consciousness)’ refers to Ven Ñāṇavimala regarding these experiences as
that of the working of consciousness.

[24] Composed around the beginning of the Common Era, and of unknown
authorship, the *Milindapañha* is set up as a compilation of questions
posed by the Indo-Greek King Milinda (identified as King Menander of
Bactria) to a revered senior monk named Nāgasena. Widely revered for its
clear analogies and probing treatment of issues like self/no-self,
*kamma*, and rebirth—an early meeting of Hellenistic and Buddhist
thought.

[25] Ven. Gnanaloka states that Ven. Ñāṇavimala would sometimes say this
when Shanaka was leaving his kuti or was returning back to Colombo. Ven.
Ñāṇavimala used this statement to emphasize that spiritual unity is
solely determined by mental purity, not physical proximity. The Buddha
could see a sincere seeker practicing in the distant. Whereas a person
full of defilements (*kilesas*) is far away, even if touching the
Buddha’s robe.

[26] Sri Lankan forest hermitages: Galduwa = Galduwa Aranya Senasanaya
aka Sri Gunawardhana Yogasramaya (Ambalangoda); Vaturavila = Wathuruwila
Aranya Senasanaya (Galle), Tapovana (Angoda) = Gothama Thapowanaya
(Colombo), Kalugala = Kalugala Aranya Senasanaya (Kalutara District).
For more information, refer to Buddhist Forest Monasteries and
Meditation Centres in Sri Lanka: A Guide for Foreign Buddhist Monastics
and Lay Practitioners, by Bhikkhu Nyanatusita.

[27] Ven. Gnanaloka suggested the likely source: This may refer to the
German philosopher, Arthur Schopenhauer, who told of a man who, upon
seeing a naked woman, felt desire, but after noticing an open ulcer on
her body, was so struck by the reality of decay that he abandoned
worldly life to live as a hermit in Egypt.

[28] Ven. Gnanaloka was unsure of the source. The anecdote bears
resemblance to two distinct historical accounts: that of Queen Anne
Boleyn (executed 1536), whose head and body were reportedly placed in a
makeshift chest; and that of Empress Isabella of Portugal (d. 1539),
whose disfigured corpse is said to have prompted Duke Francis Borgia to
renounce worldly life.

[29] Ven. Gnanaloka clarifies—he has liked studies in a previous life,
and supported someone to study in that life, perhaps even forcing it
onto somebody. This consequence of *kamma* is coming back to him in this
life with his father pressuring him to focus on his studies. He also
adds that if his father supports him in the spiritual way, he will get
similar support in the future.

[30] Ven. Gnanaloka clarifies—what you are experiencing is related to
your internal world such as defilements and so on.

[31] Refers to the practice highlighted in the *Ambalaṭṭhika Rāhulovāda
Sutta* (MN 61; the advice to Rāhula at Ambalaṭṭhika).

[32] Ven. Ñāṇavimala advised Shanaka to seek employment in fields like
the Red Cross, civil service, or as a carer/attendant. The emphasis was
on choosing a livelihood (*ājīva*) that aligned with the Dhamma (Right
Livelihood) and left ample time for practice, rather than pursuing large
monetary gain. Such work generates wholesome *kamma*.

[33] Mahānāma was a prominent Sakyan layman and a relative of the
Buddha, known for asking practical questions about how a layperson can
cultivate faith and virtue. The practices often discussed in these
discourses include the recollections (*anussati*) of the Buddha
(*Buddhānussati*), the Dhamma (*Dhammānussati*), and the Saṅgha
(*Saṅghānussati*), as well as the cultivation of loving-kindness
(*mettā*) and the perception of impermanence (*anicca saññā*). These
teachings can be found in several discourses addressed to him: AN 6.10;
AN 8.25; AN 11.11; AN 11.12; SN 55.21; SN 55.22; SN 55.37.

[34] Ven. Gnanaloka clarifies: The book ‘*The Word of the Buddha*’,
presents the essence of the Dhamma primarily through direct excerpts
from the early Buddhist texts. However, Ven. Ñāṇatiloka also included
brief explanatory passages and summaries drawn from the Abhidhamma and
the commentarial understanding. Ven. Ñāṇavimala encouraged Shanaka to
focus just on the presentations of the early texts themselves.

[35] This is Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s remark regarding the previous entry notes
about marriage & work, saying that those were only suggestions, but
giving Shanaka the freedom to choose what he thinks he should do.

[36] Shanaka was gathering a lot of information at that time about
*Suttas* and different ways of understanding—so his questioning was
about why he was gathering all this Dhamma knowledge.

[37] Ven. Ñāṇavimala encouraged monks who aimed for the ultimate goal of
*magga-phala* (the paths and fruits of liberation) to study the *Khandha
Saṁyutta* (SN 22; Connected Discourses on the Aggregates) and the
*Saḷāyatana Saṁyutta* (SN 35; Connected Discourses on the Six Sense
Bases).

[38] These teachings are found in several discourses given to Mahānāma,
the Sakyan prince and cousin of the Buddha, including: AN 6.10; AN 8.25;
AN 11.11; AN 11.12; SN 55.21; SN 55.22; SN 55.37.

[39] This is a reference to the booklet ‘The Practice of Loving-Kindness
(Mettā)’, compiled and translated by Ven. Ñāṇamoli Thera. A British
Theravāda Buddhist monk and translator of Pāli literature, he was also
Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s Pāli teacher. Ven Gnanaloka clarifies that Ñāṇamoli
Thera describes in the book “where you change *mettā* to *Khandha*
contemplation”. This was how Ven. Ñāṇavimala practised *mettā.*

[40] *Sati-sampajañña* refers to mindfulness and clear comprehension —
the continuous awareness (sati) of body, feelings, mind, and phenomena,
together with the discernment/clear knowing (sampajañña) - *Sati Sutta*
(47.35. Mindful).

[41] On numerous occasions, Shanaka accompanied his teacher from his hut
to the monastery dining hall, where Venerable Ñāṇavimala would verbalize
his *caṅkama* (walking meditation) contemplations. These exact phrases,
memorized by Shanaka, are presented in the appendix.

[42] Venerable Gnanaloka clarifies that when a meditator gains a moment
of clarity while reading or reciting the teachings, they should not get
lost in wishing for it again. Instead, they should focus on diligently
practicing the very things that led to that insight.

[43] While all of the *Suttas* in the *Majjhima Nikāya* (MN) are
valuable, the first ten (from Mūlapariyāya Sutta to Satipaṭṭhāna Sutta)
are especially recommended for meditators as they lay a foundational
understanding of the core principles of the Dhamma that lead directly to
the path of liberation.

[44] “Mind precedes all mental states. Mind is their chief; they are all
mind-wrought. If with an impure mind a person speaks or acts suffering
follows him like the wheel that follows the foot of the ox.” (Dhp1)

[45] Māra is also known as the “Great Deceiver” or the “Evil One,” that
embodies temptation and illusion and seeks to distract the spiritual
practitioner from the path to liberation.

[46] In 1971, the Janatha Vimukthi Peramuna (JVP), a Marxist and
anti-imperialist youth movement, launched an armed uprising with
coordinated attacks on police stations across Sri Lanka. The revolt was
crushed within weeks but left thousands dead and many imprisoned.

[47] The phrase ‘*Budu putha*’ (blessed as a Buddha) is a fairly common
Sinhalese term of endearment used to express that the child is precious
or beloved in a moment of great joy.

[48] *Sīla* (moral conduct): For lay followers, *Sīla* is primarily
practiced by observing the Five Precepts: abstaining from killing,
stealing, sexual misconduct, false speech, and intoxicants.

[49] *Kataññu Sutta* (AN2.32; Gratitude)

[50] To Ven Ñāṇavimala there was no second best to knowing Pāli, that
was the best way to understand the Buddha. Regardless of how good they
are, translators’ views invariably get projected into their
translations, distorting meaning to some degree. He encouraged Shanaka
to learn Pāli. However he directed Shanaka to the *Suttas* through
English translations and Shanaka/Ven. Gnanaloka would rely on them as
his primary source of meaning.

[51] Refers to acquiring knowledge of spiritual literature just for
knowledge sake.

[52] Ven. Gnanaloka elaborates: With *sīla* and *saddha* (faith) and
*cāga* (generosity), you stay with good activities— you’re elevated to a
deva state of existence. Then in such a state of mind you practise the
Dhamma.

[53] The five spiritual faculties (pañcindriyāni) are inner strengths
that sustain progress on the path: saddhā (faith or confidence), viriya
(energy or effort), sati (mindfulness), samādhi (concentration,
including the four jhānas), and paññā (wisdom or insight into the true
nature of phenomena)—*Paṭhamavibhaṅga Sutta* (SN48.36; Analysis (1))

[54] The Three Protections refer to the recollection of refuge in the
Buddha, the Dhamma, and the Saṅgha.

[55] The body’s impure nature, Ven. Gnanaloka explains, arises from past
defiled thoughts—due to greed, hate and delusion. It is a result of
*kamma*, that this body has taken the form it has. “Why is it
repulsive?” he asks. “Because it’s created by repulsive thoughts. He
means that you have to picture the reality of how gross it is.” The
reflection is meant for clear seeing: one cannot observe what one is
attached to. By reflecting upon the body’s gross nature, a necessary
distance for insight is created— “because you cannot observe something
that you are attached to.” When thoughts are more refined, the resulting
*kamma* is also more refined, giving rise to subtler bodies of
existence. Yet, all remain bound by the same law of arising and passing
away.

[56] Ven. Gnanaloka clarifies: the teachings in the *Rāhulovāda Sutta*
(about bodily, verbal and mental actions)—this should be done during the
whole day. In the evening do loving kindness meditation. Reflect upon
impermanence as understood through the *Suttas*—applying it to the five
aggregates of clinging. Reflect on impermanence when doing walking
meditation as well.

[57] *Bahussuta* (Pāli) literally means “much-heard” — i.e., well-read
or extensively acquainted with the *Suttas*. When Ven. Ñāṇavimala says
“Bahussuta is important,” he is emphasizing that close and repeated
study of the Buddha’s discourses strengthens right view, supplies the
practitioner with suitable objects for reflection, and supports
practice.

[58] Ven. Gnanaloka clarified that this was a reference to a trend,
known at the time, of business leaders using meditative concentration
developed in places like Rishikesh for the purpose of worldly
manifestation. Ven. Ñāṇavimala, who had direct experience with this
concentration power from his time as a corporate director in his lay
life, was adding the crucial teaching that all such achievements are
ultimately impermanent and “come down very fast.”

[59] Ven. Gnanaloka recalls a visitor from Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s very
hometown in Germany came to Polgasduwa, yet his teacher showed no
special reaction or emotion. This unwavering restraint starkly
illustrated the principle of reducing bonds and ties with all people and
places, even those of profound personal significance.

[60] Ven Gnanaloka clarifies, by giving the example that someone may
even think they have become enlightened—don’t assume because it may also
be wrong. He recommended better to have discussions, which may give
different perspectives on what you are experiencing.

[61] Bojjhanga Samyutta (SN 46): A section of the Connected Discourses
where the Buddha teaches the Seven Factors of Awakening—mindfulness,
investigation (clear seeing), energy, joy, calm, concentration, and
equanimity.

[62] Ven. Gnanaloka explains how Ven. Ñāṇavimala explains that
equanimity is the highest conscious experience. He would say based on
that you have the formless states.

[63] The original writes ‘(theoretical grasping),’ however, the meaning
is to get a theoretical foundation.

[64] The five hindrances are mental obstacles that cloud understanding
and obstruct concentration: kāmacchanda (sensual desire), vyāpāda (ill
will), thīna–middha (sloth and torpor), uddhacca–kukkucca (restlessness
and remorse), and vicikicchā (doubt).

[65] Ven. Gnanaloka clarifies, the idea is to not dwell or brood over
troublesome life situations, know that it is from consequences of past
*kamma* and those consequences will also pass. Rather one should take
care not to create new unwholesome *kamma*.

[66] Original transcript written as ‘turn back’; “stick to an attainment
or turn back from effort.”

[67] Ven. Gnanaloka clarifies: as people’s virtue (*sīla*) declines,
sickness increases in the world. This relates to the principle that
immoral conduct (*akusala kamma*) serves as a root cause for disease.
The canonical connection between the decline of morality and the
increase in sickness and shortening of lifespan is detailed in the
*Cakkavatti-Sīhanāda Sutta* (DN 26).

[68] The eight requisites of a monk are three robes, almsbowl, razor,
needle/thread, belt & water strainer.

[69] The Dhammapada is a revered collection of the Buddha’s verses on
ethical and mental cultivation, drawn from the Pāli Canon.

[70] The five contemplations, as found in the *Upajjhāya Sutta* (AN
5.57), are five facts the Buddha taught for daily reflection: that one
is subject to aging, illness, and death and has not gone beyond them;
that one will be separated from all that is dear; and that one is the
owner and heir of one’s own actions (*kamma*). This is also commonly
found in Sinhala chanting texts, such as the Maharagama Pirith Book (the
English equivalent being ‘The Mirror of the Dhamma’ compiled by
Venerable Nārada Mahāthera and Venerable Kassapa Thera).

[71] Ven. Ñāṇavimala stresses this reflection of bodily, verbal and
mental actions, reflecting before, during and after, as a foundational
practice that should be done always. Ven. Gnanaloka comments “I’ve
noticed that those who have developed *samādhi*, they have that
developed in their mind (also).” Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s advice about doing
things “slowly, mindfully, carefully” goes with this. Ven. Gnanaloka
recalls a rishi he once met in India that used to write what bad
thoughts he would have everyday within a cave wall. Over time it
gradually became zero.

[72] The Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s emphasis here is on rejecting practises that
deviate from the teachings found in the *Suttas* such as rituals. He
however cautioned against being openly critical, as noted on 21st April
1994 about the danger in doing that - “even what is left will
disappear.” Instead one must not encourage it is the best approach.

[73] Venerable Mahākassapa and Venerable Bhaddā Kapilānī, both Arahants,
were once husband and wife who lived in celibacy and later renounced
household life by mutual consent.

[74] At that time Shanaka was pursuing his higher studies, and Ven.
Ñāṇavimala’s comment “do your studies properly” is regarding that,
seeing that Shanaka was perhaps neglecting this aspect.

[75] Ven. Ñāṇavimala would also add to this that there is also a *kamma*
in being in solitude. In other words, do not attach to the solitude, or
don’t have to try and continuously long for it, but these conditions may
arise depending on conditions.

[76] Referring to the Buddha’s last words before his
passing—*Mahāparinibbāna Sutta* (DN16; the Great Passing).

[77] “You must sometimes accomodate” - means one must learn to be
flexible, sometimes it may seem like one is even going with the current,
but slowly gradually directing it according to the conditions, without
trying to cut across directly. In a practical real world sense, this may
appear as they have to take a decision such as taking a job or duty in
marriage, in order to fulfill a certain worldly responsibility. However
even in the face of those decisions, one must gradually steer it
tactfully towards the goal.

[78] Recommended by Ven. Ñāṇavimala was ‘The Mirror of the Dhamma: A
Manual of Buddhist Chanting and Devotional Texts’, compiled by Nārada
Thera and Bhikkhu Kassapa. This English-language manual served a similar
function to the traditional Sinhala Vat Pirith Potha, published by the
Siri Vajiragnana Dharmayathanaya Temple at Maharagama.

[79] Venerable Ñāṇavimala’s advice was directly related to Shanaka’s
service to him during his final years. He taught that while physical
assistance creates merit, the highest form of merit is gained through
the disciple’s own sustained purification of mind in meditation.

[80] This statement reflects the reverse sequence of *paṭiccasamuppāda*
(dependent origination), showing the cessation aspect
(*paṭiloma-paṭiccasamuppāda*). When *avijjā* (ignorance) ceases,
*saṅkhārā* (volitional formations) cease; with the cessation of
formations, there is no bhava (becoming), and consequently no *jāti*
(birth), *jarā* (aging), *vyādhi* (sickness), or *maraṇa* (death). The
phrase “What doesn’t arise doesn’t pass away” expresses the realization
of *nibbāna*—the unconditioned state, free from arising and ceasing.

[81] In the Theravāda tradition, Dhamma kāya means “embodiment of
Dhamma.” Like the Buddha, even realized teachers cannot make another
practice or attain—they can only point out the way through the Dhamma
they have realized.

[82] Poya Day is a public holiday in Sri Lanka, and this instruction
emphasizes dedicating the full day to intensified practice.

[83] Refer to Notes; Chapter 1, 18th April 1994 for more description
regarding his past life recollections.

[84] For a detailed account refer to ‘Pure Inspiration; Venerable
Ñāņavimala Mahāthera—Recollections Of His Life, Practice And Teachings.’

[85] The *Khandha Paritta* is a protective chant taught by the Buddha to
safeguard practitioners from dangerous creatures, such as snakes, by
radiating thoughts of universal loving-kindness (*mettā*).

[86] Kudumbigala Aranya Senasanaya is an ancient forest monastery near
Okanda on the edge of Kumana National Park, traditionally dated to the
3rd century BCE (King Devanampiyatissa).

[87] These teachings are found in several discourses given to Mahānāma,
the Sakyan prince and cousin of the Buddha, including: AN 6.10; AN 8.25;
AN 11.11; AN 11.12; SN 55.21; SN 55.22; SN 55.37.

[88] An acquaintance once told Shanaka that, in a past life, he and Ven.
Ñāṇavimala had studied under an elder named Ven. Saddhatissa during an
ancient period when Buddhism was flourishing in Sri Lanka. When asked
about it, Ven. Ñāṇavimala gently set aside such past-life claims,
bringing him back to the present and directing his attention to the
Dhamma.

[89] ‘(Maybe an attainment etc.)’ was Shanaka’s own real-time
interpretation. Ven. Ñāṇavimala himself did not make such a claim and
consistently discouraged any focus on attainments, advising instead to
simply continue the practice.

[90] The *Mātugāma Sutta* (SN 37.1) details qualities that appeal to
men, serving as a monastic aid for contemplating the danger of sensual
attachment (*kāma ādīnava*). The canonical counterpart, listing
qualities that appeal to women, is the *Purisa Sutta* (SN 37.2).

[91] Ven. Gnanaloka recalls Ven. Ñāṇavimala relating that, during his
school days, he was especially drawn to a particularly attractive girl.
He explained that this sense of closeness and attraction to certain
people—and even particular places—is due to being closely connected in a
past life. Ven. Ñāṇavimala cautioned Shanaka not to give into such
feelings or try to seek them out to try and thinking to make them
permanent, because they are merely past *kamma*. Being blindly driven by
them only ensures one is sucked into continuing that bondage in
*saṁsāra*.

[92] The story, in the *Nanda Sutta* (*Udāna* 3.2), is about using one
desire to overcome another. The Buddha promised Ven. Nanda 500 celestial
women to motivate him to stay a monk, as he was struggling with lust for
his fiancée. This promise was a skillful trick: it gave Ven. Nanda the
drive to practice until he achieved Arahantship. He realized all desire,
whether for his fiancée or the celestial women, was a form of
bondage—and he chose liberation instead.

[93] The daily practice of a bhikkhu is centred on overcoming the five
hindrances (pañca nīvaraṇāni): sensual desire, ill will, sloth and
torpor, restlessness and remorse, and doubt—by cultivating the five
spiritual faculties (pañcindriyāni): faith, energy, mindfulness,
concentration, and wisdom.

[94] *Ariyakanta sīla* — “virtue dear to the Noble Ones”; the purity of
moral conduct that is cherished and praised by the Noble Disciples
(ariya), forming the foundation for the higher training in concentration
and wisdom.

[95] The original note mentioned this individual by name; it has been
changed to ‘a yogi’ to protect their privacy.

[96] *Bhāvanā* is the exercises of purifying the mind such as reflecting
on the *Suttas* or *mettā* and so on.

[97] Ven. Gnanaloka clarifies that the “actual nature of function”
refers to the practice of applying the *Suttas* directly to one’s own
experience, rather than treating them as abstract philosophy. This
process cultivates a “dhamma picture” in the mind’s eye—an intuitive
understanding that arises from the “eye of wisdom.” This wisdom is
developed by using the *Suttas* as “signposts” for analysis. However, to
prevent this analysis from becoming purely intellectual, it must be
anchored in the present moment. This grounding is achieved by repeatedly
returning to *Kāyagatāsati* (mindfulness of the body). When this
balanced practice leads to a state of *samādhi* (concentration), it is a
sign that one is cultivating the correct “dhamma picture.” This is a
form of “right tuning,” where the mind becomes steady and free from the
hindrances.

[98] Minneriya is a large national park in Sri Lanka, a vast wilderness
with very few inhabitants. Receiving an unsolicited lunch pack while on
*cārikā* in such a remote area is highly improbable. He related the
story: Ven. Ñāṇavimala arrived unannounced where food would be unlikely.
Yet an elderly woman offered him a carefully prepared lunch, explaining
that a *devatā* had appeared to her the previous night during her
chanting, instructing her to prepare a meal for a bhikkhu who would
arrive the next morning.

[99] This refers to the personal responsibility to continue the lineage
of practice for the sake of future generations. As Ven. Gnanaloka
clarifies, the warning is not to be the person who breaks the chain of
practice passed down from a teacher, thus ensuring the path remains open
for others. He gives the example, “my teacher went on a certain path, if
I don’t go on that path and stop, then others also will stop and it will
stop.” (See the *Makhadeva Sutta*, MN 83 for the classical origin of
this theme).

[100] The first account refers to *Raṭṭhapāla Sutta* (MN 82; on
Raṭṭhapāla), and the second to *Saṅgāmaji Sutta* (Ud 1.8; with
Saṅgāmaji).

[101] An anagārika is a person who has given up most or all of their
worldly possessions and responsibilities to commit full-time to Buddhist
practice. It is a midway status between a bhikkhu or bhikkhunī and
laypersons.

[102] “\[Buddha\] tried to impart to those who sought it, and never
forced it upon others. He never compelled or persuaded people to follow
him, for compulsion and coercion were foreign to his method of
teaching.”— Piyadassi Mahā Thera, The Buddha’s Ancient Path.

[103] These teachings are found in several discourses given to Mahānāma,
the Sakyan prince and cousin of the Buddha, including: AN 6.10; AN 8.25;
AN 11.11; AN 11.12; SN 55.21; SN 55.22; SN 55.37.

[104] Ven. Gnanaloka clarifies about the statement; “otherwise one may
get on, making all these things fall off”. This refers to the one who
does not have such an obligation to help people, one can just continue
the practice and allow that sense of needing to find people to help to
“fall off”. One should not concern oneself with that, because these come
out of karmic reasons.They (the sense of imagined duty) themselves are
just feelings, perceptions, not to be regarded as ‘mine’ or ‘I’.

[105] *The Raṭṭhapāla Sutta* (MN 82) recounts the story of Raṭṭhapāla, a
wealthy young man who renounced against his parents’ wishes to become a
monk. His parents later tried to persuade him to disrobe, but Raṭṭhapāla
used the opportunity to teach them the Dhamma. The context here is to
advise acting with firm conviction when the time is right, but always
maintaining harmony.

[106] The term ‘women’ is used here as it reflects the specific monastic
context of the Buddha’s original advice to his chief attendant and
cousin brother Ven. Ānanda. While the example is framed for male
renunciants, the essential teaching is a universal one for any
practitioner: to cultivate sense restraint when faced with sensual
attraction.

[107] Brahmaloka — the heavenly realms of form and formless existence
inhabited by beings who have attained high meditative states (jhānas).
Though long-lived and peaceful, these realms are still within saṃsāra
(the cycle of birth and death) and thus impermanent.

[108] Ven. Ñāṇavimala explained that *bhāvanā* (meditation) is not
simply about being able to sit for hours. He cautioned that progress
stagnates when sitting lacks the right foundation, as some can sit for
long periods and still not advance in the Dhamma. The essential
preparation lies in reading and contemplation, which builds the
necessary foundation of wisdom and actively contributes to the quality
of *samādhi*.

[109] Some references to the Sutta Nipāta in the original notes were
misclassified or contained minor errors in sutta or verse numbering.
These have been reviewed and corrected for accuracy.

[110] The following collections—from SN50 through SN55—describe
successive stages of mental development and provide a gradual training
that culminates in the attainment of stream-entry (sotāpatti).

[111] These *Suttas* are all found in the *Vedanā Saṁyutta* (the
connected discourses on feeling) of the *Saṁyutta Nikāya*. They deal
with the nature of feelings (*vedanā*) and how to relate to them,
particularly in the context of pain, illness, and suffering.

[112] This instruction summarises a progressive path of practice based
on canonical sources. The Mahācattārīsaka Sutta (MN117; the great forty)
outlines the complete structure of the Noble Eightfold Path leading
towards concentration (jhāna). The Khandha Saṁyutta (SN22; connected
discourses on the aggregates) provides insight into the five aggregates
as subjects of clinging, supporting insight into non-self. The following
collections—from SN50 through SN55—describe successive stages of mental
development: the cultivation of the five spiritual powers (Balasaṁyutta,
SN50), the bases of spiritual power (Iddhipādasaṁyutta, SN51),
mindfulness meditation and its fruits (Anuruddhasaṁyutta, SN52), the
absorptions (Jhānasaṁyutta, SN53), mindfulness of breathing
(Ānāpānasaṁyutta, SN54), and finally, the qualities leading to
stream-entry (Sotāpattisaṁyutta, SN55). Together, these provide a
gradual training that culminates in the attainment of stream-entry
(sotāpatti).

[113] The phrase kālaṁ āgameyya literally means “to await one’s time (of
death).” In the context of practice, it signifies a state of inner
preparedness and equanimity toward death—a serene acceptance that arises
when craving and clinging have been weakened. It does not imply
passivity or resignation, but rather the culmination of insight
(*vipassanā*) and detachment, where the practitioner lives mindfully,
ready to face death without fear or regret.

[114] Ven. Gnanaloka elaborates: *<span class="mark">Rāhulovāda</span>*
*Sutta aka Ambalaṭṭhikarāhulovāda Sutta (MN61)*—the three ways of
conduct—this continuous self-reflection should be done the whole day,
using it at night to help reflect on what you have done— from body,
thoughts, words and deeds. Then determine the next day also not to do
it, focusing on how to avoid that. When you are reflecting like this,
one can get into a *samādhi*, and then proceed to attain *jhāna* (or
meditative concentration) through *mettā*. Doing this before sleep,
pushing to the point until one knows when the body needs sleep, then
allows the body to naturally fall asleep. This kind of sleep reduces
your subconscious inclinations and *kamma*.

[115] This refers to the Buddha’s counsel to Ānanda in the
*Mahāparinibbāna Sutta* (DN16) regarding *mātugāma* (women): do not see;
if seen, do not speak; if speech is required, maintain full mindfulness.
It is a renunciant’s discipline of sense restraint—a practical safeguard
against sensual desire and a protection for concentration—not a blanket
moral judgment.

[116] The five precepts are the basic moral code for daily conduct. The
eight precepts (*aṭṭhaṅgika uposatha sīla*) are a temporary vow of
higher spiritual discipline, requiring full celibacy, abstinence from
food after noon, and the avoidance of personal adornment and
entertainment.

[117] Ven. Ñāṇavimala adhered strictly to the *Sutta Piṭaka* as the
complete guide for human liberation. He once commented on the detailed
analytical doctrine, stating, ‘Abhidhamma is for the devas,’ implying
its intricate framework was primarily suited for celestial beings, while
the practical path for human effort lay in the *Suttas*.

[118] *mettā cetovimutti*—the liberation of mind through
loving-kindness—is said to temporarily purify and uplift consciousness,
strengthening the five spiritual faculties (indriya): faith, energy,
mindfulness, concentration, and wisdom. While not a final liberation,
this wholesome release (*cetovimutti*) powerfully supports the
development of these faculties toward full emancipation— *Aṭṭhakanāgara
Sutta* (MN52; the man from Aṭṭhakanāgara).

[119] Ven. Ñāṇavimala noted that the Three Wise Men (Magi), said to come
from the East, were spiritual sages (rishis) who recognized Christ as a
“spiritual king.” This exchange, facilitated by the ancient Silk Route,
suggested that established Eastern ethical principles—non-violence and
compassion—helped shape early Christian ethics. The main point is that
these core ethics had Eastern resonances, even if later religious
practices diverged from the strict morality (sīla) of the Buddhist path.
Doctrinally, however, Abrahamic metaphysics—a Creator-God and an eternal
soul—is fundamentally incompatible with the Dhamma’s principles of
non-self (anattā) and dependent origination (paṭiccasamuppāda).

[120] This is a reference to the journey of the Greek hero Odysseus in
Homer’s The Odyssey (Book XII). Ven. Ñāṇavimala uses it to convey a
powerful Dhamma teaching: Shanaka’s note fuses two separate threats to
create a single simile for sensual craving (*kāma-sukha*): the Sirens
(who used their alluring songs to tempt sailors) and the monstrous,
flesh-eating Scylla (who caused literal death). Odysseus’s instruction
to be tied to the mast and use ear wax represents the fierce
determination (*adhiṭṭhāna*) and disciplined sense-restraint (*indriya
saṁvara sīla*) required to survive the temptation of the pleasures
(*assāda*) and avoid their resulting dangers (*ādīnava*).

[121] This likely refers to the tendency of some Buddhists to delay or
postpone effort in meditation, believing they will attain liberation
under the future Buddha, Metteyya (Maitreya). Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s point
highlights the danger of such complacency—those reborn in lower realms
will miss that opportunity entirely.

[122] This progression refers to the tenfold path of a “noble one,”
which extends beyond the standard Noble Eightfold Path. While the eighth
factor is *sammā samādhi* (Right Concentration), it is followed by
*sammā ñāṇa* (Right Knowledge) and *sammā vimutti* (Right Liberation),
which together represent the final insight and complete freedom of an
enlightened being.

[123] Ven. Ñāṇavimala explained that the deliberate effort to bring the
mind to the present moment, and even the purposeful avoidance of mental
distractions, constitutes new wholesome *kamma* (*kusala kamma*). This
intentional redirection of energy in the present moment is a powerful
action that will bear positive results, strengthening the path.

[124] *Āmisa sukha* is “worldly happiness,” the fleeting pleasure that
comes from satisfying the five senses. *Nirāmisa sukha* is “spiritual
happiness,” the more profound and stable joy that arises from
renunciation, meditation, and wisdom.

[125] *Dāna sālā* is the hall where monks gather to eat the single meal
of the day, which has been provided by lay supporters.

[126] Ven. Gnanaloka recalls that this letter was written to reassure
his worried parents, who were urging him to continue his studies. During
this period, he received many practical gems from Ven. Ñāṇavimala simply
by being in his presence—many of which are documented in Reflection 07.

[127] Ven. Gnanaloka clarifies this is a teaching on skillful
disengagement. The instruction to not “fight” a defilement is because
direct resistance, born of aversion, can paradoxically give it more
energy. The metaphor to “feed” it, or treat it like a guest, means to
calmly acknowledge its presence without alarm or self-blame. Don’t take
it too seriously, else it will grow in power. Having acknowledged it
without conflict, the practitioner does not indulge it, but skillfully
diverts it away and cultivates wholesome qualities, such as the five
spiritual faculties (*indriyas*).

[128] This refers to the *Sedaka Sutta* (SN 47.20), where the Buddha
uses a powerful simile for mindfulness. A man is commanded to carry a
bowl filled to the brim with oil through a crowd watching a beautiful
dancing woman. A guard follows with a raised sword, ready to execute him
if he spills a single drop. The Buddha explains that the bowl of oil
represents mindfulness of the body (*kāyagatāsati*), which must be
guarded with the same life-or-death urgency and unwavering attention
amidst the world’s most powerful distractions.

[129] The advice on *Mātugāma* was given to Shanaka to emphasize the
reflection on the danger (*kāma ādīnava*) of sensual attachment on his
spiritual path. This challenge, manifesting through the physical form of
the opposite sex.

[130] The “immediately calming” method points to the Bāhiya instruction:
“in the seen, just the seen; in the heard, just the heard; in the
sensed, just the sensed; in the cognized, just the cognized,” given in
*Udāna* 1.10 (*Bāhiya Sutta*); a close parallel occurs at SN 35.95
(*Māluṅkyaputta Sutta*).

[131] The complete progressive framework from understanding the
aggregates to entering the stream—the full range of insight and path
teachings in the *Saṁyutta Nikāya*.

[132] *Makhadeva Sutta* (MN 83), which tells the story of a king who,
upon seeing his first grey hair, immediately made a firm determination
to renounce the world and take up the holy life. Ven. Gnanaloka
clarifies that the teaching here is about the necessity of similarly
making a resolute determination in one’s own practice. Without such a
firm determination, one’s latent tendencies (*anusaya*) will continue to
arise and take hold. Only a practitioner who has already attained a
stage of enlightenment is considered safe from these latent tendencies.

[133] This list of *Suttas* constitutes a highly specific curriculum
designed by Venerable Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera to cultivate *sammā ñāna*
(Right Knowledge) and lead directly to the final result (*Nibbāna*). It
organizes texts crucial for developing full insight into the Four Noble
Truths.

[134] “Twelve *Suttas* is enough” means depth over breadth. Take a
small, well-chosen set, learn them thoroughly—read, memorize key lines,
reflect, practice—so study (*pariyatti*) becomes practice (*paṭipatti*)
and realization (*paṭivedha*). The point is to avoid theoretical
overload, not to forbid wider study.

[135] Arahant is a fully enlightened being.

[136] Brahma world is a celestial realm of immense peace and bliss where
beings experience extraordinarily long lifespans, free from physical
pain and gross forms of suffering.

[137] The Pāli word *saṅkhāra*—translated by Bhikkhu Bodhi as
“volitional formations,” a rendering he himself acknowledges as
imperfect—carries subtle shades of meaning depending on context. Its
essence lies in its conditioned nature: phenomena that arise dependent
on causes and conditions.

Within Dependent Origination (*paṭiccasamuppāda*), *saṅkhāra* refers to
the intentional activities rooted in ignorance that condition the
arising of consciousness that propel the round of existence. When
appearing among the five aggregates (*pañcupādānakkhandhā*), it retains
these essential characteristics but takes on a subtle contextual
difference. However, for the purpose of this reflection, it is
unessential to know this distinction in depth.

Ven. Ñāṇavimala himself steered away from elaborate definitions. Ven.
Gnanaloka encourages seekers to gain a gradual understanding through
broader readings of *Suttas* and reflection, rather than through
intellectual analysis of a term.

[138] The Pāli word for form is *rūpa.* See glossary for further
description.

[139] Anāgāmi: The third of the four stages of enlightenment; a
"Non-returner," meaning one who will not be reborn into the lower
sensual realms (which includes the human and animal worlds) before
attaining final liberation.

[140] The aircraft, an Antonov An-32B troop transport carrier, was shot
down while approaching Jaffna, killing all sixty-three people on board,
including Co-pilot Flying Officer Nihal Ranjith Fernando (Jnr.) , who at
the time was the youngest pilot in Sri Lanka to obtain a pilot’s
license. It was believed to have been struck by a surface-to-air missile
fired by the LTTE. The tragedy occurred on 22 November 1995, several
months after the outbreak of Eelam War III, the third phase of the Sri
Lankan Civil War.

[141] The Liberation Tigers of Tamil Eelam (LTTE) was a militant
separatist group that fought a brutal civil war (1983-2009) against the
Sri Lankan government to try and create an independent Tamil state.

[142] For a detailed account refer to ‘Pure Inspiration; Venerable
Ñāņavimala Mahāthera—Recollections Of His Life, Practice And Teachings.’

[143] The first 60 arahants refers to the Buddha’s initial disciples,
whom he immediately sent out to teach. He was leaving the comfort and
care provided by his attending supporters (*dāyakas*)—the food,
medicine, and dwellings they offered—to prioritize his mission. The
statement challenges the monk to reject the ease of dependence on the
laity in favor of the duty to teach the Dhamma.

[144] Ven. Ñāṇavimala often emphasized that while social conditions may
shift, the fundamental faith of the Sri Lankan lay community toward
sincere monks remains strong, rooted in centuries of devotion to the
Saṅgha.

[145] Ven. Ñāṇavimala’s primary warning is against the danger of sensual
pleasure (*kāmasukha*) corrupting the spiritual path. However, Venerable
Gnanaloka adds that when spiritual practitioners maintain this
disciplined, virtuous spiritual life, they naturally become figures who
inspire and lead others to follow the Dhamma.

[146] The Aranthalawa Massacre (1987): LTTE cadres kill 33 Buddhist
monks, most of them novices, in the Ampara District.

[147] The Temple of the Tooth attack (1998): The Temple of the Tooth in
Kandy, Sri Lanka’s most sacred Buddhist site housing the Buddha’s tooth
relic, was attacked by three LTTE suicide bombers driving an
explosive-laden truck.

[148] The Anuradhapura Massacre (1985): LTTE attack at the Jaya Sri Maha
Bodhi Tree, killing about 146 pilgrims at one of Sri Lanka’s most
revered Buddhist shrines.

[149] Devā/devatās are celestial beings.

[150] See Chapter 6, December 6, 1996 for this account in notes.

[151] Preceptor (Pāli: *upajjhāya*): The senior monk who gives a new
monk his higher ordination (*upasampadā*) and serves as his primary
mentor and spiritual guide. The relationship is traditionally akin to
that of a spiritual father and son.

[152] According to the Vinaya (monastic code), a monk purifies his
*sīla* (moral conduct) by formally confessing any minor transgressions
to another monk; more serious offenses require a formal meeting of the
monastic community (Saṅgha) for resolution. This act of clearing one’s
conscience is especially important when facing potential death because a
mind unburdened by guilt is more likely to be peaceful.

[153] The peace process between the Sri Lankan government and the LTTE,
which began with a Ceasefire Agreement on February 22, 2002, and
officially ended on January 2, 2008, was marked by frequent violent
outbreaks, making the region dangerous throughout this period.

[154] This refers to a rule in the Vinaya, the monastic code of conduct,
which prohibits monks from traveling to or through a place where there
is active fighting.

[155] The Sri Lankan Civil War was rooted in long-standing political and
ethnic tensions between the island’s majority Sinhalese (who are
predominantly Buddhist) and the minority Tamil community. Ven. Gnanaloka
is of Sinhalese ethnicity.

[156] Ven. Gnanaloka Thero expressed this statement of gratitude to his
teacher, Venerable Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera, on the day the new *sīmā* was
opened at Parappaduwa Island Hermitage. A *sīmā* is the legally defined
boundary within which the Bhikkhu Saṅgha (Monastic Community) conducts
formal communal acts—such as higher ordination (upasampadā), the closing
of the rains-retreat, disciplinary procedures, and the annual
robe-offering ceremony (kaṭhina) to safeguard procedural purity

[157] Cārikā bhikkhus refers to monks who live the wandering ascetic
life, traveling on foot without fixed abode, depending solely on alms
and practicing meditation in solitude. Ven. Ñāṇavimala Mahāthera
encouraged the establishment of such monks to preserve the ancient
forest tradition of renunciation and simplicity. Interestingly, this
advice was given to a young monk—Ven. Bambalapitiye Gnanaloka—who had
been in robes for only two years at the time.

[158]  A handwritten note, possibly by Ven. Mettavihārī or Ven.
Ñāṇavimala; not included in the printed original.

[159] A handwritten note, possibly by Ven. Mettavihārī or Ven.
Ñāṇavimala; not included in the printed original.
